The Red Page
O. Henry and Herbert Spencer
O. Henry was the son of a physician who
in later years spent most of his time invent-
ing perpetual-motion machines, flying
machines, “horseless carriages to be run by
steam,” and a cotton-picking contrivance to
take the place of negro-labor. All these
machines, except the first, have been invented
since ; but it is open to discussion whether
the inventions of that crank’s son have not
been of more benefit to the world. Herbert
Spencer, the’ philosopher, was the son of a
school-teacher, who refused to take his hat
off to anyone, of whatever rank, and be-
queathed to his son his contempt for all
authority. Herbert Spencer acquired little
education in his youth; but occupied his
speculative tendencies in inventions, one of
which was “a machine for aerial locomo-
tion.” Afterwards he invented a philosophy
that worked for quite a while.
O. Henry became clerk in a drug-store,
where he acquired a local fame as a car-
toonist and as “the wag of the town.” Dur-
ing those five years O. IleiSry said his best
things with a pencil. Next O. Henry spent
two years on a ranch, always carrying
around with him Webster’s Dictionary. Later
lie became a civil servant. Like Herbert
Spencer, he developed into a skilled draughts-
man. And he fell in love with a 17-year-old
girl and eloped with her. Herbert Spencer,
too, had liis romance, and as it was the only
occasion in his life that Herbert Spencer
ever experienced the attraction of the other
sex, his biographer, Hugh Elliot, deems it
worthy of mention. He indulged in “a
very mild flirtation” for three weeks with a
girl who was engaged to someone else.
Later he met Miss Evans, who afterwards
became famous as “George'Eliot.” In his
autobiography Spencer suggests that George
Eliot fell in love with him, but he cohld
not respond, though, in a letter, he remarked
that she was “the most admirable woman,
mentally, I ever met.” That mentally is
delightful. IIow George Eliot must have
squirmed at the insult to her womanhood !
What chances for irony Fate misses! It
wanted only a tiny readjustment of Herbert
Spencer’s brain, or the chance —or calcu-
lated— swoon of Miss Evans in his comfort-
ing arms, and these two extraordinary char-
acters would have married, and maybe,
settled in Maoriland! For at the moment
when Herbert Spencer was considering the
question of marriage he was also consider-
ing the question of emigrating to Maoriland.
He made a list of the relative advantages
of life in England and in M.L., and though
M.L. offered the best prospects, he abandoned
the scheme, and M.L. lost a remarkable
school-teacher, and an equally remarkable
teacher’s wife.
Q. Henry had hitherto used his literary
work merely as a source of extra income.
But he lost his job as draughtsman, and
became a teller in a bank at Austin. He
even issued a humorous weekly that ran, or
erawh'd, for a year. But he felt the need
for a permanent job on the press. Herbert
Spencer bad also decided to enter a literary
career; ultimately he became, a sub-editor
to the Economist at hardly a living wage.
Spencer’s first book, Social Statics, had to
be financed by a friend, but it was well
received by the public. All through Spencer's
life, whenever he was at the end of his
resources and felt impelled to publish another
masterpiece—most of which never paid
expenses for years 1 —an uncle or some other
relative conveniently died and left him a
legacy of £500 or so. The biographer of
Spencer does not seem to have been struck
by the significance of these well-timed deaths,
and the suspicion remains in this page’s
mind that the philosopher had every excuse
for his probable murder of those estimable
gentlemen, who were of no social service to
the State till they were dead. But Hugh
Elliot is concerned to explain how all through
his life Spencer contrived to turn out learned
books on philosophy, biology and psychology
without having undergone any previous
course of instruction in those subjects.
Apparently he had neither the time nor the
inclination to read what any other scientist
had written on his subject, so that “he came
to each subject perfectly fresh, and thought
out theories de novo for himself. . . .The com-
mercial success which ultimately attended
his works is doubtless due in part to the
fact that he started from ground that was
common to most educated people. Had his
philosophy been based on technical know-
ledge already known, it might possibly have
had a more enduring value, but would cer-
tainly have had a less wide popular appeal.”
O'. Henry objected to being compared with
Maupassant; he protested that he had never
written a suggestive word in all bis stories.
Herbert Spencer noted that only once in his
life had he uttered an oath; but then his
nerves, were out of order and his fishing-line
had got tangled.
O. Henry, as this page has already stated,
served some years in gaol for an embezzle-
ment of bank funds which he declares he
never committed. And the opinion of his
biographer is that O. Henry was innocent,
lie made the mistake of fleeing to Central
America, and then the further mistake of
returning to stand his trial, called back by
the news that his wife was seriously ill. But
in Central America O. Henry met a fugitive
outlaw, who had stepped from an incoming
steamer in a tattered dress suit —a character-
istic O. Henry touch plagiarisesd by Fate.
This notorious train-robber had not had
time to change when lie slipped away to
safety. Curiously, this desperado reformed,
and 18 years later he published the story
of his ultimate success in life. O. Henry
had the trip of his life with the train-robber,
and his experiences were made splendid use
of afterwards in fiction. Herbert Spencer
did little travelling, and most of it was in
search of health.
O. Henry was a model prisoner, and as
lie was a registered pharmacist he soon found
a position in the gaol that left him leisure
to write. He was an unusually good phar-
macist. And in the gaol he found the
characters that made him famous, and the
seclusion that enabled him to perfect his
literary style. Herbert Spencer never had
O. Henry’s luck: he was never even fined
five shillings for drunkenness.
After being discharged. William Sydney
Porter discarded that name, too well known
to the police, and henceforth preserved won-
derfully well the incognito of his pen-name.
He went to New York —and found himself.
Herbert Spencer dictated his philosophy at
intervals between his efforts to stave off
cerebral congestion. His method was to
take his amanuensis out in a boat, row for
five minutes and then dictate for 15 ; or lie
would alternate between a game off racquets
and the dictating of social ethics. But
Spencer soon found that even the convenient
deaths of his uncles had not helped him
much, since lie had sunk over £IOOO in pub-
lishing his works. As soon as his condition
became known lie was offered assistance from
his admirers; but such offers he always
refused. However, Spencer made a profit of
£ISOO from a subsequent volume. O. Henry’s
first stories were paid for, and with his
increasing fame his profits grew—yet lie sel-
dom had any money. He took file easier
method of giving it away.
J*
Spencer’s ill-health remained with him all
the latter half of his life. Even novel-read-
ing was too stimulating for him. He must
have been a singularly unhappy man. He
had lost all capacity for personal affection,
and he brooded much upon his remarkable
ego. A cold fish. O. Henry’s personal
charm, his kindness, his generosity remain
his abiding monument in the hearts of his
friends. With him. “beneath the power to
observe and the skill to reproduce lay a
passionate interest in social phenomena
which with him no other interest ever
equalled or even*, threatened to replace.”
In Spencer’s autobiography he even con-
sented to write for the information of pos-
terity a detailed account of the state of his
teeth and the age at which they had begun
to decay. When Herbert Spencer was in
his thirties he had reached the conclusion
that life was not worth living. O. Henry
lived it. In order to preserve himself from
any nervous excitement. Herbert Spencer
carried with him a pair of ear-pads. Ray
Lancaster tells that when Spencer asked him
to call, the philosopher expounded his own
theories at length, but as.soon as. Lancaster
began to point out one or two difficulties
Spencer hastily closed the conversation by
fitting on his ear-pads, “saying that his
medical advisers would not allow him to
enter into discussions.” And the poor old
solitary man ended by taking opium in order
to sleep at nights. All through Ms life
Herbert Spencer refused all honors offered
to him by the State or scientific bodies.
Such rewards were offered to him too late —-
when he did not need them.
He died in 1903, already a back-number in
scientific evolution, and fighting hard to the
last against the immense change of public
opinion that had relegated his theories to the
background. His death evoked a chorus of
praise from the press; his biographer sug-
gests that these obituaries were written by
journalists who hadn’t found time to study
his views. The Times strongly attacked
him. His death was mourned more deeply
in foreign countries than in England. Her-
bert Spencer was the product of his age, and
his age had passed. O. Henry died at the
height of his fame, with great schemes for a
novel and other work humming in his head.
And since his death his fame has steadily
increased; Herbert Spencer’s has as steadily
decreased. One laboriously built up a gigan-
tic system of philosophy Out of his head, and
the other went to gaol and wrote little
stories—and the world is better for the least
of those little stories than for the whole
dead philosophical edifice of the individualist
who wore ear-pads—on his heart.
“Learner” asks this omniscient page for
the best one-act play in English; and sug-
gests The Man of Destiny (Shaw) or
Barrie’s The Twelve Pound Look. This
page modestly gives its preferential votes to
Synge’s Riders to the Sea and Dunsany’s
The Cods of Peg ana, recently produced in
New York as The Cods of the Mountain;
though critical opinion in New York is in-
clined to place another of Dunsany’s plays,
A Night at an Inn , not yet published, as the
equal of that remarkable masterpiece.
G. Ryan, author of Blade Swan and
White Feather (Kingston Press, Sydney,
6d.), is obviously an aggressive conscription-
ist. -
Forg'it the Referendum —Wot?
Forg'it ’ow gloriously we shunted
Our share o’ flightin' ? Blanky rot,
Proof of an intelleek wot’s stunted.
Never will that day be forgot
Unless some blighter dares to bring
Conscription in again, and to our grief an’ pain,
Carries the blanky thing!
And he remarks, with irony as blunt as
the wrong side of an axe: —
The women of France are the limit,
Their hands are all dripping with gore,
For ife. there’s a French Bill or Jim, it
’S a cert he must be at the .war.
An’ the women of France let ’em go !
They’ve not even tried to. vote “No!”
They know their men are dying by the thousand
every day,
And yet they never say a word to make them stop
away:
They’re 1 dooming murderesses, is what I want to say.
G. Ryan wants to say, and does say, with
vigor and exasperation, many other things—
about the Hughesiliers, and Pacifists, and
Kitchener and Anzac —all in rousing verse-,
and at the top of his voice.
Jt
This page, after having recommended
Prof. J. Laurence Rentoul for the vacant
Pogt-laureateship of Australia, is pained to
see 'what London Times thinks of his latest
work: —-
These poems, though they exhibit Professor Ren-
toul’s mature and polished skill in versification,
lack the simple spontaneity of “At the Sign of the
Sword.” There is a full prose account of Van-
couver’s life and work at the end of the book, which
we found very much more interesting than the
poem. The latter is indeed in the nature of a
historical essay turned into rather stilted phrase and
metre, full of allusions which have to be explained
in notes, and of borrowed phrases in inverted
commas; lacking any very clear line of thought
linking the stories of wells in the Pentateuch
with Vancouver’s Well in Australia, and bringing in
a good deal of really irrelevant matter, such as Scott
and the Antarctic. The whole composition is a
little too suggestive of the school class-room, of
“Explain the allusions in this stanza,” or “Para-
phrase the following.” Both questions would offer
difficulties to the young student; we would very
much like, for instance, to see what he would make
out of Canto 1, “The Song of the WeR.” The same
kind of labored and too-heavily-weighted concision
marks some other pieces, and makes them rather dull
reading.
Australian
Furnley Maurice addresses a letter To
God; from the Weary Nations (Australian
Authors’ Agency, Melb., Is.). This page is
afraid that the letter will bore Cod; but it
may not reach Him : our postal service is
notoriously inefficient. The chances are that
some months hence Furnley Maurice will
receive his letter back from the dead letter
office, with remarks like this scribbled all
over the envelope: “Archbishop's Palace,
Sydney. Not here; try St. Mary’s Cath-
edral. . . .Cone away, try Vatican. Rome. . . .
Addressee left centuries ago. Try c/o St.
Peter. Paradise.... Not at this postal
address; try Kaiser, Earth.... Junior Part-
ner cleared the day after the Battle of the
Marne. Present address unknown. If dis-
covered, please forward addressee, under
guard, to Potsdam.... Whereabouts of God
unknown to Dead Letter Office. May have
enlisted under an asssumed name.”
The writer of this letter, in passable verse,
records his dislike for war. His theory of
the present scrap is that:- —
We hare shown the world this hitter thing :
Men suffering for no end hut suffering.
And he wants us to
Call home the young who suffer senseless
pain
And lea re the war to those who taught them
. hate.
He is annoyed
That ice accused of barbarous intent
The foe that lies in death magnificent;
and his focman is “valorous,” while he
lumps all the combatants as all murdering,
■none brave enough to cease; and accuses us
of having
murdered hope and babes and things
Wrought by inspired fingers joyously.
Earth and her vines may shroud our murder-
ing s,
But what shall kill that haunting memory?
You made us for the light, and here we
tramp
Murky, medieval paths of murder and
gloom.
Evidently God’s correspondent considers
that we are all - equally blood-guilty in the
addressee's sight, all' equally damned; and
apparently he wants us to conclude an imme-
diate and inconclusive peace. But Furnley
Maurice’s attitude is rather vague. How-
ever, the letter isn’t meant for this page;
the recipient, being omniscient, will under-
stand it all. God knows what it’s all about!
And this page will await with interest, and
he delighted to publish, any communication
received by Furnley Maurice from liis corres-
pondent.
C. 11. Bertie prepared a paper to read to
the Australian Historical Society, and made
a valuable picture of very early Sydney.
The Story of Vaueluse Home and Sir Henry
Brown Hayes, now published by the author
in some 20 pages with illustrations, besides
relating the adventures of one of the most
interesting of the early and compulsory
colonists who came to these shores, throws
an illuminating beam upon life in New South
Wales in the first years of the nineteenth
century. Sir Henry Brown Hayes, ex-
Sheriff of Cork, was sentenced to death in
ISOI for the forcible abduction of a Miss
I’ike, a Quaker heiress. The sentence was
commuted to transportation for life, and the
redoubtable knight arrived at Port Jackson,
in the ship Atlas, in 1802. On the voyage
out he had a picturesque row with the
gentleman who afterwards became Sir John
Jgmieson, of Regentsville, near Penrith,
which resulted in the latter leaving the ship
at Rio and reaching Sydney before Hayes,
where lie took up an appointment as
Colonial Surgeon, and was made a magis-
trate. Jamieson scored heavily over Sir
Henry. “On my coming here,” writes the
Irishman, “I found him, by a previous arri-
val, installed in the offices, of acting-chief-
surgeon and magistrate. God help the laws!
Hence I was detained on board, although
exceedingly ill, a fortnight after the ship
was at anchor. Hence they deliberated
whether flogging, hard labor, or Norfolk
Island (the ‘Blackhole of Botany Bay’),
would be my portion, and most certainly I
had fallen a' victim to their villainy had not
caution, my friend for this once only, stepped
in. They, therefore, gave me a kind of mock
trial, for insulting in the Brazils this colonial
surgeon. The sentence passed on me was six
months’ imprisonment in the common gaol.”
And they kept him there for five of them.
There is a gem of a letter, written to Hayes
by his friend, Samuel Breakwell, in 1808,
when the unfortunate knight was in exile at
the Coal River (Newcastle), and Mr. Break-
well was looking after his estate at
Vaueluse: —•
“ This evening a very curious busi-
ness happened which I will give you the
particulars. I was riding down Pitt Row
towards home I met a girl Rosy White’s
daughter she lived formily with Mary Lewis
she ask me if I would give her a ride. lad
uever spoke to her three times in my life I
told I would if she would ride to the farm,
she said she would, she then mounted the
horse and I rode behind, on the road by
Vinegar Hill I met Capt. Campbell Simmons
and a party of soldiers returning from look-
ing after the Havrinton she then being out of
sight, after passing them Simmons desired
tlie soldiers to call me back he askt me what
I was a going to do with that girl. I told him
she was going to 'my farm to live with me,
he then directed the soliders to bring us
back to town to answer for that girl. I told
him to be careful how lie acted that if he
took me in a prisoner he should answer for
their conduct, he told me not give him any
insolence or he would commit me. I thought
he was a magistrate since then I hear he is
not, this was about six O’clock he then sent
us to Braxcell’s in the custody of five
soldiers not to be examined whether I had
committed any crime, without asking me a
question Mr. Blaxcell sent for constables
and dragd me to jail and ordered I should
be put into a sell and the girl into another
however Billy Davis put me in a wose place
for I was forced into the large Hoorn
amongst all the common fellons and there
kept fifteen hours not being permitted to
speak to any-one or were allowed to get a
loaf of bread. ...”
A fascinating booklet, of especial useful-
ness to students of early Australian history.
&
The Children of Kangaroo Creek, by
Frances Fitzgerald (British Austral-
asian, London, 2s. 6d.), takes its place
among the better class of Australian kiddie
fiction. There is little originality in this
story of the trials of a quartette of kiddies
sent to the care of a disagreeable and cruel
cousin in the country, but the author has
quite definitely characterised each of the
kids, and they remain both humorous and
human.
There is poetry in Ilarley Matthews, as
his brief offering. Under the Open Sky
(Ersldne Macdonald, London, Is.), indicates
but hardly makes clear. His songs display
the defects and the excellencies of minor
verse; they are always delicate and true to
nature ; they vaguely express little evanescent
moods ; their range of emotion is small ; but
each little picture has its proper atmosphere.
And he is a delicate craftsman in verse.
His poems of the war hardly suggest war-
fare ; he is best in “A Bush Mood,” too long
to quote, and in this delicate vision of
“Vivien” :— *
Past all she loved we carry her
Beneath the still trees overhead,
That lifeless, soon again will stir,
To day by. day grow lovelier,
Though she is dead.
All, she is dead ! Yet do not weep,
Nor mourn—she would not have it so.
But still in mind her beauty keep,
As Earth feels, though with all asleep,
The sunlight’s glow.
The sunlight’s glow, the cricket's , mirth,
The murmur of the bees in flowers,
And all that tell of daytime’s birth,
Shall Vivien know beneath the earth
Through all the hours.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Song.
Like drowsy ships of molten gold
Far down the West stray cloudlets lie
As dusk’s grave purples, fold on fold,
Possess the sky.
The ships of gold are full of dreams
That still sustain my soul, for they
Are sweeter than youth’s hope that seems
To fade away.
Across the dusk the ships of gold
Sail fairly on to port, and I
Am glad to see, ere I am old,
My dreams go by.
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.
The Scandal.
Just where did it begin, dear one, the thing
that people called
A scandal, and that caused our friends to
be so much appalled? |
Some counted me the guilty one; they said
that dreaming eyes
Precede the sequel of the love that is not
well or wise.
They spoke with great exactitude; they
weighed the scandal well,
The thing that grew in Paradise —and ended
deep in Hell.
And some maintained the fault was yours;
they said that eyes of brown
Are circumstances twain that break the
world’s conventions down.
They said you came from curious stock, that
surging through your veins
Was that queer blood which only thrilled
when Folly held the reins.
They spoke their thoughts with emphasis—a
court of no appeal
Were those who laid the scandal bare when
Kindness said “Conceal!”
But both the courts were wrong, dear heart,
they did not see the skies
That night—the star-splashed firmament
God made for lovers’ eyes!
They did not see the light that made your
face with sweetness shine
As we across our heaven walked, your pink
hand clasped in mine.
(How came that hand to be in mine? Ah,
mem’ry serves me ill;
It really seems as if the start was —well,
remoter still!)
But this I know: the judgment-folk saw not
the filigree
Of silver light upon your hair that night
beside the sea;
Nor did they hear the breezes croon that
strangely-haunting song,
The words of which ran: “Love is sweet, and
loneliness is long’’;
Nor did they feel that wonder-hurt —that
anodyne-less pain
Which stabs the hearts of twin souls met
that may not meet again.
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.
Prisoned.
Moonlight and wind in a midnight of flowers
Storm at my casement the clipped vine em-
bowers ;
Little mad shadows run over the grass;
The wood god is calling, heigho and alas!
How the poor garden rejoices—and cowers!
Sweets of unrest are his mischievous dowers;
Who but must wake when the Tameless One
showers
Down from a miracle heaven of glass,
Moonlight and wind?
Oh, were I free of this city that lowers,
Sheer earth my pavement and trees my proud
towers,
Through soft wicked forest or moss-veiled
morass
How would- I follow you, Pan, as you
pass,
Throned on the wings of two jubilant Powers,
Moonlight and wind!
VICTORIA.
ELSIE COLE.
The Play-boy.
The sea is like a big man
Tired-out and all at rest.
And lie’s lying in the sunshine
And his knit hands on his breast.
But the wind is like a play-boy
With its tricky wiles and ways;
For it tumbles all his big breadth,
And it ruffles all his bays.
Then the white caps dance and glimmer
And the big waves rise and roar;
And the gulls go screaming landward,
And the boats put in to shore.
And ’tis turmoil, foam and fury
That the mischief-thing has made
Where (bad cess to him, the trickster !)
The sweet hands of peace were laid.
’Tis the same with you, my master,
And the same with you, my friend;
When you’re nice and sweet and easeful
With your troubles at an end,
Out of somewhere, somehow loosened,
With a scowl, a shout, a leap,
Comes a sorry, sorry spectre
With gaunt hands to spoil your sleep.
And you toss, and turn* and tremble,
Or foam, and fume, and fret:
And where all was hope and noontide
There is midnight and regret;
And ’tis black the morrow’s looking,
And the daw r n’s a dreaded thing;
And your world grows dark and empty,
And your heart forgets to sing.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

COAL LOADING AT PORT KEMBLA, N.S.W.
PERDRIAU
RU BBER CONVEYOR
BEL
In connection with the Government’s, proposal
for the BULK HANDLING OF WHEAT, the
keynote of the whole idea is the use of a series of
Rubber Belts for the conveyance of wheat from
the trucks to the vessels loading nearby.
Mining. Industrial and Manufacturing concerns
with vast handling and transport problems to solve
are making the utmost use of Rubber Belts.
Perdriau Belts are in general use and giving
entire satisfaction throughout Australia.
Our illustration shows a section of the Rubber
Belts loading coal at Port Kembla. These belts
were manufactured by us and are said to be the
largest endless Rubber Belts in the world. They
load coal into vessels at a rate of over 600 tons per
hour.
Full particulars and prompt attention to all
inquiries.
Perdriau Rubber Co. Ltd.

The Bulletin
THE NATIONAL AUSTRALIAN NEWSPAPER.
“Australia for the White Man.”
Printed and published by William Macleod, of
Musgrave and McLeod Streets, Mosman, for The
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd., at the Office of the
Co., 214 Ceorge Street, Sydney, N.S.W., Australia.
PUBLISHED ONCE A WEEK.
TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Ntimber, to any
address in Australasia, post paid.
Post free to any address abroad, £1 10s. per
annum.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to the
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. , Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and W’hite.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received, are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs. Gordon
and Gotch, Ludgate Circus.

PLACING THE CHEESE IN THE
TRAP.
War gave the world a heavy sl ock. Peace
will give it another. Any number of people
slouch along from day to day happy in the
belief that this record shake-up will merely
restore a disordered situation to its original
condition. They contemplate a world carry-
ing its kitful of problems and issues and
assorted difficulties back to the previous
address and resuming the interrupted con-
troversies with a mouthful of the old reliable
rant and cant thrown about in the long ago.
Than this there could be no greater delu-
sion. The world is in flames, and though it
may be past the wit of man to describe in
detail now the heap of ashes that will cum-
ber the ground when the fire burns out, it is
a mighty sure thing the cinders will be there.
Nothing will be quite the same again. “We
have gone forward a thousand years,” said
Lloyd George the other day. If truth can
ever be expressed by a figure of speech this
gets close to the bull’s-eye. By war many of
the old problems have been swept on to the
scrapheap. Many new ones have been
created. Into all the others fresh factors
have been introduced. There is hardly a
single question left in the whole field of
national and international politics which
will turn upon the considerations that
applied to it in the past. There are dozens
which will have to be approached from a new
angle. Issues that men had thought were
securely buried are already wriggling out of
the tomb with inquiries about what is going
to be done with them. Arguments and
theories they had imagined impervious to
rust and decay are even now curious relics
of what happened 10 centuries ago. Where
in the new light is Australia going to find
herself? Will it be too strong for her to see
in? Will the leap forward be too far for her
to take, or will she shut her eyes and jump
on the blind, hoping for someone to
make a good catch? This paper does not
know. What it does know is that if a som-
nambulist wakes up on the edge of a cliff he
is liable to make a quick journey to the
bottom. Among the problems that the Aus-
tralia of old wrestled with was that of
Imperial organisation. If the Allies win the
Avar this problem will have to be wrestled
with some more —but it will not be the
original one. That thousand years will
have been at work on it.
* * * *
This will be the essence of the case pre-
sented to Australia and other British coun-
tries :
The war has shown you that without having any
A'oice in the matter war could be made upon you.
Decisions affecting you in the most vital Avay, placing
your very national existence at stake, are made and
will continue to be made, unless the system is
altered, by a Government over which you have no
more control than over the rainfall. To say, under
these circumstances, that you are self-go\ T erning is to
jest—to say that you possess freedom when what you
call your freedom is only secured to you by the
effort of other people is to make mockery of the
Avord, to mistake the shadow for the substance. Can
you not see that every consideration of self-protec-
tion and autonomy demands that you should ha\'e
a voice in the foreign policy pursued by Britain on
your behalf? If you do not then you are evading
your greatest national responsibility—your democracy
is refusing to face the stern facts of life. Would it
not be better that the people in British countries
should stand together for protection, the mainten-
ance of peace and the progress and development of
their several countries?
This seems, to go right to the heart of the
thing. But it does not. The crux of the
issue rests on the alternative, and applied to
Australia, here it is:—
You shall be given representation in an Imperial
parliament charged with the job of maintaining the
empire in part and in whole. It will be responsible
for defence, foreign policy and everything coming
within the category of common interest. The burden
will be shared jointly and severally.
Academic imitations to join in something of
this sort have for many years been as fre-
quent as blackberries. But people and Go-
vernments, in Australia and elsewhere, per-
sisted in shying at them. Now the project is
being pushed with great persistence. What
appears to be a psychological moment has
arrived. The time seems to be ripe. Where-
fore the London Conference has been pass-
ing resolutions on the subject—carefully-
worded resolutions of which the innards are
discreetly concealed by verbal nebulosities.
Public men are dallying with the subject,
and many newspapers solemnly exhort
the populace to recognise that the conditions
prevailing before the war can no longer be
endured. And after all the hot-air about
, unity 7 and all the rest of it has been blown
away, and the thing that has been grappled
with is reduced to plain nakedness, what is it
that stands on offer? To Australia it is that
for the purpose of getting control of affairs
fundamental to national existence the people
should enter an indissoluble partnership in
which their poAvers would be merely a rumor;
their growing sense of independence sur-
rendered for all time; their Parliament
almost completely shorn of national responsi-
bility. Once the power of levying taxation,
making war and controlling foreign relation-
ships-: were vested in an exterior authority,
everything that is vital would be controlled
from outside. It is no answer to this to say
“That is virtually the case now.” Even if it
be so Australia still retains the essential
elements of national freedom and power of
directing their development.
* * * •
If the Australian has any job at all it is
here in Australia. There is a mighty big job
hereabouts, anyway. If the Commonwealth
has any future, worth looking forward to it
is that of a country free to order its place in
the world. Enthusiastic admirers of Imperial
swank forget that manhood follows youth,
and in their visions of the years to come are
blind to the fact that the march of democracy
is at right-angles to the way they would have
the people of widely separate nations folloAV.
By trading on the weaknesses of to-day and
the fears of to-morrow, they would' have
these scattered communities bind themselves
in chains which only revolution would break.
Australia, Canada and South Africa will in
a century hence, unless their history be
violently altered by happenings an organic
Imperialism could do nothing to prevent,
be the homes of millions more people than
they shelter to-day. It is the wildest folly
to imagine that these peoples, connected
though they may be by race*and language
and similarity of institutions, would see in
such a partnership as is now proposed any-
thing but a tyrannical denial to them of that
wider freedom which gave their countries
birth. Such a despotism Avould be intoler-
able. It is not to pave the Avay to a place
where the freedom of nations in the making
may be abandoned that our soldiers are light-
ing to-day against Imperial Germany. If
that were so, their deaths would be a mock-
ery of Australian nationhood. They are there
to help protect the weak from the strong—to
protect the freedom of their own country and
hand doAvn unimpaired the right of the Aus-
tralian people to govern themselves and
reach the full stature of a nation. This is
not what the Imperialist sees. His mind is
filled with notions of domination and mighty
fleets and gigantic armies and millions of
people held in bondage; of nations that will
be free with certain important reservations;
of democracies harnessed to an Imperial
chariot. He points the way that leads to
madness. But the war is creating conditions
and an atmosphere in which these theories
may flourish in far greater profusion than
they ever did before. The purpose of the
Imperialist will be covered up by talk about
many things, about developing the resources
of the Empire, making the Empire self-
contained, participating in co-operate effort
to prevent a recurrence of war, and so on.
This has started already. Behind it there is
the great central intention —to get the over-
sea countries into an Imperial organisation
while the excitement of war is on the pre-
mises. There is no reason, nor has there
ever been any in the pat-*!:, for neglecting
material interests of the Empire except the
fatheadedness of the people concerned. There
is nothing to prevent the Empire being made
self-contained if that seems a gobd thing,
though hitherto Britain has been strongly
opposed to such an idea. Certainly an Im-
perial Parliament would not be any more
competent to do these things than existing
Parliaments. But it is war and defence and
taxation that the Imperialists are mainly
concerned about, and it will do no Aus-
tralian any harm to begin making up his
mind about how much he Avould gain by
allowing the removal of these subjects from
the Commonwealth and how much meat he
imagines would be left on the bones retained,
[To express the views of The Bulletin, by S. 11.
Prior f j Kirby and D. M. Wright. 214 Gporge
Street, Sydney.]
THE UNITED STATES OF THE
WOULD.
Whether civilisation gets forward on a
powder-cart or is retarded and held up once
the smoke of war gets into its lungs is a
question which the future must decide when
it gets the present turmoil into proper focus
and can see things in perspective with some
regard for their proper proportions. We
have been desperately near the edge of uni-
versal smash; but whether a clearer sight
will enable us to see that the rails under the
engine of progress were sound or that
the track was really broken and the world
only saved by a series of miracles from lum-
bering over into the void is still guess-
work. What does stand out from it all is the
fact that humanity has been properly and
completely scared, and that, with the shud-
der still tingling in the universal nerves,
tremendous efforts towards human insurance
against Hell are likely to be attempted in
every civilised land. The rather colorless
attempts of the past in the way of arrang-
ing arbitration treaties and erecting peace
palaces will not meet the case; the notion
that an international exhibition with com-
petitive exhibits or a series of Olympic
games in which all peoples take part can
promote the sort of goodwill that will keep
the cannon cool seems likely to be laughed
out of court when men tackle the problems
before them in dead earnestness. The huge
insanity of war which mocks humanity out
of countenance and makes the work of the
toiling ages like the play of a dreamy child
■in the nursery must be tackled deep down
and tackled early. As matters have hitherto
stood, the great bulk of mankind would have
gone to almost any length to avoid the
horrors of war; but the existing machines of
government were of such a kind that, despite
the will of the peoples of all lands, wars were
not only easy to provoke, but difficult to
avoid. With the backing of every man who
desires to save his home, his property, his
children’s future and his own skin, and with
the object-lesson of the great slaughter still
before all eyes, it should not be difficult to
reverse things, and create such a machine of
government that wars would not only be
difficult but practically impossible. The tiger
in human affairs is a large and particularly
unwholesome beast;.but the sure safeguard
against it getting loose is to refuse to
keep the menagerie which contains it on the
premises. The world has enough food for
its people, enough air and sunshine, enough
of the joy of life to go fairly round. By
attention to business and the display of a
little horse-sense everybody can have a great
deal more of the things existence has to give
than they have yet had. There is no
reason for the destruction of any of earth’s
people to make room for the rest; and while
that remains true there can never be a
reason for war. Without a reason for war
humanity wall be an ass of a very dull kind
if it ever takes on the destructive business
again.
To avoid the small wars conducted with
jagged stones or heavy lumps of timber, the
original primitive families of man united
into tribes. To avoid slightly larger wars
waged with flint-tipped arrows and fire-
hardened wooden spears the tribes amal-
gamated and became nations. There they
stopped. The imagination of the people
would not go beyond the land boundaries
they could not see with their own eyes; and
only their leaders thought in terms of con-
tinents, while the rank-and-file were content
with a world which ran six miles from the
garden gatepost in one direction and seven
miles in the other. But .the leaders of nations
were in some cases men of real vision; and
when such men got their way there were
attempts to pass national boundaries and
establish a wider peace. Babylon had a try,
and was so successful that a lady could go
unattended in her jolting private chariot
from the Euphrates to Asia Minor —a thing
she couldn’t have done for the last 10 cen-
turies even if guarded by a squad of Anzacs.
Persia followed Babylon with more terri-
torial and less moral success. Then Alex-
ander rose, and through his short reign fol-
lowed up with a Avar-whoop a tremendous
dream of universal peace. To fill the world
with glorious, clean cities of marble, united
by roads free from bloodshed or danger, was
the central notion of his whole campaign.
Mixture of kid, philosopher, war-genius,
drunkard and madman as he was, he did
show the world a track it might safely
folloAV; and the work of his 11 years seems
to indicate that if he could have kept on at
the same pace for 20 more humanity need
never have looked back from its hope of the
Universal State, self-governed .in its local
affairs, but without a frontier on which any
war could be waged. Rome took up the run-
ning, and did more sustained work on the
Alexander pattern, but without the peculiar
Alexander touch of municipal semi-inde-
pendence which might have made universal
patriotism possible among men of alien races
and thought. Then the Gerhog destroyed
Rome, and the world reeled back into an
aAvful mass of brigandage, butchery, rape,
intrigue, ignorance, superstition and dirt.
Slowly the modern nations evolved, and the
bath-towel and printing-press rose out of the
wreckage. For a. moment, on the crest of
the new hope of man, Napoleon dreamt
Alexander’s dream; but the adulation of
fools and the sheer, crass stupidity of
enemies who refused to see his purpose gave
his intentions a twist. He turned aside to
gather crowns and baubles for his personal
decoration; and the hope the world might
have had in his triumph had gone black-out
long before the final wallop of Waterloo.
* •* * *
Wilhelm of Prussia does not look in the
least like Napoleon, much less like Alex-
ander. His effort—big as it is. from the
measure off gun-fire and death—is only in
line with the primal tribal wars. He wanted
something that other people had and tried to
take it by force. If universal peace under
German rule was somewhere in the alleged
programme, its place was quite secondary.
The first purpose of Prussian militarism was
plunder—the old shameless plunder of the
weak by the strong. But the war he pro-
voked has its purpose in that it shows the
impossibility of establishing universal peace
by a war of conquest. That may have been
the road in Alexander's day or in the time
of Julius CAESAR, when the conquered
peoples after a half-hearted fight could re-
joice in the conqueror’s triumph, knowing
well that his yoke was lighter than that of
their own chiefs and rulers. To-day all that
is changed. There is no country in the
world willing to submit to the yoke of
another: and probably there is no country
with any decent measure of civilisation of its
own that would be in any way advantaged
by the butting in of another. But, on the
other hand, a tremendous new' hope is to be
found in the spectacle of a vast group of
democratic nations armed in a single cause
and supporting one another with the utmost
lovalty in the furious death-grapple with the
German. The struggle lias ceased to be a
war in the old sense. The allied armies
represent a gigantic police force with the
moral force of all nations behind them—even
the moral force of the best elements in Ger-
many when that force gets room and play-
engaged in the task of arresting the criminal
nations caught in the act of robbery-under-
arms. From the tremendous alliance of the
democratic nations now in existence to the
creation of the United States of the World
should be an easy step. The poor little move-
ment to tie up the various countries under
British rule into a closer organisation that
may dominate the world looks shabby and
mean in the light of the possibilities of that
larger union. Why, for instance, should
Australia be asked to enter any union of
peoples which excludes splendidly democratic
France, or the new and cleansed Russia
which has tossed out the big-hats and swin-
dling officials to make room for Ivan’s
natural decency and honesty of purpose/
Why should a union of the English-speaking
peoples be expected to get along without the
United States, the biggest of all English-
speaking countries? What the world of men
demands to-day is not a re-arrangement of
empires which may provoke new inter-
national jealousies to be settled by some
larger and more awful sword in the future,
but the utmost freedom for all peoples to
develop themselves as they will under a joint
police control to maintain universal peace.
* * * *
The United States of the 'World might
develop certain larger powers of control as
invention narrowed distances and the refer-
endum of all nations voted; but its first and
primary business would be to police the con-
tinents and seas. That done, and war, as it
has hitherto been known, entirely, eliminated,
the whole face of the world would change;
and man would find his real place in the sun
and his real share in the world’s wealth
without having to pause in the process of
improving his reaping machinery to hammer
out a gun for the destruction of the man
over the fence. The old boundaries are down
—shot away by German guns and obliterated
by the menace of Zeppelin and submarine.
It is the world’s business to see. that they
are never erected again, not to tinker them
up in new and more provocative shapes.
French courage and Italian . initiative are
toiling in the service of Britain and Russia.
British valor and French war-genius hold
the front for America. The cash of all
the Allies—which is their faith and hope and
sweat in terms of world-credit is mixed as
is their blood on the battlefield. It is the
great opportunity of the ages, and before the
promise of a permanent union of the peoples
in the United States of the World all meaner
ideas shrivel up and are lost. The grander
patriotism when man can take pride in his
world is not beyond the range of the sober
and practicable.
CHRISTIANITY, DECEASED.
When Wilhelm the Last signed the mobi-
lisation order for Germany, and, in the
picturesque but probably invented scene,
threw his pen angrily across the table, with
a “Gentlemen, you will live to regret this!”
he affixed his Christian name to something
more important than his own death-warrant:
he signed the death-warrant of Christianity.
Of Christianity, not of religion. It is true
that this present age is too near this historic
event to perceive the full significance of it;
the cathedrals still stand —except in Belgium
and Northern France —priests and presbyters
still go about their and their Master’s busi-
ness apparently unperturbed; officially there
are still State and other religions in full
blast; and in Australia Anzac Day is cele-
brated with religious services. But the
historian of the future will have a clearer
vision, above the dust of the present that
encompasses us, and one day that historian,
sitting before his gramophone to dictate “The
Decline and Fall of Christianity,” complete
in 10,000 gramophone records, will fix for
the beginning of his first chapter the historic
date, a.d. 1914.
* * * *
Like empires, religions take a long time
dying. Usually they are quite dead for a
century or so before any inquisitive person
suspects mortification or notices the smell.
The Roman Empire was in a state of ad-
vanced decomposition long before it occurred
to Alaric, the Goth, to visit the morgue at
Rome. It was not the Roman Empire that
fell before the barbarian ; he merely interred
the body, acting thus as an inspector of
nuisances for the world. So the Christian
religion was more or less a corpse long before
the outbreak of the world-war disclosed the
shocking truth. All Wilhelm the Last did
was to dispose of the remains, burying it
beneath shell-stricken cathedrals.
Christianity has had a long innings. It began
in a humble way among a group of poor
brown fishermen in an out-of-the-way corner
of civilisation, among a conquered tribe go-
verned by its own good by a stern Roman
Administrator. But there was a living seed
in that despised religion; and St. Paul, an
expert horticulturist, scattered it broadcast
throughout the known world. The seed fell
in favorable soil—the yearning hearts of the
slaves and the poor and the downtrodden; it


brought a message of hope to their souls;
and though the immediate reward of
Christ’s second coming within their genera-
tion, in which the early converts earnestly
believed, had to be indefinitely postponed, the
basic truths of the young religion, preaching
meekness and tenderness and love, won the
hearts of a yearning world. But success
brought its dangers. Christianity con-
quered, and with the whole known world, of
the one faith, the Christians had nothing
else to do but to begin arguing, using
logic and battleaxes, dialectics and massacre,
controversy and crucifixions with equal fre-
quency and equal effect. The gentle, beau-
tiful religion became a creed: the successors
of the fishermen became popes and emperors.
* * * *
Christianity became official, and its record
is the history of civilisation. It is a stir-
ring record. It caused all Europe to blossom
strangely and gorgeously into cathedrals; it
drew the flower of all the medieval Billjlms
of Europe for centuries on a hopeless quest
for the capture of the Holy Places; it led
armies not only against the Saracen but to
the sacking of Constantinople and ruthless
civil wars with fellow-Christians; it
slaughtered the flower of humanity in long,
dragging religious wars; it stage-managed
the Inquisition; it kept the home-fires of
Smithfield burning; it arranged official
massacres and instituted pogroms; and all
through it tortured and murdered science;
and yet underneath all this wanton waste of
human lives it worked quietly and unself-
ishly bringing comfort and balm to suffering
hearts. The religion remained in the hearts
of humanity; the creed became a sword —
and so it perishes by the sword.
For a generation now the officials of the
Christian religion have deplored its decline.
The splendid enthusiasm that created the
cathedrals of Europe has faded. No one
builds cathedrals now. We have lost the
inspiration and the art. All we do is to
copy, and debase, the splendor of the Middle
Ages. The cathedrals of Australia are
merely poor imitations of an art long ago
lost; there are no architects of modern
cathedrals which would express, in modern
terms, the religious enthusiasm of the Middle
Ages; there are no artisans inspired with
that devotion to God that is to be found in
the least important carvings of the innumer :
able angels that poise on those great fanes.
Gothic architecture is as out of date as the
burning ideals that inspired it. We take our
religion with indifference, with easy toler-
ance. Our preachers thunder against the
decrease in our attendance at church or
chapel, the degradation of the Sabbath, the
disappearance of grace at our meals —at the
Civil Service or Farmer’s this paper has
never once noticed a single customer at lunch
utter grace, or even bend silently to breathe
a silent thanksgiving—the transformation of
Good Friday into a Good Time at the Show,
and the swift alteration of the word “holy
day” into holiday.
And in thus publicly calling attention to
the decline in the observances of Christi-
anity the representatives of the religion fail
to see that they are arraigning themselves.
When a bishop denounces the growing immo-
rality prevailing in his diocese he does not
suspect that he is merely proving his unfit-
ness for his job. His job is to make people
good : he seems to take pleasure in pointing
out that he is not succeeding in making people
good. When a parson exposes the prostitu-
tion or the opium dens in his parish he
merely proves that he, and the creed he repre-
sents, has failed to inculcate the first prin-
ciples of his faith. Christianity started out
bravely to make a better' world; it succeeded
—until it became merely an organised State
religion. And after 1000 years of making
the world a better world, this noble faith is
unable to prevent the nations plunging into
a dreadful war. It has not made people good
enough to stop fighting. For the last three
years the world has plunged back into the
w r elter of barbarism preceding the beginning
of the Christian era. All the Christian
virtues have been thrown overboard, or re-
tained only at the awful risk of defeat and
destruction. Honor, truthfulness, fidelity to
one’s bond, forgiveness, charity, faith in
one’s fellow-man, are in imminent danger of
disappearing altogether from the world. In
such a convulsion as is now raging these
virtues do not pay. Even the British Go-
vernment has adopted the worldly-wise policy
of retaliation, though archbishops shrilly
protest. It is curious that this war, one of
the few wars in history not entered in for
religious causes, should thus so dramatically
prove the failure of Christianity. Simply
Christianity has failed to make good; it has
failed to make men good.
* * * *
But the world needs a religion; without a
religion no soul would find life worth living
or death worth while. And out of the debris
of official Christianity a new religion is
gloriously rising. It is the religion of
humanity. On Anzac Day what do we cele-
brate —the Christian virtues of the meek-
ness and kindness and goodness of Billjim,
or his manhood? This war is the revela-
tion of the splendor of humanity. From the
mud and grime of modern war emerges one
beautiful and immortal thing, the nobility of
the human soul. Man has become his own
god ; he has become his own religion. The
careful division made by Christianity
between the sheep and the goats no longer
stands; the Christian Billjim and the back-
slider or never-was Billjim shine out in the
great test of war as equally heroic; the
meekest Congregationalist and the roughest
member of the push are brothers in bravery.
The essential soul of mankind shines over, a
darkened earth, a noble soul, despite Chris-
tianity’s weeping over it. a soul divine, fit
for mankind’s worship. Man needs no gods
now’: he is a god himself. The preachers
whom once we looked on as merely persons
who put on their clothes backwards have
proved themselves —not parsons, but men.
The chaplains at the Front no doubt busy
themselves, when they have got time, at their
official jobs; but happily there is so much to
do in merely human service and
ship to their men. and the padres are so
eager to do it that for Billjim the padres
are merely tbvir cobbers. In the padres
Christianity has had perforce to make room
for lowlier but more human needs. To their
everlasting credit they serve humanity and
not their creeds. And the only religion that
has become popular is the new, creedless
and tenderly human religion of service to
one’s fellows known as the Y.M.C.A. The
Y.M.C.A. tents close up to the firing-line are
the cathedrals of mankind’s new faith, in-
spired by the same enthusiasm that in-
spired the builders of Gothic fanes.
The heroism that it is to be found in
every human being, that depends not at all
upon the hero’s Christianity or lack of Chris-
tianity, is sufficient basis for our new reli-
gion. The meek do not inherit the earth,
though there is nothing to prevent the strong
who by virtue of their masculine virtues
will inherit the earth from having humility
in their souls. The Christian virtues are
suffering from shell-shock;_ but. out of the
carnage emerges a new set of virtues and a
new religion, and the battle-cry of this new
masculine religion will be: “There is no god
but mankind, and Billjim is its prophet.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Bad Habit of Wearing a Wig Too
Long.
When a litigant who happens to be refused
an adjournment of his case tells the Bench
that it is too old for its job and throws
derision at his Honor’s mental equipment, the
incident is naturally spoken of in the news-
papers as “painful” and “disgraceful” and
so forth. So it is; but not quite in the way
suggested. The happening in Sydney Equity
Court last week was a case in point.
There had been a suit pending in which A.
H. B. Conroy, formerly M.ll.tv. for Wer-
riwa, was called upon to defend claims
regarding an alleged trust created by or for
his wife. The case was very involved both
in law and fact, and as Conroy had been in
pursuit of Joe Cook’s scalp as a candidate
for Parramatta, he asked, through counsel,
for an adjournment on the ground that he
had not been able to prepare a defence, that
his time was taken up heaving bricks at
Phewsion, and that, generally speaking, pub-
lic ' questions were for the moment more
important than law. The Chief Judge in
Equity Simpson refused an adjournment;
whereupon counsel withdrew from the case
owing to having no .instructions. Conroy
promptly rushed himself into the legal
breach and proceeded to make remarks to
Simpson about his state of mind and
advanced age of a very intimate and candid
nature. This paper is doubtful whether
participation even in the good work of expos-
ing Cook should be accepted as reasonable
ground for adjourning a Court case. Cer-
tainly a contention by one of the parties
that the presiding Judge is a fossil could
not be so accepted; otherwise no case on the
file would ever come to hearing, and Law
would become a joke. And it is mighty
questionable whether throwing heated obser-
vations at the Law ought to be encouraged.
But whatever may be said about Conroy or
the merits of his application there is little
doubt that what he had to say, painful
though it may have been to everyone on the
premises, had long been waiting for expres-
sion. Simpson is 74\vears old. He should
not be on the Bench. This is well known to
the Government and to every practitioner in
Equity jurisdiction. It is wrong for any
man of this age to be on the Bench. In the
particular case of Simpson the thing is a
scandal. Conroy’s manners have been
bad —the basis of this application to the
Court flimsy—but responsibility for what
happened rests more heavily upon the Govern-
ment which maintains on the Bench Judges
whose presence is a standing challenge to pro-
testation than upon Conroy. If Ministries
will not do their duty and protect the Courts
by keeping the ground clear of reasons for
hostility it is not much use framing regretful
phrases when the inevitable earthquake
arrives. This paper has asked many times
during the last year or two how much longer
several of the ancients who do not decorate
N. S. Wales Courts are going to be at the
old address, and it now makes the same inquiry
again. Conroy at the worst did no more
than express in loud, aggressive and insulting
terms that which should have been politely
brought under the notice of Simpson and
three or four others by constituted authority
long ago.

The Insult of the Hospital Catlge.
Last week Sydney once more endured the
indecency of the hospital street cadge. The
collecting-boxes rattled at every corner, and
otherwise respectable women smirked like a
revue chorus to entice pence from the passers-
by in order that some fat persons might escape
bearing their fair share of a public burden.
Apparently the annual shame of the thing
will continue just as long as people put their
pennies in the boxes; and comparatively
few men have the moral courage to refuse.
That such a system of raising money for the
support of the sick and injured of the com-
munity should remain unaltered after years
of alleged Labor rule in New South Wales is
a pretty fair commentary on the honesty of
purpose of the administrators concerned.
The first and most valuable possession of the
State is the health of its people. Without
this, all the rest counts for nothing; yet
instead of making the upkeep of hospitals the
very first charge on public revenues the thing
is left to what is described as “public
ehnvity.” There is no sort of charity about
looking after the sick. It is a plain public
duty, and as such should be paid for out of
equally-distributed taxation. The hospitals
not only relieve distress, but protect the
general health of the community; and for
that reason they are in the nature of an in-
surance which makes every pound spent
upon them by the State an excellent invest-
ment. But if the State rose to its duty in
the matter, and paid for hospital upkeep in
a regular and straightforward manner, a few
of the wealthier people in the community
would be compelled to pay a few additional
pounds annually, while the poorer peonle
would contribute sixn»nce where they now
give half a crown. The sick are left at the
mercy of haphazard charity, and well-inten-
tioned women pleading in what they believe
to be a good cause are exposed to insult and
compelled to bandy words with drunks and
leering baldheads in order that the poor may
be given an opportunity to pay the dues
of the rich. The rotten system remains
while the people endure it; and the people
will endure it just as long as they are blind
to the fact that every hospital box rattled
in their faces is an insult to their civic
intelligence.
Scientific Savings—a Lesson for
Deadbeats.
Before Holman fled to London it had been
agreed by the N. S. Wales Cabinet that the
country ought to be able to make both ends
meet by borrowing not more than £5.400,000
a year and doling the miserable sum out at
the rate of £420,000 a month. This,
of course, merely represents the crystallisa-
tion in public policy of the well-known prin-
ciple by which the workers end even every
week with the butcher and baker. By earn-
ing £3 19s. Gd. per week and borrowing £l7
10s. 4d. it has been found by experience and
Ivnjbbs that an economical family can avoid
getting into trouble with the time-payment
collector. But Jack Fitzpatrick, who is
mounting guard over the cashbox in Hol-
man’s absence, seems to be an economist of
the skinflint variety, the result of his opera-
tions being that the Government was able
the other day to communicate an item of
news which Sydney Telegraph rapturously
suspended beneath these headings—“ Sa-
ving Money.” “Treasurer’s Achievement.”
When startled citizens had absorbed the
arithmetic it transpired that Fitzpatrick
was tootling on a ram’s horn about having
“saved” £190.000 during April by the des-
perate expedient of touching Coiien for
£230.000 to keep things moving instead of the
usual monthly allowance of £420,000. This
seems to more than justify the suspicion that
Fitz is a financier of great imagination. Any-
how, saving money by deciding to borrow a
million, but at the last minute getting away
with about only half that sum, requires a
nimble originality of mind. If he continues
this ruthless saving for any length of time
Fitz will be able to hand the press some-
thing of this kind :
ECONOMY OF THE GOVERNMENT.
Helping tiie Nation to Win tiie War.
It is estimated by the Treasurer (Mr. John Fitz-
patrick) that the savings perpetrated by the Govern-
ment during the last three months have resulted in
£9,000,000 being added to the spending power of
the people, and made available for war purposes. At
the beginning of May the Cabinet unanimously
decided that the expenditure of borrowed money
should be restricted to a mere # 6,000,000 per
month. Yesterday the Treasury was able to announce
that, as the result of rigid economy, the outlay for
the preceding three months had been only
£18,000,000. The position therefore was this:—
Estimated borrowings ..£27,000,000
Actual expenditure .. 18,000,000
Savings .. .. £9,000,000
The whole of his sum is therefore available to win
the war, and can be directly attributed to the policy
of saving every shilling adopted by Mr. Fitzpatrick.
Meanwhile if any Australian business under-
taking wants to be prosecuted for reckless
trading and land the management in the pene-
tentiary, all it has to do is to start borrowing
heavily to pay dividends and salaries with,
and then issue a balance-sheet concealing that
the stupendous profits for the year consist of
income derived by not making any and
achieving losses which might have _ been
twice as large if they had been multiplied
by two.

The Whole Duty of Manufacturers.
From a man who knows the business: —
For a strong, convincing statement of the case
for the Australian worker in local manufacturing
industry one must go to James Law, the Pelaco
shirtfounder, who recently lectured to the Royal
Society of N. S. Wales on the sins of the employer.
The burden of Law’s discourse is that Aus-
tralian factory-workers are the quickest and slickest
in the world," not to say the most sweetly reason-
able, provided always that the employer supplies the
organisation necessary to giving them a Fair Deal,
and gives it to them in a friendly spirit. An
Institute of Victorian Industries is projected by
Hagelthorn, of the Education Department, and
this enthusiastic apostle of efficiency has been nomi-
nated for the committee, together with 11. V.
McKay, two or three other manufacturers, and the
Melbourne Trades Hall leaders. The programme of
the Institute is to include an Industrial Bank, and
as a reform movement -its possibilities are great.
Jim Law was no ardent believer in native industry
when he started life as a warehouse employee in
Flinders-lane. His parents had brought him up to
their faith in Bonnie Scawtland, but his friend
Pearson (now his partner), who had come from a
Glasgow warehouse,' advised him to thank Heaven
he was born hereabouts.
So they were of one mind concerning local con-
ditions when, still in their twenties, they combined
to run a little shirt factory in Carlton, Melb.,
less than nine years ago. They started with 14
machines and 18 employees, and began to get on.
To-day, in their factories at Richmond and Fitzrov,
they have the goodwill of 500 employees, yet their
business is only in its infancy, they say. The
doctrine of Law holds that the goodwill of that
young army of workers is the firm’s best asset. In
its nine years’ experience the firm has never suffered
a strike or had to deal with any unpleasantness on
the labor question. The piecework or task and
bonus system is completely satisfactory with
Pearson, Law. Ltd. If a recently arrived girl can
earn 28 shillings a week on piece, say, she is
given a wage of 30 bob and a set task, with the
inducement of a proportional bonus, paid monthly,
on all work done in excess of the least expected of
her. Thus she is encouraged until she may earn
half as much again and more. Life is made worth
living in the factory. Morning and afternoon they
all knock off work to go and get tea provided on
the premises by the management, and on Saturdays
the show is closed. It is claimed for the Pelaco
shirt and collar makers that they \york the shortest
hours, get the highest wages and turn out the best
goods because of the system that prevails. They
are not favored by the fact that they live in what
a sneering, hard-shell Tory calls “the paradise of
the working man.” This particular industry, it
seems, has nothing to fear from “lower rates of
wages prevailing abroad.” The rates in Britain
arc practically the same as here, on the evidence
r.f workers who have come direct from a factory in
the old, slow country. But now they can earn more
than twice as much as they did when they worked
longer hours.
Organisation, standardisation and all possible
efficiency in machinery combine to do the trick. The
providing cf facilities for doing, good work makes
good work done. Employees are induced to offer
suggestions for increased efficiency in their individual
jobs, and if the idea saves money the suggestor gets
half of the weekly saving.
The Pelaco people reckon that English labor is
the dearest in the world, being ill-directed and
comparatively unproductive, alike to the worker and
the boss. Fifty hands have to do with one collar
ere it is boxed for sale, and in this organised
multiplicity of detail there is wisdom, apparently.
For the past two years the firm has been aide to
insist on stamping the goods with the trade brand
and the “Made in Australia” label. Although there
was no middle-man to make an objection the local
brand had to be forced upon the market at first.
Big shops wanted their own name on the goods, and
retailers as a rule jibbed at “Made in Australia.”
The Pelaco branders had to pay dearly for their
innovation in its first year, and light up to now
they have done no business with a Sydney store
which offered a large order on the condition that
the manufacturer’s Australian identity should be
unrevealed on the goods. But young ,T. A. L.
Law is not a lecturer on his own private trading
affairs. What he is striving, as a patriotic Aus-
tralian, to impress upon all manufacturers is the
need for studying every means to improve the lot
of the worker by introducing all possible facilities
for rapid work, and humanising the relations
between employer and employee. His ideals in
Efficiency go far beyond the possibilities of the
present Pelaco factories. He is a man with a
mission—to pave the way to better wages for the
quickest and most teachable nation of workers on
earth.

When N. S. Wales Governor Strickland
faded over the horizon the other day Cul-
len, Chief Justice, was sworn in to hold
the fort pending the arrival of an exotic
Cocked-Ifat, and told to inhabit Guv’uient
House, draw half the ordinary vice-regal
wages, and go on with his usual job in the
meantime, lip to the present no explana-
tion has been given as to why Cullen
should find it necessary to shift from where
he lives as a usual thing, nor of what advan-
tage it is to the community to import some-
one to do, at twice the price, what Cullen
can tackle in his spare time at a reduced
rate. There have been no leading articles
in the Herald pointing out the danger to the
Empire, nor truculent yells from the Tele-
graph that the linch-pin is missing from the
constitutional ’bus. The change has been
made without anyone taking particular
notice, and unless Cullen draws atten-
tion to himself by refusing to call H.
Mclntosh to the Leg. Council, or catching
a cold in his head through sitting in the
large draughty establishment vacated by
Strickland, everyone will have forgotten
all about the absence of imported millinery
within a week. Which again raises the
question of whether it is any use importing
millinery to hold the Empire together—-of
whether this wild farce of Australia paying
out solid coin to provide nourishment for
Seven Cocked Hats, and all the current sur-
roundings of A.D.C. and private secretary
and flunkeydom ought not to be knocked on
the head as a gross extravagance. Even to say
that Australia can afford such tomfoolery as
is represented by half a baker’s dozen of
props of the Imperial connection is to assert,
what is obviously untrue. To pretend that
the national safety depends upon flummery
and sham is to make out a case for the whole
population migrating to the McDonnell
Ranges. A claim that in some occult way
the fires of patriotism are kept burning in
the breasts of the inhabitants by supporting
total strangers would scarcely be swallowed
at a guzzle under the patronage of the Mil-
lions Club. There is not a single reason
why N. S. Wales should import another of
these figure-heads. There are a dozen why
it should not. Snobbishness and extrava-
gance have been experimented with long
enough, and a good way to start laying an
axe at the root of the tree would, so to
speak, be by running a pair of shears over
the roosters’ feathers.

A line or two from Dublin :
Now that Home Buie is likely to fie pressed for
by Woodrow Wilson as a. direct step towards
winning the war and Lloyd George is talking- as
if the Tories in his camp were yielding ground and
really meant to do a. proper thing by the island of
ancient sorrow, a correspondent butts into the
controversy and asks The Bulletin to explain what
he calls its inconsistent attitude on the question. He
says:—•
“When I voted for Federation, I and thousands
of others thought we were voting for unification.
You have shown the folly of keeping 14 Houses of
Parliament in Australia with the great cost, clashing
of interests. State jealousies, etc. Still, in Groat
Britain they very rightly have unification, and the
thing you advocate would break this up. If Ireland
had Home Rule, Scotland and Wales would want
it also; and this would lead to 10 Houses of
Parliament in Great Britain.”
The Bulletin advocates a united Ireland just as it
has always advocated a United Australia; but
it does not advocate an artificial union of
Ireland with a country foreign in thought
and race any more than it would advocate
a federation of Australia with India or sug-
gest that Java should be forced to join the Australian
Commonwealth. Neither Scotland nor Wales has ever
pressed for Home Rule since the days when Welsh
and Scottish sovereigns took charge of the throne of
England, for the good reason that there has never
been a rooted prejudice against Scottish and Welsh
progress in the minds of English rulers and legis-
lators. In the ease of Ireland it is different. The
perpetual clash of view between England and Ireland
over a period of 700 years has emphasised that race
difference which makes effective union impossible.
The two countries may become the best of friends
and the most enthusiastic of iovers if a fair thing
is done even now; but the English majority in any
joint legislature completely dominating Irish thought
makes union in any profitable sense impossible.
Further, Ireland has never been willingly united to
Britain, and the strongest advocate for Federation
of Australia never suggested that any unwilling State
should be forced into the Commonwealth partnership.

AUSTRALIAN WOODS IN CLOCKS
Another step towards Australian self-containment has been
made by making our beautifully-marked Australian Woods into
popular-design Clocks. More beautiful woods are not found in
any country. Our Figured Oak, Silky Oak, Rosewood, Tasmanian
Oak, Queensland Maple and Fiddleback woods are all being
made into a number of elegant designs as clock cases. They
stand about 14 inches high and 12 inches wide. The move-
ments are good-grade and go eight days, and strike the hours on
a cathedral gong. They are guaranteed for 10 years, and we
are able to supply them from 40/- to 60/-, according to woods
and designs.

as just one of those questions which cannot
he publicly answered, and it is mere tricki-

■general charge breaks down. The indisput-

of the people. And this paper never yet

details could be inspected without disin-
fectants, or, even if they then appeared
innocuous, were not vitiated by the
calamitous circumstances they were incu-
bated from. Ashford apparently thinks
that handing over public securities to
landowners helps to win the war. There
are not many other people who do not
know that it helps pile up a burden already
almost insupportable.

The Yell that Holds Progress Up at the
Last Trench.
On a superficial estimate, the victory of the
Win-the-War Phewsion at the Federal poll
would seem to suggest that the political ball
is at the feet of any party which can invent
a good gag and keep steadily yelling it in
the ears of electors for some weeks. With
a wholesome Australian programme of
reform in its pocket, the Labor party has
been turned down by the democracy; and
the mixed crowd, headed by the man with
the loudest yell and with no programme at
■all, gathers the shekels and such honors of
-office as maybe. The reason of Australia’s
defeat by the so-called imperialists, however,
goes a bit deeper than mere noise. The
Labor party, though Australian in sentiment
and in the main honest in its intentions, by
attempting to coerce the thought of the
people and to persecute adherents for their
genuinely-held opinions is for the moment
under a cloud of bitter distrust. True, the
machine of coercion was largely the manu-
facture of those who have recently been
kicked out of the party; but the' public
realises slowly and forgives slowly, and
Labor has hitherto only given the slightest
hint of contrition. The hint is contained
in Tudor’s revival of the plank in the party’s
platform which proposes to take the first
step towards the abolition of Party. The
matter was not pressed on public notice as
it should have been; but the germ is there
and out of it may grow the tall tree of Aus-
tralian liberty of thought. Labor, while its
opponents are blatherskiting about “winning
the war” and showing no means of doing so,
will have leisure to work towards a truly
national ideal. If Labor takes the wise course
and the winning course it will bring Refer-
endum with Initiative, coupled with Elective
Executive and Electoral reform, right into
the firing line. With the absolute freedom
of thought and action which these measures
would confer, Labor, apart from Party,
would win every time. Under the
new political system which these simple
reforms would introduce Phewsion could
not last for an hour; and no mere
Win-the-War gag could possiblv count.
Last week’s elections result may be of
great value. The Hughes-Cook’ combine
represents the last stage In the progress of
Party government. That it should have a
fair chance to prove its own futility may be
the best medicine for the Australian demo-
cracy. The Commonwealth is learning a
lesson; Labor as a detail is learning a
lesson; and if the Phewsion in office is the
price to be paid—well, we can’t expect an
advanced educational course in political
common-sense for nothing.

Prior, F. J. Kiriut and P. M. Wrioiit, 214 George
