The Bountiful Murders of Providence.
Apparently the Rear is thinking about the
Fusion or something. Anyway, there are
circumstantial yarns about parades and com-
mittee meetings at the rear and a suspen-
sion _ of bash at the Front with nobody in
particular being in charge anywhere. All
of which sounds mighty like chaos. That the
situation is going to result in somebody get-
ting hit on a sore place seems certain, for
whether a separate peace were made or not
heavy defeat along the northern sector
would practically eliminate Russia from the
conflict. There are dozens of reasons whv
the German should hit our northern ally
with the heaviest blow he can deliver, and
there is nothing in the published accounts of
what is transpiring to suggest that Russia
may . not . reel back into confused anarchy,
lo imagine that hot-air about the retri-
bution. which overtook Czardom and the
assertion of democratic principles at Mos-
cow has been of assistance to the enemies of
Germany is merely silly. What has occurred,
and is happening now to Ivan are, so far
as this fight is concerned, about the very
last troubles the Allies could have wished
for. The snow will soon be off the ground,
and when that vanishes most of the gloom in
which the Eastern Front is shrouded will
disappear. What may be behind all this
darkness would not be the most pleasant
thing connected with the war to think about
if it. had any features that were other than
horrible. The character of the struggle
going on along the Western Front can hardly
be guessed a.t. The only certain thing about
it is. that, in that red hell human blood is
running like water; that the full story is
not being told because it cannot be. How
long the ghastly tragedy will continue —
where the end will be and when—no man
can tell. Our own lads have been heard
from, again going strong and bravely on.
Heaven only knows how many will be left
at the finish—-but it will be a restful
tnoment for their countrymen when the
Yank arrives to give Billjim a hand.
Likewise it will be a day for John Bull to
look round for any fatted calves there may
be left on the premises when Uncle Sam
puts, in an appearance with a reliable sub-
marine-catching apparatus. Just at present
an inventory of what may be in the pantry
next week makes no mention of fat calves.
Now that ex-Speaker Richard Denis
Meagher has been appointed to the Legisla-
tive Council The Bulletin expects that
before long he again will apply for admission
to the roll of solicitors, and it suspects
that in the light of “his services to the
nation,” say, on the conscription issue, the
Law Institute will forego its old opposition
and open the fold to the stray lamb.
No organisation has applied itself to the
job of looking after Billjim. with more
devotion, courage and well-ordered method
than the Y.M.C.A. If any reader of The
Bulletin wants details of what this selec-
tion of the alphabet has done he need only
ask any returned soldier or write to head-
quarters. In the meantime the Association
wants funds for its war work, and is now
appealing to the inhabitants of N.S.W. for
cash. It is a practical appeal for practical
purposes. This paper verv cordially com-
mends the Association’s efforts to the support
of every citizen, and will guarantee that
donations can be made with absolute confi-
dence in the integrity of the men who handle
the money and the completeness of the
machinery for carrying some of the comforts
of life to Billjim where he wants them most.
Sailed on his last voyage, from the Port
of Adelaide, at 77, Captain Glenn, master
of the Gulf and West Coast service. In the
days before steam he used to sail the ketch
Lillie Hawkins as far afield as Hobart and
Sydney. But his most dangerous experience
was in harbor, in 1892, when the Marine
Board found it had nearly 500 cases of
dynamite in its magazine damaged and liable
to blow Port Adelaide to Hades at any
moment. Glenn had the job of shipping this
out into deep water and getting rid of it.
He. retired from, the sea after just 50 years
of it, during which he had never lost a day’s
duty by illness.
Young McKinley, whose death at the
Front was reported the other day, is the
second of the Paunch McKinley nephews to
fall in the. war. There are still two on
active service. The lad \yho passed out
a fortnight back was thfe only son of
Henry McKinley, the tall, grey Melb. solici-
tor. He was a mere youngster, and had
been through the fighting from the
first day at Gallipoli. The last letter
his parents received from him urged them
to have no fear on his account, as he felt he
had a charmed life. He had just come out of
an engagement in which one of his sleeves
was shot to rags, the other was penetrated in
two places, and a bullet had gone through
his clothing, cutting his shirt, while his hel-
met was so flattened on His head that he
thought it was nailed on.
Montague Purcell, killed in action with
the A.I.F. lately, was born in barracks at
George’s Head, Sydney, where his father
was sergeant-major of the permanent detach-
ment of submarine miners. A brother is an
Australian bluejacket, and was one of the
crew of the Sydney when she finished the
Emden’s career. Two other brothers are
wearing khaki, one as a volunteer from the
Marshall Islands and the other as trump-
eter with Australian artillery in France.

Miriam was a prostitute. She had grad-
uated in her profession in the usual way—
an easily-moulded girl—too easily moulded,
left with a baby, forced to make her living
in the only job open to her, her one way to
escape her environment closed by her “past,”
living from day to day, left in Monte Carlo
one morning with £2O (but not blaming the
man, since he had to go home to his wife),
finally plying her business in Piccadilly, and
doing fairly well, sometimes starving, some-
times in funds, but possessed of three un-
usual and useful attributes; striking beauty,
an engaging charm, and a sense of humor.
She knew her way about, all right. One
night she met Geoffrey and his two male
friends in his flat. Geoffrey was merely
going to the dogs with drink and drugs, since
his girl had chucked him for a millionaire.
He discovers in Miriam a useful antidote to
drugs; and, finding her a good sort, he
determines to give her a chance. He pro-
vides her with a flat, and visits that flat,
with the usual results. Miriam improves in
manners and appearance, and falls in love
with Geoffrey. But the man is still in love
with Valentine, now the wife of a titled
millionaire. He finds Miriam an ideal mis-
tress; yet is not such a fool as to marry her,
especially as he doesn’t love her. But the
girl yearns to marry him, though recognis-
ing that she has no claims upon him. The
usual rows follow. Geoffrey’s common-sense
tells him that marriage with a reformed
prostitute would mean only misery to her,
because this is England, and Geoffrey is a
gent. Then Valentine discovers that her
millionaire is a very attentive husband —too
attentive. She had married him at her
mother’s bidding, without any intimate idea
BERT BAILEY.
of wliat marriage might be like. She clears
out, and goes to cuddle Geoffrey again.
Geoffrey wants her to run off with him to
Buenos Ayres (places like Buenos Ayres
are put on the map for the sole purpose of
helping English dramatists out of diffi-
culties), but Valentine, who is a decent sort,
wakes up to her duty to her husband, and
Geoffrey, feeling lonely, makes the same offer
to Miriam. Still in love with Valentine, he
sees no reason why he shouldn’t make the
best of what is left, and he even offers to
marry the girl. But now she knows better:
her education is complete; she loves him, but
knows that marriage would mean only
misery to them both. So she goes with him
to Buenos Ayres—as his mistress.
* * * •
This is the interesting slice of life that
was served up for the first time on the Aus-
tralian stage at the Criterion on Saturday.
Coming after the cloying sentimentality of
the impossible “Daddy Long Legs,” Hubert
Henry Davies’ “Outcast” is like a breath of
keen, fresh air. The age-old problem of the
prostitute is treated with a common-sense
and a directness and a truth and a freedom
from the least suspicion of sentimentality
that are seldom to be found in the' plays
from our latest Ally. There is not one
false touch or trace of exaggeration through-
out this intensely realistic drama. And it is
an inexpressible relief once again to hear
the English language on an Australian stage.
The small talk is natural and unstrained;
the rows are just rows, without any trace of
a “curtain”; they end without a climax,
with both parties endeavoring to recover
their poise; throughout the dialogue there
is thought and a grace of diction,
and nobody attempts to be smart or
to play little theatric tricks that will
raise a laugh. The man and his friends
are simple, ordinary chaps that we are
pleased to meet, and the prostitute has all
the experience of the ordinary Flossie and
wears no halo on her gorgeous hair. Any-
how, that hair needs no halo.
Charles Waldron proves himself, now that
he has a chance, a thoroughly convincing
actor, equal in his interpretation of the
character of the man to Ivathlene Mac Do-
nnell’s remarkably convincing portrayal of the
prostitute. And that should be sufficient
praise for Charles Waldron. With Louise
Kimball and Lewis Willoughby, put
thoroughly at their ease in natural parts, we
have a trio of altogether charming fellows.
Beryl Bryant, as Valentine, has a most diffi-
cult thing to do, to make the other woman a
human being worthy of sympathy; for the
audience, fresh from plays of the oozily-senti-
mental type of “Daddy Long Legs,” persists
in regarding the love-affair between her and
Geoffrey as mere make-believe, whereas it
remains the only mutual love-affair in the
drama. Beryl Bryant succeeds in ultimately
convincing that hostile audience. As for
Kathlene McDonnell, all this paper can say
of her is that she is the realest, the most
attractive, and the most eerily beautiful of
all the trades-women it has ever met. In
“Outcast” she proves herself one of the most
thoroughly-equipped actresses we have seen
on the Australian stage, to -which she adds a
personal charm and the conquering triumph
of youth. “Outcast” is announced for only
a brief season.

Recruits are recommended to listen to
“The Story of the Rosary” at the Grand
Opera House (Sydney). Nothing that they
may experience on the fields of France will
be as awful as the horrors of the warfare in
this alleged military drama. And they will
pick up quite a lot of useful information re-
garding military discipline, methods of de-
fence and the manners and customs of
officers’ messes (meaning tucker, of course).

The demise of “ Jinker,” at the Royal, has
been unavoidably postponed till Saturday.
“The House that Jack Built” continues to

The Wirths are finishing up their season
at the New Hippodrome, and the managerial
advertisement is doing its best to raise a
panic about it. But the elephant who plays
the mouth-organ betrays no nervousness,
and the flying lioness doesn’t linger in mid-
air to do a thoughtful scratch when going-
through the blazing hoop. A benefit per-
formance for the Ist and 3rd Battalions
Comforts Funds and the R.S.A. is to happen
this (Thursday) afternoon.
The Follies are now mixing all their most
successful drinks in a farewell week at Syd-
ney Tivoli. Little Billy Rego, who was
trodden on by a restive piano, has hobbled
back on to the bill, but isn’t doing any acro-
batic work. One of the cheeriest items is
the burlesque by the small convalescent,
helped by Cannot and Welch. Basso Mal-
colm McEachern and Gwen Lewis provide
valuable artistic support; but Gwen’s musi-
cal version of “Ginger Mick” is too stagey
and feminine. Mick isn’t a good subject for
lady performers. Nellie Stewart has been
engaged to start a Nell Gwynne farewell
presently.

the upper layer of Maoriland, who is helping
the Williamson management, every night
and at matinges, too, "to make the' “House
That Jack Built” such a bewitching edifice.

Last Saturday’s advertisement announced
the last 10 nights of the Marie Tempest
season at Melbourne Royal, therefore “Pene-
lope” should see it to a fairly crowded end.
The comedy of W. S. Maughan has wit and
humor in the dialogue, and strength in its
situations. The characters, conventional or
otherwise, are convincing, and a couple of
incidental visitors to the doctor’s consulting-
room are screamingly funny exaggerations.
Also, the company, with the exception of
Graham Browne, is better-fitted in this
comedy than in “Marriage of Kitty,” which
it resembles as far as the adventuress
(Gwen Burroughs) is concerned. Gregan
McMahon and Sidney Stirling have capital
character-parts, as the uncle and father of
the distressed heroine who is keeping a stiff
upper lip and trying to console herself with
new hats, and frocks. Marie Tempest, han-
dicapped by a bad cold, made a very inter-
esting person of the doctor’s wife, but some-
how the magnetic attraction that ought to
be there must have been partly lost on the
voyage from Europe, or whichever was the
last place the distinguished lady came from.
Her art is very charming, but not bewitch-
ing. One is not haunted by memories of any
particular moments of “Penelope,” but there
is always an impression that the best scenes
between Miss Tempest and Graham Browne
would be more effective in a smaller theatre,
and a feeling that Stirling’s part would be
great in the hands of a gifted “old man”
character-actor.
Some changes in the appearance of “Very
Good Eddie,” at Melbourne King’s, were
necessitated last week by the lamentable
mishap to Lupino on the previous Saturday.
In the first place the temporary retirement
of the dancing comedian brought his under-
study, Leslie Donnagliey, into the name
part, and the foolish character of the little
man in a brown hat which had previously
been Donnagliey’s job was kindly omitted.
The show seemed brighter for the absence
of the wandering interloper in the brown
hat, whilst the man in Lupino’s place did
everything his original had done, bar the
acrobatic dance. Donnaghey, one-time of
Pollard’s Juveniles, is considerably smaller
than Lupino, though not so boyish-looking.
He made a more grotesque creature of
Eddie, and his smallness proved to be the
essence of the artless Yankee joke. The
humor in the story is mainly physical. The
contrast between Mr. and Mrs. Kettle must
be the reverse of the contrast between the
other honeymoon couple. If Mr. Darling
and Mrs. Kettle were each seven feet high
the situation would be screamingly funny in
its rudimentary way. So the non-imported
“VERY GOOD EDDIE” AT

as Dick, feeling
happy.

upset.
Donnaghey got along joyously on his natural
sense of absurdity and his striking lack of
inches. He might have been less imitative
with even better results. In other respects
the show was as before, and the music of the
two principal numbers continued to suggest
a couple of melodious hymn tunes served
with coon sauce.

At Melb. Majesty’s they are shifting
“Canary Cottage” on Saturday to make way
for some more “So-Long, Letty.” The
tragedy of “The Three Twins” is understood
to be in rehearsal, but a revival of “Letty”
is one of those things which may go on
indefinitely, anyhow.
Ada Reeve has tightened her grip of Mel-
bourne’s good opinion by adding a tabloid
tragedy to her big share of the bill at the
Tivoli. “Half an Hour,” by J. M. Barrie,
is a condensed variation of the old story of
the smart woman whose sneering cad of a
husband taunts her into an intention to
elope with the other fellow, this prospective
co-respondent being all that a lady could
desire under the scandalous circs. But when
she is about to start for Egypt from the
man’s rooms he rushes out to get a cab and
is killed by a motor-car, whereupon the doc-
tor advises her to escape from her awkward
predicament. So she returns home, where a
small dinner-party awaits her, and the doc.
aforesaid is one of the guests. Also hubby’s
suspicions are presently aroused. Never-
theless the heroine’s powers of dissimula-
tion are equal to the awful strain, and she
goes triumphantly off to dinner on the
solemn medical person’s arm. Ada Reeve is
not quite an Ethel Irving in such an exact-
ing part, but she is more than effective
enough, and the support scores a highly-
respectable average. Blakiston, Talleur
Andrews, and Ashton Jarry figure in the
support, the latter presenting a forcible type
of luridly-offensive husband. The odd point
about these tragical stage marriages is that
whereas the ill-used wife is invariably sup-
posed to have the best of the brains she
never knows how to make her husband
reasonably unpleasant. In the next part of
the programme Ada Reeve sings a large
assortment of songs in sundry tones, rang-
ing from homely delf to finest Dresden china,
and her only trouble is to get away when
she has had about an hour of it. Louis
Nikola is doing some new tricks, which he
calls illusions, and would be justified in
describing as miracles. Also, Jacobs, the
giddy young man in charge of the orchestra,
delivers patter ditties with the same in-
gratiating smile as before.

“The Bunyip” panto, at Melb. Princess’s,
is going on until the 25th for certain. The
kids of the city have claimed it for their

There is no shortage of sugar at Hoyt’s
Theatres, Melbourne, this week. The film-
fancier will find large quantities of the sub-
stance in “The Promise,” a five-act Metro
specialty wherein Harold Lockwood battles
against a formidable opposition of dirty lefts
and parental curses to reach the expectant
arms of beauteous May Allison. Crane Wil-
bur is responsible for the heart-throbs in the
other Hoyt narrative, “Wasted Years,”
which alleges that Bohemia is adjacent to
Bedlam when a bloque goes thoroughly gay.

Douglas Fairbanks, the restless Triangle
comedian, is chasing a girl in “The Matri-
rnaniac” at Melbourne Majestic this week,
and is tumbling over the usual tribe of
obstacles in his rush for his objective. The
film runs to five acts of violent energy, but
a practised lunatic like Fairbanks can easily
do the distance. Dorothy Dalton spreads
herself in the Triangle-Ince drama, “The
Female of the Species.” Dorothy is described
as a lady of “daring unconventionality,” and
wears frocks to prove it.

A notable show was a performance of “As
You Like It” at Adelaide Queen’s Hall, with-
out a man in it, in aid of the Red Cross
funds. The caste had been long and carefully
coached, even to bringing in the expert
Shorthose to advise on the wrestling-match.
The Society crowd that turned up to scoff
at the sight of its younger female members
in unusual guise got a pleasant surprise, for
the acting was unexpectedly good. Touch-
stone scored heavily, Celia made her part
stand out, Orlando and Rosalind and Jaques
were sound, and the rest, even to the fem-
inine Charles-the-wrestler, played up man-
fully ; and, strange to say, the voice of the
prompter was not heard. James Anderson,
the coach, had done wonders with the girls,
most of whom were without any previous ex-
perience of the stage.

Jawbone Webster has issued an illustrated
address to his constituents declaring that,
during his reign at the G.P.0., the Gwydir
electorate has been “transformed in its
post offices and telegraph and telephone ser-
vices as a result of his efforts, and holds
premier position of distant country electo-
rates throughout Australia.” If this means
anything it means that Webster has used the
country’s money to bestow special postal
and telegraph facilities on his own electo-
rate, and in return for this favoritism he
craves continued support. There is, of
course, no suggestion of bribery. It is
merely Webster’s way of winning the war.
At time of writing a German named
von Rinterlen is being tried in New
York on the charge that he, being a pro-
pagandist with German money to burn, did
endeavor to stop the supply of munitions
and otherwise upset policies favorable to the
Allies. The country where this man operated
was neutral during most of the time that
he was spending the dollars, if not all of
the time, but the American Government is
calling him to account and is making no
mystery about it. That German spies and
agents of the von Rinterlen class were, and
are, to be found in this country is a common-
sense supposition. Have there been any
public trials of suspected persons? Is the
personal sentiment of the Government, or
its lack of secret service, accountable for
the suspects’ immunity from prosecution?
The question is asked by every thinking
citizen. One of the stock excuses of the
slackers is that they will not enlist whilst
dangerous aliens hold billets where they
have the chance to be mischievous, or whilst
anti-war propogandists, whose propaganda
is obviously pro-German in the face of the
present war map, are talking at large.
These scandals have brought Phewsions’
Win-the-War cry into intelligent contempt,
but if anything was ever said about them
during the blessed election campaign the
Censor must have kept it out of print.

PENELOPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Penelope, a doctor’s spouse, loved hubby very dearly,
Whilst he unto his marriage vows was not so true,
or nearly;
But, victim to a wanton whim,
A smart grass-widow captured him.
Oh, she was tall and she was sliml
They kept the matter dark, or dim,
But Wifey saw it clearly.
The rhymster, needing easy scope,
Prefers to call her Penny-lope.
She called her Mother to her aid and asked for her
advising,
Her Uncle and her lawyer staid were kind and
sympathising,
And, best of all, her ancient Dad
Reviewed the situation sad
And bade his daughter not be mad,
But treat her hubby’s latest fad
As something not surprising.
“Be patient, child, and give him rope,”
Said Dad to pining Penny-lope.
“You flattered your tin household god; you openly
adored him;
You danced attendance on his nod—in point of fact
you bored him,
And now, to win your lawful mate,
Don’t whimper and bemoan your fate,
Or ask him where he stopped out late;
But let him crave your kiss—and wait
Awhile ere you reward him.”
“Thus will I with the temptress cope,”
Quoth patient little Penny-lope.
For weeks the slim grass-w'idow smiled and simply
went on grazing,
Whilst Wifey played the artless child, no row or
rumpus raising.
Poor Hubby, at her call and beck,
Found Beauty’s halter round his neck.
She called upon him for a cheque,
And passion’s craft became a wreck—■
Which was not so surprising;
Then finally he cut the rope,
And turned again to Penny-lope.
But when he learned that Wifey wise had curbed
her agitations,
And gammoned to believe his lies and clumsy fabri-
cations,
Whilst smiling at each vain pretence,
His indignation was intense;
He railed against her “moral sense.”
Still Wifey kept him in suspense
lie marital relations,
And day by day he got his dope
Of cool neglect from Penny-lope.
It serves its purpose—on the stage, where dramatist
romancer
Can make the Missus sweetly sage, and quite a
killing-glancer;
But oftentimes in real life
The trouble leads to bitter strife,
Philander, lacking timely knife
To cut his rope, deserts his wife.
Her tactics didn’t answer.
To stop him slipping down the slope
Philander needs a Penny-lope.
VICTORIA.
O. K.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“0.K.”: One of the Marie Tempest pufflets
alleges that when the unreticent lady pro-
duced ‘‘Penelope” in America some of the local
members of the co. persisted in calling the
word Penny-lope. At first the ear-shocked
Marie felt that she must scream or assault
these awful people, but afterwards the
annoyance subsided into a source of amuse-
ment, and she forgave them their ignorance.
The idea that they may have intended the
Penny-lope pronunciation for satire doesn’t
seem to have occurred to Marie Tempest
“Stargazer” : We have a clutch of picture-
film censors here in Melbourne now, chosen,
apparently, because of their aloofness from
mere drab humanity, and now that they are
in action picture-theatre patrons would like
to know what they are doing, and to hear
their reasons for permitting a few film
dramas now in operation to continue off the
chain, There is one continuous frenzy, for
instance, called “Peg o’ the Ring,” which
for rampant and frenzied folly beats any-
thing I have efer seen on earth or dreamt
of elsewhere, and my experience of shows
dates back to Richardsonian booths at old-
fashioned fairs. The old Richardsonian melo-
dra'ina, with its buck-toothed, broad-belted
pirates, and its virtuous village milkmaids,
was a model of intellectual propriety and
ethical repose compared with “Peg o’ the
Ring,” which is sheer madness from post to
finish. I am assuming that the duty of the
censors is to safeguard the picture-patron’s
commonsense, while keeping his moral pinny
clean. A series of debased episodes like the
dramatic kidney fits dished up by the perpe-
trators of “Peg” is far more dangerous to
the large callow section of picture-theatre
frequenters than any loose or lewd five-reel
flight I have yet seen. Three doses of it are
sufficient to leave a youngster’s mind in a
putrid condition, and I doubt if the more
stupid kind of small boy who is allowed to
see it out will be able to think lucidly again
in 10 years. The censorship that permits
this rubbish to stalk at large has already
published its own utter condemnation.
“Snell”: “0.K.” in claiming that the
mannerisms of an actor are not more con-
spicuous or more to be condemned than those
of the ordinary citizen who displays per-
sistent peculiarities even in the way be lights
his pipe, is blinding himself to the nature of
the actor’s art, it seems to me. In claiming
that marked mannerisms are merely an evi-
dence of strong individuality he may be per-
fectly correct, but the inoffensive, suburban
citizen is not called upon to subordinate him-
self and his special peculiarities to the de-
mands of an exacting art. The actor is in
that place in which it is demanded of him
that lie sink his aggressive mannerisms in
order to bring home to us with tolling P>rcc
the mannerisms and special qualities'of the
character he is attempting to portray. I
contend that an actor is a bad actor to the
extent that his own pronounced peculiarities
are stamped upon the numerous personalities
of the play to whom he has introduced us,
whether the actor be Gaston Mervale,
George Titheradge or Gus Franks.
Just established in Australia a branch of
the British Ideal Film Co. —a star-gemmed
organisation that should make the Yank
firms nervous about their hold on the Aus-
tralian movie business. Starting about five
years ago in London, the Ideal Co. went
after the leaders of the English stage and
got them. Amongst their players are George
Alexander, Henry Ainley, Albert Chevalier,
•Tames Welch, Ellen Terry, the Tree lady,
George Robey and John Hare. The opening
pictures to be reeled off in Australia are
“The Yicar of Wakefield.” “The Second Mrs.
Tanqueray,” “Lady Windermere’s Fan,”
“The New ‘Chum” and “Her Greatest Per-
formance.”

THE EFFIGY ACTOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He sought a brief engagement there,
And told the city swell
Such minor parts as they could spare
Would suit him very well,
But truth to tell he meant to go
To London in a year or so.
This place would never more than give
A pro. the chance to merely live.
Just clap him into anything.
He’d fit the part. In fact
He had a notion he could sing;
He knew that he could act.
“What have you done?” The tone was rude.
He-struck a splendid attitude.
“Shaw, Sliakspeare, Romeo, Melnotte,
Macbeth, and things I’ve quite forgot.”
They put him on. He was a treat.
No worse had trod a stage!
He fell across the hero’s feet,
And bit him in his rage.
“Come off,” the great man yelled, “and go !
Where was it you played Romeo?”
“At Swithin’s Waxworks.” Then, with pride,
“It ran two hundred nights!” he cried.

PENFOLD'S
WINES
KEEPING HIS HAND IN.
FIRST OLD FELLOW: “That’s the worst of
Dr. Brown, when he’s not playing the
piano, he’s ‘harping on the internal
organs’!”
Australian soldiers have' fought like men
and died the death of men. In tight places
and hours of unexpected, sudden peril they
have stood the test. But if the Australian
soldier has a sense of duty he also has a
sense of humor, and this is being put to
a severe strain. Wherefore it seems about
time that the Censor or someone came to
Biiljinvs rescue and modified some of the
adulatory mixtures he is being sprinkled
with. It is making Billjim very tired this
stuff, for there are other soldiers in this
fight, and the Australian has seen them.
Having seen them, he reads about himself
being a hero and various brands of super-
man with a great weariness, often with a
hot face. Some of the official messages about
the Australian stir him to fury, though per-
haps an exception ought to be made of the
yarn about the sinking of the Ballarat, which
drew u moving picture of Billjim maintaining
the tradition of the Birkenhead playing two-
up, This was calculated to make Billjim
suspect that a humorist was pulling his leg.
The story of the Commonwealth troops' valor
will not suffer if someone or other could
manage to draw it a bit mild and cease from
irritating the men it refers to.
* * * *
“O.lv.’s” idea of kings and coronets: —
H. G. Wells, having candidly referred to the
“great volume of Republican feeling that has always
existed in the British community,” is now trying to
persuade himself that practical encouragement of the
Republican movement in Central Europe “need not
conflict with loyalty to the Throne” in England.
But “loyalty to the Throne” means loyalty to the
wholly un-Republican idea that the mummery which
doth hedge a king is worth preserving for senti-
reasons. A monarch without mummery is
not worth considering. But a limited monarchy
without a titled aristocracy and a House of Lords is
well worth thinking about. Take away ail the
coronets, but leave the Crown. There’s a King of
Heaven. People sing of Him in church. There’s no
duke, marquis, earl or K.C.M.G. of Heaven; but
religion demands a king.
“Fitzshrapnel”: One of the advantages of
serving under a real Australian O.C. is that
you have a friend who will tear down
stupid red-tape entanglements to get you
reasonable comforts. One good Australian
in charge of a camp for wounded Anzacs
found that, /while his own men were bedded
on two planks and a straw tick, the Huns
in a neighboring internment camp were repos-
ing disgracefully on modern iron beds, with
wire springs and decent mattresses. An
Imperial officer would have hesitated, but
the Australian charged straight at the Home
Office and pretty soon—well, the wounded
Anzacs had the best beds.
“The Old Soldier” : Apropos British army
decorations and the allegation of “Bio” (B.
20/4/’l7) that there are too many of them.
After the Crimea a C.O. of a rifle battalion
who fought there was asked to recommend
men for the V.C. (then just instituted). He
sent in a complete list of the rank and file,
expressing the opinion that they all deserved
the medal and that he could not single out
one man more worthy than another. He
was right, too.

“Rio”: In the “Nineteenth Century and
After” for February C. G. Wade has an
article on the Australian Conscription Refer-
endum, written last November. In it he
says :
Nine morths of the war had elapsed before a
single Australian was killed in action.
So it took N. S. Wales’s Agent-General just
two years to forget all about the officers and
men killed at Rabaul and the three sailors
who their lives in the Sydney-Emden
scrap! Yet I’ll lose a bet if he didn’t make
many a speech assuring soldiers that their
deeds would never be forgotten. One would
have thought that a North Sydney man, a
footballer and a ’Varsity graduate would
have remembered Brian Pockley a little
longer.
* * * *
Bondi (Sydney) scores the prize horror
of the week. There was a “carnival,” and
one of the amusements was a shute into the
sea. Two youngsters took a turn. The
first was impaled on a splinter, about
an inch in diameter, which went right
through the boy’s abdomen and projected a
couple of feet behind. The poor lad re-
mained affixed to the terrible thing until some
strong hands broke the wood. The other boy
was bored at the buttock, and a doctor later
on extracted I Sin. of firewood from his body.

Concerning a more or less ornamental job:
The office of Lieutenant-Governor of N. S. Wales
has been variously filled. In the old days, the
N. S. Wales Corps Commandants took the chair in
the absence of his Ex. During Governor Bligh's
suspension, Johnston, Foveaux and Pat*son, all of
the “Rum Corps,” acted. Other lieutenants
came in with Major-General Stewart, 3rd Buffs, who,
in 1825, “acted” for 12 days and pardoned a number
of men left for death by Governor Brisbane. When
the Imperial soldiery left Australia the Supreme Court
got a show, Chief Justice Alfred Stephen being the
first to administer. James Martin succeeded Stephen
as C.J., and while Governor Robinson was annexing
Fiji-—he was six weeks absent —his Honor claimed
to act, but Henry Parkes refused to bring into
activity the dormant commission. An unseemly
■ squabble ensued, undignified on both sides, soiled
linen was hung out, with the upshot that Alfred
Stephen was appointed to the job for life. He
signed the register four times. On his resignation,
through old age, C.J. Darley succeeded, and after
him W. P. Cullen, now holding the office. Cullen
will reside at Government House, as Darley did for
a while; none of the others occupied the place.

From Flanders: Jim, transport driver,
famous even in the metal polish and
bath-brick cursed Army Service for the
brilliancy of his steelwork, arrived back at
his lines after the nignt’s work at Dead Dog
and other salubrious spots. Around his head
dispersing the mists of morning flickered an
aureole of Australian curses. “What’s
crawlin’ on yer, cobQer?” asked a sympa-
thetic friend. “Drove through a belt of that
blanky .gas !” stormed Jim. “ ’Ard luck !
Did they get you or the ’orses?” “Ob, blarst
me an’ the ’orses— look at me blanky steel-
work /”
* * * *
Judge Docker (75), lately sentencing a
Sydney woman who had been convicted of
getting money by false pretences, heaved a
curse at the tote:—
It seemed to him that when the gambling spirit
got held of a woman it was evil for her as the
drink habit, and he expected now that the Govern-
ment had legalised gambling, and was about to take
a part of the proceeds, he would find many more
women in trouble because of its influence.
But why? Is lovely woman likely to
become a more frequent gambler because she
is able to gamble with the honest tote instead
of with the more-or-less honest Moses?
Anyhow, by gambling with the honest tote
—which will pay her a fair div. —she will be
less likely to appear before Judge Docker for
dishonesty, because she will be less likely to
go broke.
“Lip”: That Brother Billjim who told
The Bulletin some time back that the Tom-
mies’ boots aren’t in it with our own Austra-
lian issue must have written from
Horseferry road. Grudgingly though I admit
it, it is the other way about. The heads
evidently admit it, too, for the latest of our
divisions to arrive in France came shod with
Tommy boots,
their own issue
having been taken
from them before
they left Eng-
land. The Aus-
tralian boot ap-
pears to have
been b'u ill for
Egypt and sand,
and the French
paved roads play
the devil with
them. Those of
us who arrived
with the first lot
saved our Aus-
tralian boots by
immediately copy-
ing the Tommy
issue and getting
iron heel and toe
plates and studs
put on—at our
own expense. An-
other grouch we
have is about
our great-coat.
Many a time I’ve
done a perish in
one even in
Egypt; yet we
are expected to
stick it in the
same old coat
over here, where
it’s 20 degrees
below freezing
point, and the
snow that fell
three weeks ago is
still lying around
half a foot deep.
Hundreds of us
keep decently
warm in bor-
rowed (or otherwise) Tommy coats now.
We are all proud of our Australian clothes
and reckon there are none like ’em—in a
proper white man’s climate. For this sod-
den wilderness you need the heavy clothes
made by people who know the horrors of
winter.
A countrywoman of the lady’s in protest:
Professor Scott, of Melbourne, sacrificed historical
truth for the smart sentence when he said of
Catherine the Great of Russia—“ She had. the heart
of a fish and the morals of a suburban catl” (B.
19/4/’l7). Apart from mere material reforms
Catherine realised the higher needs of her people.
She did everything possible to attract great literary
men from all parts of Europe to her court, and in
1755 she founded the first Imperial Russian theatre.
Incidentally she was an author, and wrote several
plays. In its grasp of life and knowledge of human
nature her letter to her grandson, as a guide for his
conduct, is a masterpiece. Her most passionate love
was given to her country. As to her other loves—
well, she was abnormal in intellect and character,
so, like most abnormalities, artistic and otherwise,
she cannot logically be measured by the common foot-
rule. But measure her how you will, the fish-cat
valuation is strained and false.

DOING HER BIT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was a madame stout and stern,
With eyes that seemed to fairly burn,
A nose that lift to sniff the air
As though it scented treason there;
And, as the tram went on to town,
She eyed the stranger tall and brown
And thus reproachfully began:
“Why aren’t you at the Front, young man,
Where thousands of our boys have bled ?”
“Well, I ” the young man said.
“Why not she urged. “Your country calls.
Racecourses, movies, music-halls—
These are the things that keep you back,
Although our noble heroes lack
The help of strapping youths like you.
There is the Empiah’s work to do;
You should enlist at once—to-night—
And help Australia’s men to fight!”
The young man blushed a rich, warm red:
“W’y, well, y’know”—he said. •
“Excuses will not help you. No!”
She snorted. “Do not argue. Go!
Our splendid men demand your aid.
Yet you hang back —are you afraid?
The young man grinned a painful grin,
And fidgeted, and rubbed his chin.
“W’y, missus!” he protested, while
His grin became a cynic smile
As he hopped off, and straightway fled,
“W’y, I, I’ve been 1” he said.

All Australia’s battlers are not at the
Front. There’s James Bond!, miner, of
Cobar (N.S.W.), for instance. The other
day he fell 180 ft. from the top of a shaft
and when liis mates went to collect the pieces
he casually entertained them with an account
of his journey. Items: fractures of the right
and left thighs, right kneecap, right ankle,
left leg, both collarbones, both arms (in
several places), etc. After a bit of tinker-
ing up lie travelled all the way to Sydney
without threatening to write a letter to
the Railway Commissioners about the jolt-
ing of the train.
“Queenslander” from France:—
Notes re life in trenches in Bulletin of 30th
November, 1916, are very true. Rats are in swarms
and little can be done to get rid of them. We
have had some snow, and the footprints on the
surface give' some idea of the size and number of
the pest. Parcels from Australia containing food-
stuffs should always be sent in tin boxes, as when
a quantity is received at night they have to remain
undelivered for some hours and Johnny Rat has a
real blow-out. When we’re able to have a blow-
out ourselves the beer is light, cheap and good, but
I cannot give much of a character to the wine.
English papers are procurable in billets a day after
date of issue, and The Times is in demand. Rations
are plentiful and of excellent quality. Our Aus-
tralians are standing the extreme cold well and the
general health of the troops is good. Girl is almost
an unknown quantity and what there is of her is
not of the kind we expected from the advertisements.
Australia’s good-lookers are absent.

“Copabella”: John Hop is a versatile
bloque. The other day frenzied wails from
the police office led me in to investigate what
unfortunate was getting the third degree.
Inside I found a fatherly senior-constable
vainly trying to pacify a tiny stray with
helmet and handcuffs for toys, while the
youngster from the depths of the sergeant’s
chair called high Heaven to witness his
intolerable wrongs. “What we want in this
office,”. said the perspiring sergeant, “is a
good picture-book!” He was serious, too;
he had been through the trouble before. It
is indeed a serious thing in an • efficiency-
loving department that the omission of this
necessary requisite hasn’t been rectified long
ago, because there are times when even a
watehhouse-keeper is too busy to play “boo !”
or “ring-a-rosy” with stray toddlers, especi-
ally when a real “crook” might be lugged
any moment.

“M. 8.”: What’s wrong with leaving the
age limit out of consideration in enlisting
men willing to go to the Front? The mark
has been raised to allow men of over 45
to get to where the iron compliments are
being swapped. But. why 45, or 55, or 65?
There are men in the far back as good at
70 as ever they were, and they were good
goods always. When I was in the Far
North, old Ned Simmons was colt-breaking
wherever there were horses to be broken.
He was 70 then, and he was not the only
horseman in the North whose face-weed was
very white. I’d enlist any man between 17
and 70 who wanted to fight or to help the
fighters aud who could answer medical tests.
“E.8.L.”: Every weary step our soldiers
take puts more money into the pockets of
greedy war profiters. I have a wrapper
off a packet of wool, which wool I have been
buying at Is. Id. a packet (two to each pair
of socks). I believe that a couple of months
ago it was sold at ,B4d. per packet. On
Saturday week I got enough for a pair of
socks at 2s. 2d- But the other day the same
quantity was 2s. 8d. —a rise of 6d. in a
week. And here is the plain track of the
bushrangers. During the week of the rise
various Australian papers had made special
mention of the fact that 100,000 pairs of
socks were needed by the end of May. You
will notice that the wool is an Australian
make. The firm may have a good excuse
for their extraordinary inci’eases. If they
have, it would be as well for them to adver-
tise it promptly, as I am only one of many
wondering what brand of patriotism theirs is.
This is the sort of thing that helps to Win
the War.
“Snell”: Here is a nice little item con-
cerning a gifted spruicker who was employed
by the teetotal organisations in one Aus-
tralian metropolis to go out and boost the
cause of prohibition. He boosted all right,
but in a fortnight's time the leaders of the
cold-water cause felt themselves called upon
to admonish the eloquent one for his own
somewhat notorious friendship for the
bottle. “Look here,” was the reply; “keep
me or leave me. I simply can’t preach pro-
hibition till I’m three-parts tanked!”

“A.E.L.” holds an inspection:—
When word was brought along' by the train orderly
that Brigadier-General Blazes was coming over to
inspect us, camp was cleaned up, teeth were cleaned,
everybody had a shave and a brush-up, and a work-
ing party scrubbed the skyline. At the appointed
hour the drivers were all strung out in line, each
dangling a pair of shining bits that had been polished
and cursed over, and an orderly sergeant was posted
to signal the coming of the person responsible for
all the fuss. ‘Presently he whizzed over the hill
in a motor-car flying the usual red flag, and sur-
rounded by his red-tabbed staff. We saw him
scramble out of the car trying to look like a wooden
Indian as he walked rapidly past, followed by his
Man Friday. Within five minutes he was back in
his motor, his staff packed round him. When he
was well away after lunch the orderly passed the
word that he was a d(ned fine fellow.
“Hussar”: Re saluting (B. 19/4/T7).
According to customs and regulations of the
British Army, if any number of officers are
saluted by one or more men, the senior
officer only acknow-
ledges the salute. It
is not the uniform the
soldier salutes; it is
the King’s commission.
The courtesy may
seem irksome to some,
but they cannot be of
much value as soldiers
if a small thing like
that disturbs their
equilibrium. All sol-
diering is based on dis-
cipline, and a good
man never shrinks
from it or cavils at it.
Some Australian sol-
diers are, however,
tricked into giving
needless salutes in a
curious way. The
Commonwealth drill
instructors wear a
commissioned officer’s
Sam Browne belt. In
Queen-street, Bris-
bane, yesterday, I saw
a returned wounded
soldier salute one of
these non-coms., who
gravely returned ’the
courtesy, instead of
explaining that he was
entitled to none. On
the other hand, at a
corner I saw four new
soldiers utterly ignore
a returned wounded
officer, a man of at least 35 or 40, with two
African medals aud a Military Cross.
“O’Phimerty” : Two old Hobart pubs are
about to go dry. One of them, the All
Nations, in Collins-street, was the Speck's
first post-office. When the P.O. was moved
to its present site, the old building was
enlarged and turned into a drinkery. Now
it is to be pulled down to allow the Commer-
cial Bank of Tas. to build something impres-
sive. The Alabama, in Liverpool-street, is,
it is said, to be changed into a rag ware-
house. This place was built on the plan of
the old London coffee houses, and until quite
recently it was possible to see the queer, box-
like compartments into which the customers
retired to drink their ale like Englishmen.
“Splinters” gives the home-people a look-
in :
First you hear the boom of Fritz’s gun, then the
whine of the shells coining over; then —bang! A
head-splitting crash that ties your inside in a knot
and makes you feel like throwing yourself down on
your face and crying. They are so cruel, these shells;
The splinters lying about speak for themselves. About
here there are tons of them. The one I hold nearly
covers the palm of my hand, with an edge sharp
as a razor. The top is bent over into a hook, its
jagged edge with teeth like a saw, weighs nearly
a pound. A piece from the shrieking parcel would
perhaps tear off a leg. I have seen some ghastly
examples of mutilation by shell fire, and have come
to imagine every iron splinter a living devil that
at the moment of its body’s bursting starts out in
search of live flesh, with the venomous intention
of inflicting as wicked a wound as possible.
Up in wood there’s a young tree with what
looks like a rough iron spike sticking out of its
battered trunk a few feet from the ground. It
isn’t a spike, but a shell splinter, driven so hard
into the trunk as to be immovable.
If you happen to be in the vicinity of a shell-
burst, and a splinter lobs your way, don’t try pick-
ing it up, give it time to cool; and while it is
cooling beat to a healthier atmosphere, for Fritz
is sure to put another over, and j'ou might get the
whole show.
“Lucky I didn’t get a crack with that,” you
think. “But,” you think again, and you think for
a long while. “Suppose I did get a crack with it.
Suppose I got a ‘blighty’ out of it. Just a nice
little blighty, no bones smashed, no limb chopped
off. Not a wound like that chap down the road,
who writhed in the mud with his breast slashed
open, while the horse he had been riding lay
spattered. No; but one just good enough to have
me sent to Blighty, and a short spell of comfort. To
sleep between white sheets. To be waited on, and,
above all, to be clean 1 No reveilles, no parades.
Then, convalescence, discharge from hospital, and 14
days’ furlough. Fourteen glorious dais, free l
Whiz-z-z-z, duck, duck down for your life—Bang!”
There are remarkable Victorian honor-
ariums who have apparently a sort or ouo-
dued sympathy for the ways of the Hun.
Recently two of them considered the case of
William Cairns, 36, who had recklessly
thrown a “sprat” off the Port Melbourne
pier and then invited a toddler of five to dive
for it. As the water was 18ft. below, and
there was only 3ft. of it, the infant declined,
whereupon William pushed him in. As
another little lad, but a year or two older,
happened to be handy, the compulsory diver
wasn’t drowned, so the astute beaks reckoned
it worth a whole £1 —15s. for the shock,
4s. 6d. for the wetting and 6d. for the lost
sprat.

TO THE GLORY OF SPRING!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In violet time,
Let’s ring a chime,
Of joy upon the bells of folly.
Let's make a rhyme
hi violet time;
Who cares a jot for melancholy?
Primroses climb
With sweet wild thyme
About the ribs of Winter’s brolly.
Eacli forest mime
Trolls jocund rhyme,
In chuckling notes so fat and jolly.
In violet time
’Tis no great crime
To change old loves for new ones, Molly.

“Chevron”: The best shot in his battalion,
Percy Black, who rose from private to major,
and could write his name with machine-gun
bullets on a practice target, has fired his last,
round. Killed in action in France. He en-
listed from the Yilgarn goldfields (W.A.)
in the war’s first month, and was
at Gallipoli with the 16 th Battalion
from the Lauding to the Evacuation. A
D.C.M. and mention in despatches. In
France he won the D.S.O. for leading his
company across No Man’s Land under fii-e
and capturing an enemy’s strong point. He
also gained the French Croix de Guerre and
the Medaille Militaire. In one action he
was wounded in shoulder and neck by a
bomb, but rejoined within a month. This
was his first absence from his battalion.
Black, who was 39 years of age, had been
recommended for a lieutenant-colonelcy, and
was the champion rifle, revolver and
machine-gun shot of the Sixteenth.
Arthur Adams salutes his brothers: —
Harrimac, Billjim’s brother from Maoriland, now
present in bulk in Sydney streets, reveals himself
as a distinct racial type. The bloke who wears
the peak of his hat on top, in order to distinguish
himself from Billjim, not in disdain but in a proper
patriotic pride, is less of a lissom streak of careless
slenderness and more of a nugget than Billjim. His
ruddy- and placid face, his closely-knit frame, with
its suspicion, doubtless unwarranted, of fat, and his
sturdy but somewhat brief legs and thighs show
what one century of the boisterous, liquid, temperate
climate of the windy isles can do for a race.
Harrimac has remained closer to his British stock,
though he betrays the better food and the better
life that have been his since his birth. Billjim has
sprouted more. Harrimac remains phlegmatic and
English; Billjim is casual and already half-Latin.
Harrimac will make the ideal infantry plugger, the
thoroughbred ; Billjim is the ideal mounted infantry,
rather like a racehorse himself, with more nerves
than are good for him. But why does Harrimac
spoil his uniform by wearing black boots? Our
flappers don’t really care for those boots. By the
way, in this welcome consignment the proportion of
conscripts is very small. On their first day in
camp they demanded butter. Most of them grow
butter, and they look as if they liked it.
The size of the colony of Australian
artists in London is appai-ent from the num-
ber of Australian pictures to be seen at the
present Royal Academy. Streeton, Long-
staff, Burgess (the painter of the Australian
Fleet entering Sydney Harbor in the N. S.
Wales Gallery), Power (whose big painting
of the Australian Light Horse adorns the
same place), Quinn (who has fine portraits
there, too), Coates (who holds a travelling
scholarship), Birley, Webb Gilbert, the Mel-
bourne cook who first sculped pastry but
rose by the art in him to bigger things in
marble, Miss Evan Benson, from Westralia,
and Harold Parker, another sculptor who
learned his trade in Brisbane —this is an
array of names that shows that Australia is
there. There are others missing: Lambert,
who may, of course, disdain the R.A., Tom
Roberts, who is perhaps too busy as a hospi-
tal orderly, Mackennal, the sculptor, Fred
Leist, Will Dyson, who is painting official
pictures at the Front—and official pictures
by this caricaturist should have at least an
historical and humorous value —and Bunny,
who is too far away in Paris to bother with
English art.
Walter C. Hawkins, who died at Black-
town (N.S.W.) last month in the 95th lap,
was the first Anglican priest to pass a plate
on the Manning River (N.S.W.) away back
in 1860, when he had to pull up and down
the river per boat to round up his parish-
ioners. He continued to expound the Word
in various parts of the Ma State till 1911,
and was one of the last Anglican priests to
draw a regular Government pension, dating
from the days of “State aid” to religion,
which was abolished in 1863.
“Chevron” : Two light-horsemen who will
never meet again on the field are the Thros-
sell brothers —Lieut. Hugo, V.C., and his
younger brother Eric, a second-lieutenant,
who received his commission in Hugo’s
battalion on September 8 last. > They met
each other on a battlefield in Eastern Egypt
only a few days before the action in which
Eric was killed and Hugo was seriously
wounded: The elder brother won the V.C.
with the 10th Light Horse in the three-
weeks’ vigil after the disastrous charge on
Russell’s Top, Gallipoli. The occasion was
a furious bomb-fight in a trench which was
the key to Hill 60. Of Throssell’s com-
panions, Corporal Ferrier had his arm blown
to bits, Corporal McNee won the D.C.M.,
and Trooper Renton, who lost a leg, also
gained a D.C.M.
“Catchline”: Starting in life as a London
coster and ending as a property-owner
worth £5OOO, old “Apple Jack,” of Williams-
town (Vic.), has died at the age of 84. The
ancient was John G. Pennell, who began to
accumulate his fortune at the age of 10,
hiding on a cellar rafter the tips he got fox-
holding horses and running errands. Weary-
ing of his job as a bricklayer’s ’prentice, he
joined a man-o’-war, left it to marry and
emigrate, and landed with a wife and fully
Is. 6d. in Adelaide. Moving on to Melbourne,
the couple lived on the Yarra Bank, and sold
fruit to incoming boats, went to the diggings,
without luck, and held a stall at Paddy s
Market by day and a barrow outside the old
Queen’s Theatre by night. At Williams-
town he was chimney-sweep, bellman and
fruit-hawker, and he introduced the great
British pastime of Aunt Sally at local fetes.
Most of his money goes to his nephew, a lad
of 14.
“Veritas” from Palm Isle: —
A recent visitor to Norfolk Island gives some
wrong impressions of the late Mrs. Fletcher Nobbs,
better known here as “Aunt Susan” or “Grandma.
She was a very intelligent and popular woman, and
it was her strong personality which made her famous
and respected. She was not at all keen to have
tourists call upon her, as stated, but if they did
visit her home most of them were welcome. She
was most kind and hospitable. Far from meddling
in “matrimonial matters,” she never bothered about
them, 'except where her own children were con-
cerned.
Passed out in Texas (U.S.A.), aged 76,
James McClenahan, who for 25 years sailed
the waves of George’s river (N.S.W.) as
master of the steam ferry-punt at Tom
Ugly’s Point. McClenahan drove a railw:xy
engine between Waterford and Limerick in
his native Ireland before coming to Aus-
tralia in 1881. He settled in the U.S.A.
after retiring from the bridge five years ago.
“N. or M.” : My remembrance of the late
missionary Brown dates back a number of
years to a Science Congress held in Hobart,
when he and his friend and brother-mis-
sionary, the genial Lorimer Fison, author
of “Tales of Old Fiji,” addressed one another
and —incidentally—the rest of an interested
audience, on Island Customs. I remember
Fison leaning back, appreciation in his eye
and a red handkerchief on his bald head,
shaking that old bald head dubiously over
some point. “Never mind shaking your
head,” said Brown, stopping in his discourse,
“we’ll break a lance over that afterwards.”
One of his out-of-the-way experiences was
his discovery of a new-born island, while it
was still boiling, and its re-discovery 20
years later, when the disintegrating effect of
weather, the accretions of sea-drift and the

of soil on the warm volcanic pumice. Cocoa-
nut palms, from nuts by the sea, had
sprung up, and the place was a riot of tropi-
cal growth. Fison corroborated this wonder
story, which had elsewhere been received
with some doubt, and remarked that when
he saw fellows who had never been away
from their mothers doubting the statements

“Jobber’ raises some doubts: —
M’s.P. fighting for their country ? Certainly!
There’s one running awful risks as a warrant-officer
at Horseferry-road, London; powder all round him—
pearl powder. Pay? Somewhere about 14s. a day,
plus a living allowance and Parliamentary screw.
Name, of course, censored in order to Win
the War.
Another patriot has been discovered.
Harry J. Weston, the artist, has sent his
only father, Henry, to make munitions in
England. Harry says he only wishes he
was old enough to go himself.

Though the influence of his youthful
environment was too strong for him and he
returned to a drapery counter in the end,
there was much of the spirit of A. H.
Adams’s “Grocer Greatheart” in the compo-
sition of Jimmy Levens, the Father of the
Collingwood (Yic.) Bench, who has just
surrendered to his bail at the age of 89.
At 14 he draped at Leamington, Warwick-
shire, where Louis Napoleon, two years
afterwards Emperor of the French, would
allow none other to serve him. Later
Levens, lured by the call of the diggings,
sold out and reached Australia. He was
something of a sportsman as well as a
haberdasher. On one hunting trip in the
country he met two young men who greatly
helped him in securing game. They turned
out to be Ned and Dan Kelly.
The cable mentions the death of John
Lang, in Scotland. He was the brother of
Andrew, the writer, and came to Australia
in ’75. After a failure as a Riverina pastor-
alist he j'oined the National Bank, and was
manager at Traralgon and Maffra (vie.).
Next he took the Melbourne Club secretary-
ship, but it did not suit his health. There-
after during a number of years he was a
Corowa vigneron. But that wasn’t a success
either, so he got back to Scotland. In Aus-
tralia he had been an enthusiastic member
of the Victorian Light Horse, under Tom
Price, and a keen rifle shot. When war
broke out he offered his services as instruc-
tor a dozen times before they would over-
look his age—6s. But once in, he worked
like three young men, so they made him
A.Q.M.G.
Commander L. S. Bracegirdle, D. 5.0., who
organised and took away the siege train in
May, 1915, is back in Melbourne, his useful
unit having been absorbed in other forma-
tions. This was the dependable push which
at Suvla Bay built a pier in 20 minutes
under hot fire, and on another occasion
erected a structure 420 ft. long in defiance of
a stream of Turkish shell. The siege train
did noble work for the Anzacs on the Suez
Canal, making dozens of bridges and saving
hundreds of lives. Its own casualty-list was
heavy, and it could not fire a shot in reply.
Bracegirdle was a middy in the Australian
Naval Brigade, and went to China to inter-
fere in the Boxer riots. “A kind of a giddy
harumfrodite, soldier and sailor, too,” he was
with the South African Irregular Horse in
the Boer War, and it was for his services in
the present disturbance that he gained the
D.S.O. He returned in charge of a hospital
ship.
Lieut. George Clune, who lately returned
from France, helped to hunt the Hun out
of the Pacific islands, and was invalided
home to Australia suffering from malaria.
He volunteered again for service, but was
rejected. Then he tried Canada, and, being-
turned down, went to the U.S., where he
was snapped up by the American Ambulance
Corps, which was then packing its bag for
France. He won his commission at Paris
Headquarters, and was afterwards with his
Corps at Verdun. This Chine’s uncle is
the Archbishop of Perth, senior R.C. Chap-
lain ; and there are other Clunes spotted all
over the army.
MY PLEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Listen to me for a moment, because
I’m the largest of Noises that ever was,
The prophet of bounce and the priest of bunce,
The greatest and littlest all at once!
There is never a rdle that I am not It,
Never a space that I could not fit.
To the wildest windings of Circumstance
I’m equal (with help) if I’m given a chance.
Suffragists, soldiers, flag-waggers all,
Many may trip, but I shall not fall;
Therefore I conjure you, beg and implore—
Ask what you like, only—
Give me the floor!
Surely in vain, for my sleek little head,
Spies out the net of the keen fowler spread I
Out of the thrall of the tiniest mesh
I flutter with ease, and I twitter afresh.
Sometimes Tm silent, but never for long,
For my neck is as tough as my gamut of song.
You may grab me and hold me, beak down, in the
dust,
But you can’t keep me silent; I’ll sing if I bust!
Oh, I don’t care a what the music’s about
As long as I’m sure that the sound’s coming out,
While I warble, and wobble, and warble some
more—-
Brothers, I’m proud of Me —
Give me the floor 1
Alp of prolixity’s uppermost range,
Character-artist and captain of Change
(Whether the change be in millions, or small,
Brothers, shall trouble me nothing at all!)
Peer with the parvenus, bloke with the crowd ;
Call me these things and you’re doing me proud !
Show me the penalty following fault,
I’ll show you my latest, most intricate volte;
Mention proceedings that savor of smell,
I’ll brand you as spawn of the sweepings of —well,
Somewhere in Germany. Waver no more,
Crown me prize showman, and—
Give me the floor !
Whisper it low, while these bourgeois slay
Each other with guns, in an out-of-date way,
It conies to me here that I never, before
Have stated my method of Winning-the-War 1
I evolved it in bed one dark night (I repeat
These others keep most of their brains in their feet)
And perhaps you’ll perceive, when I’m through
with my say,
That riddles are weevined, and censors passe—
But we’ll win in a canter, if only we chalk
This on our pediments, “Mighty is talk!”
And my larynx is rich, though my principle’s poor,
So allons ! To victory !
Give me the floor !!

There are shops that put as much as they
can in the window, and there arc shops that
put frost-on the glass, merely writing up their
name, and thus saying in effect to the world
what our friends the French say: “Good
wine—no sign.” And there are the two sorts
of bank, too. There are those that pile up
their figures, keeping, for instance, then-
premises in the balance-sheet at somewhere
near cost, and there are others which, fol-
lowing the example of the Bank of England,
write down premises account to nothing or
thereabouts. The Union Bank is one of
those that frost the window. For the past
half-year it publicly puts by only £484. In
effect, it says: “You know our policy. We
don’t, disclose surplus profits unless we are
sure they are profits—and we can’t be sure
of anything just now. So, if you don’t mind,
we’ll put something in the drawer for the
time being.” The chairman described it in
the way chairmen have a habit of doing:
“As regards the bank’s investments, I need
only say that the directors have made very
full provision not only for any depreciation
shown on cash prices of to-day, but even for
a further possible drop in values.”
In the circumstances, then, it isn’t much
use taking any notice of this bank’s figures
for a single half-year. Sweep in the whole
table, and you get a fair idea. But even the
table, covering seven or eight years, doesn’t
tell you that in its nearly 80 years the Union
has never missed a dividend. The manage-
ment, however, hasn’t got that fact posted up
on the back of its mind. What it prefers to
remember, even though it may not be very
fond of talking about it, is that, after the
boom of the late ’Bo’s and early ’9o’s, the
dividend got down to 5 p.c. When that
shuddersome 5 p.c. is remembered somebody
in command always snips off a bit of the
profit of a good deal and hides it away some-
where. As for the depositor, the facts that
concern him are that the bank owes
£24,457,869. Liquid and semi-liquid assets
(including the public securities which have
been written down below present market
values) total £9,443,091. That leaves
£15,014,778 of debt to stand against
£19,186,636 of advances and other assets at
their book values. And in some cases those
book values are absurdly small—as, for in-
stance, “Bank premises in Australia and
Maoriland, £378,600.” The position is, there-
fore, one on which the depositor can turn
over and comfortably go to sleep. And it is
the best thing in the world for a bank’s bor-
rowing customer when the depositor can do
that: it’s when the lender is nervous that
the borrower may look out for trouble.

Australia hasn’t caught on to Uncle Sam’s
plan of little local banks. There are only
two banks in the Commonwealth with head
offices outside a State capital. The National
of Tasmania is one; the Ballarat is the
other. And of those two the latter is the
only one to stick to the American plan of
having no branches. It is interesting to get
the more or less disinterested American’s
view of the two banking systems after he has
had a fair experience of our own. Generally
he admits that our erratic seasons would
make the path of a local bank thorny in most
parts of Australia. The advantage of an in-
stitution which spreads itself over the coun-
try is that, if it can’t get money in in one
part, it can generally get it in somewhere
else; and it can call in quite a lot in the
aggregate without producing any alarm. But
the American insists upon the tremendous
value of a local bank in booming a district;
and he insists also upon the tremendous
value of booming a district if you want to
attract population. His theory is that in
attracting population the draper shows the
way; lie doesn’t advertise in general terms
about his establishment, but hits you in the
eye with praise of one line to-day and of
another line to-morrow. In this work the
Yankee local bank is a great help, Whereas
the Australian bank, with widely-scattered
interests, is not concerned about booming
any part.
The Ballarat Bank has had bad luck in
one way—its “stand” is in a mining field
which has gradually dwindled. But there is
fine country round about; and, though the
bank doesn’t make much headway, it more
than holds its own. Deposits and all other
debts amount to only £228,103, which is very
little more than its own capital and reserves
combined. With so little of the public’s
money to work on, large profits couldn’t be
expected. The 9 p.c. dividend means less
than 4£ p.c. on capital and reserves com-
bined ; and there is certainly nothing very
alluring about that—nothing to make any-
body else want to rush off and establish local
banks in other places. The American may
stick to his local bank as a matter of princi-
ple, and thus make a success of it. It is
pretty clear that the Australian doesn’t.. And
perhaps the American wouldn’t either if he
had the choice between a local bank which
he didn’t know and a national bank with
which he had had dealings in the place that
he formerly lived in.

The profit for the past year is a bit better
than for the previous year, notwithstanding
that £72.025 is charged for taxes, as against
£35,754 in the 12 months to the end of
June, 1915. Ten years ago the total charge
was £7986. Of the past year’s £72,025 no
less than £38,889 is paid in Britain; this
includes the excess profits duty—the bit
which the nation gets back out of war
profits. Some of the old shareholders must
rub their eyes when they see these figures.
Twenty years ago it did not look as though
the co. could ever get out of the soup, and
it only did it by throwing overboard a lot
of capital and writing down foreclosed
securities to correspond. Some of what
happened subsequently the table shows;
when assets were sold they realised far in
excess of written-down values, and part
of the capital was restored. At the same
time the assets of the N.Z. Land Association,
which this co. wholly owned, were sold ; and
there have been splendid additions to profits
from that source. But. they are at an end
now: the £6353 which dropned in last year
was a final payment by the liquidator.
Practically the co. has now ceased to be
a station-owner. The next table shows how
the sales have proceeded during the last 10
years, and what has been done with the
money:—
Liabilities.

The share capital is £1,823,514. The 10
years have seen remarkable changes: over
a million and a quarter paid off the deben-
ture debt, partly, however, by conversion,
and nearly half a million added to the
liquid assets. But meantime a further
£1,249,796 has gone out in advances of one
kind and another, carrying the total up to
£3,181,119. There are risks in that busi-
ness, of course. But all the conditions have
been favoring the co. Big prices have cer-
tainly been paid for back-country and Maori-
land properties; but such big profits have
been made, too, that advances have been
pulled down hand over fist. There will be
a turn in the tide, and the last man in may
be carried out of his depth. It is, of course,
the business of a co. like this to back every-
body but this last man; how far it will
succeed only time can tell. In any case, its
financial arrangements are now such that it
can afford to nurse any securities that it
has the bad luck to have to take over; for
all its debentures are fixed, and the. only
floating debt is £586,786, against which it
has £1.037.237 of liquid assets, £376,820
advanced on wool and produce on hand and
afloat, £204,053 of merchandise and £761,394
of current account advances. Risks are
therefore small nowadays compared with
the funds at the co.’s disposal and taking
the nature of the business into account;
and that the profits are. so great must be
due largely to fine commission earnings, the
result, to a great extent, of the abnormal
prices for all kinds of pastoral produce.

There are three pairs of eyes to look at a
concern of this kind: (1) the depositor’s,
(2) the shareholder's, (3) the borrower’s.
The co. owes in all £94,352. Deduct £15,516
of liquid assets remaining after the current
dividend is provided for, and you get
£78,836 of net debt. Against this there are
advances of £166,245 (book value) and
£7062 of property and sundries. That
looks most substantial security for deposi-
tors ; and no doubt it is what it looks. There
may be a few lame ducks among the assets;
but judging by the interest earnings
(£12,723) there are not many birds in the
flock that don’t lay. The co. has been •‘going-
slow” during the last three years (advances
have decreased in that time from £172,150
to £100,245). Assuming that the manage-
ment knows its business —and it ought to
by this time —this means that, whereas old
loans have been reduced to safe limits, new
loans have been made on a very conserva-
tive valuation of securities. So the depo-
sitor need not worry; and when the
depositor doesn’t worry, the borrower isn’t
likely to be worried either, so long as he
does a fair thing. As for shareholders, they
must expect to find business slack for some
time to come; but there is no visible reason
why they should be hit through having large
quantities of depreciated securities thrown
on their hands. If they are it will be
through bad management; for there has
been plenty of time to put the house in
order. And it seems to have been done.

For many years S. Aus. had only one trustee
co.; then almost simultaneously two old-
established pastoral-agency cos', went into
the business. Elder’s had a big pull, for no
concern has a better name in the central
State than the parent Elder, Smith and Co.
At the outset, naturally, it didn’t have a
great deal of w.ork to do; but very soon pri-
vate trustees who knew they had no special
qualifications for the job transferred their
trusts, and no doubt largely in this way the
value of the estates in hand has moved up
remarkably:—■
1911 .. £53,562 I 1915 .. £1,405,146
1913 . . 525,268 | 1915 . . 2,460,347
The co. has the benefit of Elder Smith's
organisation; and that must be of great
advantage not only to the co. but to the
beneficiaries as well. On top of that the co.
has its own specialised knowledge of the
law governing trusteeship; moreover, like
other trustee cos. and unlike the private
trustee, it has put up a guarantee —of
£20,000 —that it will be a faithful steward,
and has made shareholders —a moneyed lot
—responsible for another £BO,OOO. In addi-
tion to this expert knowledge, these special
facilities and this bond to be of good beha-
vior, it has the great advantage over the indi-
vidual trustee that it is not mortal. A
testator who makes one or two individuals
his trustees generally names people who
are not much" younger than himself. Fre-
quently they predecease him, which means
expense; at other times they die soon after
he does, and that is liable to mean the
administration of his estate by people whom
he wouldn’t think of appointing. The appoint-
ment of a public co. secures a continuity of
management which may be of the utmost
importance.
It is no wonder, then, that the plan of
appointing a company trustee is making
steady headway. Generally the co. has to
wait a good while for decent returns; in
this case it is more fortunate. It already
pays 8 p.c. and puts by over 6 p.c.; and that
is a very fine result indeed in view of (1)
the very small risks in the trustee business
itself provided there is reasonably capable
management, and (2) the fact that the
whole of the share capital is in public
securities.

After Charles Anderson had carried on busi-
ness on his own account for some' years he
turned the undertaking over to a private co.
in 1903, and took shares for his goodwill.
The co. did reasonably well, and gradually
the goodwill item was written off altogether,
profits being accumulated to provide tangi-
ble assets in its place. Then new interests-
came into the business —the shares were
listed a few years ago-—and the old question
cropped up : Was it a prudent thing to have
a capital so small that, in order to absorb
the profits, the dividend would have to be- at
the rate of something well over 10 p.c.? It
was decided that it wakn’t prudent; so the
only thing to do was to form a new co., to
which the old one could sell its goodwill, for
shares; hence the doubling of the capital.
As at Dec., 1914, freeholds and plant stood
in the books at £42,390. To-day freeholds,
plant and goodwill stand at £93,344. How
much of the extra £50,954 represents new
premises and plant, how much of it is value
added to old premises and plant on revalua-
tion, and how much is goodwill this paper
doesn’t know. It is, no doubt, one of the
things that the directors don’t want the
public to know. As for other assets, etc.,
this is how the co. has fared in four years;
1912. 1914. 1916.

Following the unearthing of the 20,000,000
tons of mineral stone at Mount Tennyson,
near Yethoime, in the Bathurst district
(N.S.W.), another molybdenite field has been
located just off the Sydney-Bathurst road, a
few hundred yards behind Brooklands House
tourist resort. There is not any bulky out-
crop as seen at Mount Tennyson. Rather
there is a blanket of overburden of a few
feet of rich soil. The importance of the
find has yet to be tested. Unlike the forma-
tion on the Mount Tennyson field, the new
stuff runs in pockets, the molybdenite stand-
ing out in flakes in relief against the light-
ore background. Patches of the stuff are
rich, and on the lines of the New England
district molybdenite. One reef has been
proved at 3ft. wide, and sinking is proceed-
