# * * #
Machinery is being carted from Bathurst
(N.S.W.) to the Mt. Tennyson molybdenite
field in large quantities. If the deposit can
he proved payable, the Yetliolme district
promises to become a prosperous and popu-
lous locality, while the advent of a vigorous
mining field in the district will do much to
stimulate prospecting for other minerals.
Undoubtedly, one of the reasons why mining
has so completely died out in regions of
N. S. Wales, where at one time it was a
large and prosperous industry, is that when
the easiest won gold had been secured, pro-
spectors drifted away, few of the minerals in
high demand to-day being then worth the
looking for. Now industrial metals are in-
finitely more valuable such localities possess
no mining community to search for and
develop them, in consequence of which we
are presented with the strange anomaly of,
in many cases at least, the most promising
localities for systematic prospecting being
identical with those in which mining was
first indulged in. There are probably many
of the oldest mining districts in Australia,
abandoned except for a few ancient fos-
siekers, who have a large knowledge of the
primitive arts of the early gold days and of
little else, which offer a considerably better
field for systematic prospecting by' experi-
enced men than many newer and less easily
available regions. It is now a matter of
years since the discovery of the Mt. Tenny-
son molybdenite field; yet the new find on
Ponaldson’s property, three miles away, only
the other day, illustrates how with the pre-
sent dearth of prospecting in N. S. Wales,
valuable shows may remain undiscovered for
a substantial time even in a locality to which
public interest has been directed for a
material period. The new show, by the way,
is considered to be far superior to anything
yet discovered on that field. There is'sound
reason to hope that energetic search may
unearth many more prizes in the locality.
* * * *
“Quidam”: While much money is continu-
ally being spent in opening up unpromising
wild cats in inaccessible regions, where no
sane person -would ever want to go if he could
reasonably keep away, it seems strange that
such a deposit of valuable ore as that in
sight in the old Sunny Corner (N.S.W.)
mine should for so long have remained un-
worked. A producer of enormous wealth in
the past, worked from as far back as 1876
for, at various stages, copper, gold, silver-
lead and copper-gold, Sunny Corner long ago
established a record that will compete with
most of the giants of the past. And it has
not, like many of its compeers, been gutted
of everything that could offer a reasonable
chance of paying. expenses. The old mine
has in sight at present thousands of tons
of ore containing gold, silver, lead, zinc, cop-
per, arsenic and sulphur, the aggregate value
of which, at the present prices of metals, is
extraordinarily high. Any new proposition
which could show one face of ore similar
to those to be seen in many of the work-
ings of the Sunny Corner mine would set
the market jumping, yet it has laid dor-
mant for years, in favor of infinitely inferior
shows. Against various troubles and dis-
asters of the past, when methods of separa-
tion and refining of metals were considerably
inferior to those of the present day—and
to which long periods of magnificently pay-
able results may be set as counterpoise—
there exists the one outstanding feature that
overshadows all trivial matters of detail and
ancient history: that the various faces in
the mine at present display splendid quanti-
ties of. ore that is strikingly similar in its
composition and constituents to that of the
Broken Hill South, w T ith the exception that
it is just about thrice as rich in every com-
ponent metal. When ore, very much inferior
to the last obtained in large quantities dur-
ing the previous operations of the mine, can
be taken from the old dumps and despatched
to fai*-off smelting works, and the returns
for only two of the metals therein (i.e., silver
and lead) are sufficient to show a satisfac-
torily payable result, it may be reasonably
predicted that, when modern methods of
separation and treatment enable all the
metals to be turned into money, as is being
done with many similar ores nowadays, the
old mine will certainly take its proper place
among the principal producers of the Com-
monwealth.
Outside of the mine in question, more-
over, there is much room for prospecting
and development work in the Sunny Corner
district, which presents marked potentiali-
ties far in advance of many regions more in
the public eye. A tremendous lever towards
the development of its visible and probably
still greater undiscovered wealth would be
the advancement of the metal-smelting and
refining industry in Australia to the stage of
successfully, grappling -with complex ores, so
that the miner could get payment for each
and every metal, instead of, as at present,
being paid, with heavy deductions and
charges, for a couple, and penalised for all
the others. A continent in which an ore
containing metal values of, say, £2B per ton
is worth £8 or £9 to the producer, much of
its value going into the atmosphere or the
slag dump, can hardly pride itself on being,
metallurgically speaking, in the forefront of
civilisation. When Australia cannot only
mine, but scientifically and economically
treat, ores containing minerals which all the
world desires, it will have fewer fields like
that of Sunny Corner lying dormant and
practically deserted; while it will have a
much better chance of attracting and main-
taining enough population to find a. sufficient
local demand for its own products instead of
rooting them out and sending them abroad
as at present.

Long Tunnel, Gundagai (N.S.W.), around
which cluster memories of prospector George
Rice and his £20,000 surprise packet, has
been taken over by a company, with Geo. J.
Hepburn as manager. During the past five
years the mine has been gouged solely for
bonanzas by a syndicate, and practically
nothing has been done to open up the pro-
perty. The syndicate ignored a reef from
12ft. to 20ft. wide on which 30ft. of stoping
has proved a -value of 12dwt., and a lode at
260 ft. level from 3ft. to Bft., from which a
trial test crushing of 70 tons cleaned up for
109 oz. from the plates alone last Christmas
eve. Hepburn is putting down a big shaft
to 300 ft. and installing a battery to crush
1000 tons a month. The co. is going after
the big bodies of stone. Amongst the syndi-
cate’s plums were 3500 z. from 3001 b. of dirt;
1730 z. from three dishes; 500 oz. from 3001 b.
of stone; and 900 oz. from 2cwt.
* * * *
Chairman Wharton, of Y Water Tin
(N.S.W.), excuses last half-year’s average
of fib. of tin oxide per cub. yd. to losses of
very fine tin, but there is a hope of improv-
ing the recovery by making some adjust-
ments. The estimated yield of the latest run
is 43 tons of tin oxide; and with tin at the
present high price a profit should be made.
Last half-year 348,000 cub. yds. were sluiced,
but the management expects to increase the
yardage, for it is only by dealing with very
large quantities of dirt at a profit of 2d. or
3d. per yd. that this co. can expect to pay
dividends.
C.S.A., near Cobar (N.S.W.), produced
182 tons of copper matte in six days 19
hours recently, containing from 41 to 48
tons of copper, averaging from 22 p.c. to
26 p.c. copper per ton of matte. When the
smelter is running regularly the average
metal contents in matte should improve.
* * * *
Now that there is a probability that zinc
concentrates will have to be stored at
Broken Hill for lack of shipping tonnage,
producers are bestirring themselves and have
engaged English metallurgist Gilbert Rigg
to advise them respecting distillation works
in Australia. The B.H. Prop, gave an
object lesson, but.the plant was expensive
and costs of treatment at a decent rate of
wages became a bug-bear. The electrolytic
method isn’t a way out, simply because the
first unit timed to start next August will
produce only 10 tons of spelter per day, or
say cStiOO tons a year. There is talk of
Australia producing 45,000 tons of spelter a
year; we are pretty certain to do it, for we
have coal near the coast and the cost of
producing zinc concentrates at Broken Hill
from tailings is moderate. Climatic condi-
tions and expensive freight to Europe on
spelter are against us; but all the other
adverse conditions are created by ourselves.
Low wages and cheap coal are not essential
to the spelter industry unless we come into
competition with lower wages and cheaper
coal elsewhere.
* * * *
“On Looker”: Much hot air was expended
at the Kalgoorlie mining conference about
Westralia being one big auriferous belt, etc.
But the fact is that the Golden Mile has
practically comprised its gold industry, and
that area is rapidly becoming depleted. There
have been good mines, such as Sons of
Gwalia, Fenian, Cosmopolitan, Edna May
and others, outside of the Golden Mile, but
these are relatively few, whilst the great
majority have proved bad speculations. The
subsidising of prospectors, either by the
State or privately, is not likely to unearth
new mines. The financially assisted pro-
spector rarely finds anything for his backer.
The successful prospector is generally the
one who provides his own turn-out
and tucker and battles on his own. He will
work then, but when he is engaged by a
benefactor who pays him 30s. or 40s. per
week and half interest in discoveries, he
often takes life very quietly. Work in the
mines is now plentiful, wages good and the
pace not too fast; and, in these circum-
stances, there is not much inducement for
some prospectors to go into the bush. A
number of the right sort of prospectors are
still giving the game a trial, and, although
it is a long time since a “gold mine” was
discovered, they often make wages locating
small ore bodies and utilising public crush-
ing plants for treatment purposes. Some
good might be accomplished by the State
assisting prospectors after they have found
something attractive, but as for assisting
them at the outset —well, anyone who has
had experience of backing prospectors won’t
stand for that.
* » * *
Minister for Mines Robinson is getting a
formidable list _of Westralia’s abandoned
leases and their performances ready for
publication. He would have done so sooner
but for the delay caused in tracing the boun-
daries of some of these old holdings, many
of which have been taken up again and
again, and whose shape and size have been
altered several times. In some cases no record
lias been kept of the work done or the crush-
ing's obtained, but Robinson is making the
best of what so far has been a very hard job.
He started a publicity campaign the other
day by sending a cable to the Agent-General
about the State’s mineral resources, and
whooped over the fact that in the last 30
years production had exceeded £137,000,000,
and dividends £26,000,000. Something was
also said about the great shortage of skilled
miners in the West and the formulation of
schemes for the absorption of our own re-
turned soldiers and those of allied nations.
He wound up with the assertion that the
State contained the largest tract of unpro-
spected mineral country in the world, which
is probably true. Later on, Robinson pro-
poses to employ press agents to deluge and
dazzle the world with statistics and informa-
tion respecting Westralian mining, and he is
also considering the advisableness of offering-
special cash prizes each year for the best
gold, base metal and precious stone dis-
coveries, and for the best invention calcu-
lated to benefit the treatment of refractory
ores.
Edna May's ore reserves on October 29
last were 35,951 tons, of which 13,526 tons
have since been crushed, leaving 22,425 tons
in reserve above 300 ft. The 385 ft. level,
three parts opened up, has yielded 1413 tons.
The bulls say the 22,425 tons will go 80s.
Surely Williams doesn’t hint otherwise when
he. wires “will have no difficulty in main-
taining tonnage, but grade must necessarily
be dependent on the average quality of ore
to be opened up at bottom level.” Does
this mean that the rest of the ore has gone
from above 300 ft.? Williams really nails
that deduction hard down when adding that
“it can only.be anticipated that this (the
quality of ere from the bottom level) will
be lower than from shallower levels.” Even
so, the mine isn’t gutted. Over 22,000 tons
have come from each level, and from the
300 ft. to the Deeps boundary at 476 ft. gives
176 ft. of backs. If the shoot hasn’t
meandered into the Central there ought to
be 40,000 tons of ore to get out at that
depth. Costs shouldn’t get much above 255.,
so that if the grade falls to 15dwt. the co.
should have £105,000 profit in sight, plus
that from the dumps. The stqne crushed by
Deeps is a fair sampling of the shoot at
480 ft., and it has gone 17dwt. The doubtful
factors for Edna May are the length of the
shoot and its general average. It is up to
Tricks to find out.
* * * *
In his Tasman and Crown Lyell Extended.
Queenstown (Tas.), report J. Nicholson
reports progress from the time he took
charge. First he declares that his air blast
treatment does the trick. Then, “after due
consideration and by experiments,” he finds
it would be cheaper and more economical
to treat the heavier galena, mentioned in
his patent, first by wet concentration, and
treat the lighter afterwards by the dry and
air pressure. So he slimes the ore, gets the
heavy mineral off on Wilfley’s, then classifies
the residues and dries them, by which time
they are fit to be introduced to the air blast.
This looks like going back to the old days,
but Nicholson considers it’s right or he
wouldn’t pen this—
With the 100,000 tons of galena ore proved in
sight, and part of the crude ore which is valuable
enough to bag without treatment, and the valuable
copper lode, which increases the value of the mine,
and gives us returns straight away, this all tends
to prove to those who have invested that all future
operations are assured.
Yet, of the copper lode he says that if it
proves 10 p.c. value it will be “the most
valuable lode in the mine, taking into con-
sideration the price of copper.” On the
result of the assay depends the future opera-
tions of this lode. Nicholson says “the
treatment only requires concentration and
selling of concentrates to the smelters which
are at Queenstown.” Just so.

Pioneer (Tas.) is paying a Is. dividend,
following a similar distribution in March.
Last half-year the output of black tin was
271 tons, and if the Pioneer gets
plenty of water an output of 10 tons of
tin oxide per month may be maintained for
a time. The official figures show" that re-
serves equal to last half-year’s average of
1.231 b. per yaM are dwindling, and the gay
talk about a nve years’ life is based on an
immense quantity of lo>v-grade wash dirt.
* * * *
A single dredge producing from 30 to 40
tons of tin oxide per month cannot be ex-
pected to pay frequent dividends on
1 00,000 shares, That is the position
o/ Katrut Basin .Tin, Siam. The first Is.
(ioOOO) proved a stick to hammer the
market with. Tin dredging is very profitable
under existing conditions; and though a
yield of 120 tons of tin oxide every three
months isn’t impossible, it is improbable
that Ratrut should clear an average net
profit of £4O per ton of tin oxide for the
next five years, and it requires to do rather
better than that to return 20s. per share
* * *

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, May 7, 1917.
My Dear Arini.—
Papua’s tall Governor J. H. P. Murray,
passed this way en route to Melbourne from
Port Moresby. Mosquitoes were in full
blast, free orchestral accompaniments day
and night, when the popular Excellency said
good-bye to his territory. A wife and only
daughter are waiting on the other side of
the submarine menace. But no news comes
as to whether son Terence found time to
leave the Great Fight to attend his own
wedding with Miss Philippa Kitchener. She’s
a pet niece of the late AVar Lord.
Hospital Friday, with the coat-tails of
every man flying before the whirlwind of
female collectors, brought a good harvest,
considering the times and the taxes. Pro-
bably there’ll be nearly £7OOO in the bag.
At a big meeting last week in the Town
Hall (Deputy-Governor Cullen in the
chair) it was decided that June 1 should
be Red Triangle Day. The Y.M.C.A. wants
£50,000 from N. S. Wales for the benefit of
the Kangaroo at the Front.
You remember Dr. Brissenden, a well-
known Sydney barrister, who enlisted as a
private in order to hurry to the Front?
From France he writes in feeling terms of
the recent frost-bitten winter. “Thermome-
ters ranged from nine below freezing point
in mid-day, to 30 below at night—the
coldest winter here since 1890. Everything
was frozen hard as a stone. The water in
one’s basin was always solid in the morn-
ing, and thawing one’s fingers after dressing
was a painful process.” Now, how about
that 100.000 pair of socks the AVar Chest
wants this month? Our new Ally, U.S.,
reckons that the war is likely to be a longer
one than many people anticipate. Socks
sent from Australia in June will be ready
for Billjim’s chilled toes when autumn
leaves are falling in France.
J. 11. Cann, president of the National Bel-
gian Relief Fund, and the joint lion.,
secs, announce the close of the fund on
May 15. Cash in hand will then be re-
mitted to Agent-General Wade, to be used
for the purpose for which it was raised. And
that is likely to keep the London office busy.
The Frank Davenports have lost their
younger son, Guy, killed in action on the
AVestern Front. The handsome mother is a
persistent worker for the children of mean
streets, and is one of the founders of the
Free Kindergarten schools.
About the only collection day which bad
an aesthetic odor upon its hours was Violet
Day in August, 1915. No frenzied citizens
were pursued by Amaryllis; instead,
levies of quiet girls sold the exquisite
blooms at ordinary market rates, and
£lBOO was the return. This sum went
towards the fund that enabled Dr. Mary
Booth to open the Soldiers’ Club. She’s
been its fond Mother Hubbard ever since;
but the cupboard,is getting bare. Unless the
recent State edict, “No street collections for
war funds,” is repealed (the Y.M.C.A. has
jumped the Billjim’s home in
George-street may have to shut up. Another
Violet Day couldn’t be an affliction since the
rattling cashbox nuisance would be absent.
A slim, briglit-eyed young woman, of pre-
possessing appearance, carrying 1001 b.
weight of sausages along a main street (with
juicy samples breaking through the wrap-
pings) doesn’t cause a riot in well-fed Syd-
ney. So Mabel Grant Cooper, loaded
as described, passed down George-street
to the Soldiers’ Club (where she’s
the youthful missus of Saturday feasts
for Bill J.) ; and nobody called the attention
of the traffic police. When the mysteries
frizzled in the pan the boys fell to, while
Dorothy Hopkins fiddled melodiously and
Miss Laing and other pretty damsels gener-
ously piled the plates.
Melba passed through this town on her
way to Coombe Cottage at Lilydale. After
leaving the ’Murkan boat the singer was
surrounded by a regiment of reporters, and
gave glad tidings of Much Money raised by
herself in the States. For the Allies’ stock-
ing, of course. Now, Australia hopes to get
another bucketful of gold from the same
course.
The A’eterans’ Home at Bare Island —the
bleak rock that used to be fortified in the
days of short-range guns—is in lean circum-
stances. Private generosity has kept a roof
over the head of the old soldiers w T ho fought
in Britain’s wars long ago; now Melba
comes to the rescue. Our sovereign voice is
going to entice a fat cheque to the very
Bare Island. Madame has seen the munici-
pal “heads” about the matter, but whether
the Town Hall rent is likely to come down
as a result deponent knoweth not. Mean-
while, John Lemmone pulls off his coat to
do the necessary bullocking in organising
the show.
The Sydney Phil. Society will offer the
“Damnation of Faust” (a fine fire-eating
Falstaffian label) by Berlioz, in the Town
Hall this week.
Maitland wore its best frocks for the wed-
ding of John Pearson Eckford, of Ballata
station. The charming lady in the case was
Margaret Netta, eldest hope of the R. F.
Grahams, of Bellevue House, Largs, West
Maitland. Oyster-tinted shimmer with
orange blossoms and tulle completed the
bridal finery. Bridesmaid Marion Graham,
in cream, smiled under cherry-colored millin-
ery, with brown furs to warm it up. Gunner
Logan Graham was second best-man. Honey-
moon at Abbotsleigh, Queensland, the
station owned by Graham Bros.
Tamworth did itself proud on Anzac Day.
Eighteen maidens mounted on prancing
steeds each led a horse with an empty saddle
in the procession. These Australian Joans
were turned out in smart dark habit-skirts,
white coats and cadet hats. Each spare
saddle bore the invitation, “Your comrades
who were able to go earlier want your help.
Be a sport—enlist now!” Eighteen recruits
leapt into the vacancies. Irene Sexty, presi-
dent of the local girls’ association, organ-
ised the parade.
Leura Links committee has a virile rival
in the rolling uplands where they chase the
small white ball at AVentworth Falls. Both
clubs are out to make money for war funds,
with the help of professional goffers. Re-
cently Charles Campbell (Leura), Carnegie
Clark (Royal Sydney Golf Club), Fred
Popplewell (Australian Golf Club) and D.
G. Soutar (Manly) tossed their fees into
the hat at the close of exhibition matches
played at the Falls on April 14 and Leura
on April 15. In consequence £54 goes to
the War Chest..
A Croix de Guerre came into the Arthur
family lately by way of Colonel Fraser
Clark, of the R.A.M.C., who . married a
daughter of Mrs. Richard Arthur. Fellow
sharers of the French decoration are Field-
Marshall Haig and knight William Robert-
son (on whom the Kitchener mantle is
reported to have fallen), who has experi-
ences all the way up from the ranks of
Britain’s army,
Gum this notice to your vanity-box. Kate
Rooney will warble in the Town Hall on
May 18. Proceeds go to the Home of the
Good Shepherd at Ashfield.
This laughing, boy-
ish face belongs to
Midshipman Donald
Gyles, one of the.
heroes of the de-
stroyer Broke. Three
years ago this photo
was taken here when
the lad was a junior
cadet on the train-
ing-ship Mersey. At
the outbreak of war
the pink-cheeked
kiddie was trans-
ferred to the navy.
When, a week or two
ago, he was wounded
in the face while in
charge of the Broke’s
fo’castle during the
Channel fight, he was
temporarily stunned, but picked himself up,
wiped away the blood with his coat-sleeve
and rushed to his gun. And when some burly
Germans got aboard the Broke the young-
ster, with the help of a couple of gunners, had
a desperate hand-to-hand fight and, eventu-
ally, savs the boy’s simple narrative, “we
cleared the decks of the enemy, killing those
who intercepted us, and made the rest
prisoners.”
There was a huge recruiting rally at the
Stadium the other evening, with R. L. Baker
in charge. The Sportsmen’s Unit shook up
the applause with a bayonet display that
made the more timorous feminine feel
creepy. It was so deadly earnest that the
glittering steel looked wicked enough to
scare an enemy battalion. Recruiting
speeches by Mayor Richard Meagher, Ser-
geant-Major Bohm (a live-wire returned
soldier), Lieuts. Seaborn and sportsman
Diamond in khaki, unseated some of the up-
to-then barnacles. They cam# with a rush,
72 of them, from the back seats, and handed
in their names for the war register. The
Austral Quartet sang in refrain, “I Hear
Their Gentle Alices Calling.” Then the
recruiting-sergeant very appropriately got
busy.
This keen-looking soldier, who sat to
Judith Fletcher for his photo,, is Major
Frank Hewitt, M.C. Enlisting as a private
in October, 1914, he fought his way to a
commission in the immortal struggle at Lone
Pine, and was mentioned in despatches. Then
came the evacuation, and lie was amongst
the heroic band who melted away while
Johnnv Turk was pounding at the
old address. From the Dardanelles Frank
Hewitt went to France, where he was
wounded and awarded the Military Cross, in
1916, and returned to Australia with the
Major’s crown on his shoulder-strap. He
was then placed on the reserve list perma-
nently unfit for active service. Now he is mod-
estly surprised that anyone should mention
his little performance at the Front. Pre-
sently he returns to his former civilian job
in the Solomon Islands.
Very few people know that there’s a mag-
nicent display of Antarctic photos, in the
Education Building’s galleries. One
stumbled on it by chance, looking for some-
thing else. The other half of the admirers
present was a young man, who had evidently
stalked the lonely exhibition by similar acci-
dent. The pictures, by the Sydney man,
P. J. F. Hurley, are vivid reminiscences of
the Mawson Expedition of 1911-14. Douglas
Mawson got a knighthood out of those chilled
months in the frozen south, and our own
Prof. David got fat on blubber, seal steak
and candle grease entries. Don’t miss the
photos., which give domestic habits of lady
sea elephants and grandpa penguins, in
detail.
Australia is entitled to feel proud of Alice
Cashin, matron of the recently torpedoed
hospital ship, Gloucester Castle. She is a
clever Sydney nurse, and had a reputation
in her profession even before the war started.
She controlled the General Hospital in
Calais at the beginning of Armageddon ; and
later on went to Egypt, where her work
earned her the highest decoration a woman
can obtain —the Royal Red Cross, first-class.
There died at Coogee a few days ago, Mrs.
Ena Maurice, a pathetic little figure to
whom life seemed to always grudged a fair
deal. She was the eldest daughter of the
late Thos. Finney, Brisbane’s biggest mer-
chant in the ’Bo’s, and early learned to wield
a facile pen. Her booklet, “A ’Possum
Abroad,” was a remarkably amusing and
readable account of an Australian on the
wing through Europe. “’Possum” (Ena’s
penname) was then chief contributor for
Bobbie Burns, on Brisbane Figaro. Later
Mrs. Maurice wrote some delightful matter
on Japan. Helping lame dogs over stiles
was a hobby of hers, and few persons outside
her very intimate circle knew how numerous
was that limping pack.
The big body of Maorilanders who were
here last week look tough customers
for the arrogant Prussian to be introduced
to. These broad-chested sons of the fern
glades have saucy millinery with a red strip
of felting round their high-crowned khaki
hats. The crowns are not as high as Mt.
Cook, but the ambitions and courage of the
wearers are.
The Old Gold and Silver Fund clamors for
your old-fashioned trinkets. Even your cast-
off teeth, with or without a stuffing of gold,-
are heartily welcomed in the committee’s
curiosity shop, 62 Hunter-street. D. Went-
worth and J. S. Richardson are hon. secs, of
this good excuse to melt down relics and
help the patriotic plate to overflow.
A favorite singer whose honeyed temper
makes her the prey of bores, remarks, in
parenthesis, that “New York artists are
carefully tended ere they appear before
audiences. At the Flail, for in-
stance, there are beautifully-fitted bath,,
dressing and lounge rooms, and a maid m
attendance. Thus, musicians having busi-
ness in the city all day bring in their suit-
cases in the morning. Without the tuss or
returning to some possibly distant home, they
can have a hot bath, dress and come on
feeling fresh and at their best. Sydney
Town Hall, on the other hand, lias a dugout,
accessible to any stranger who chooses to
wander in and torment artists, who are at
the mercy of many dreaded bores. The same
thing applies to every concert hall in the
city. At the other side of the world, at the
Roval Albert Hall, for instance, a martinet
guards the artists’ room. No one can gain
admission without sending in their name
and getting permission to enter from the
performer they wish to see. Even this con-
cession is only granted after the first half
of the programme.”
The craze for beastie skins makes this
winter’s trimmings all heads and tails. Tittle
animals, carefully stuffed, sit on the hats of
our Best People, and imitation cub tigers
snuggle round the necks of our affluent young
matrons. Yet there’s only eight or 10
weeks of winter in this sunny clime. 1 sup-
pose that somebody will be sure to econo-
mise .soon and help win the beastly war.
Artist Lister Lister’s annual show in
Anthony Horderns’ Art Gallery is remini-
scent of South Coast hills and dales, giant
-urns anywhere, and sandy beaches beyond
Narrabeen. Lister’s pet bird, the sea-gull,
is at the old stand on the creamy sand
preening its feathers. The Bega district,
where the fat cheeses come from,, is well
represented, and sun and shadow glints from
Burragorang valley. .
A grocery afternoon at Graythwaite,
Thomas Dibbs’s generous gift to convalescent
soldiers, brought along a ton or two ot
produce. The Red Cross, by the. way, has
had to date practically no exes, in connec-
tion with running this roomy mansion. The
Cullen lady opened the tea party.
Artist Gladys Owen has folded her easel
into a corner while she warms the chilly fringe
of recruiting meetings. Her latest effort has
the “Anti-Shouting” text, which she’s work-
ing along the South Coast. This isn’t a
dodge to try to keep the Kangaroo on cold
tea. It’s really a plea to save sick men—
returned soldiers in country, convalescent
homes, particularly—-from unthinking friends.
These invalids are not physically fit to
dabble in the flowing bowl, but it’s often
forced on them with the best intentions.
Then there’s trouble for the V.A.D.
The Fruit and Vegetable Fund (hon.
organiser R. M. Pontey) is now officially
registered with the State War Council. Its
premises, 16 Carrington-street, Wynyard
Square, are provided rent free by the Govern-
ment. This winter 1100 soldiers’ wives and
4000 kiddies will make their pilgrimage,
bag in hand, to collect the goods. Every-
thing helps. Coffee, cocoa, tea might be
added to the praties and pumpkins you send
to these game women and youngsters.
As Britain draws in its waist-belt and cuts
down its rations, overlapping Australian war
funds might take the hint and go steady .with
supplies. An Australian girl who married a
naval officer a few years ago writes: —
We are not move hungry than we were, for our
household cut down its bread and meat when the
first appeal came from the Official Pantryman. Hun-
dreds of other families did the same thing; but,
unfortunately, thousands in London squandered money
on lavish meals in fashionable restaurants. So the
cheeseparing is now an edict.
The generous Australian is apt to give too
much food when there’s a feast for Billjim
on hand. How about organising the amount
of sustenance each patriot brings?
Mrs. Gilbert Lodge is settling down here
again after a long visit to dear, little old-
fashioned Tassy. She is. the handsome lady,
you remember, whose trim tailor-built figure
tops most dressy functions by an inch or
two.
One of the late John Lee’s tall daughters
(father was the squire of Kelso, up Ballarat
way) is in mourning for her husband, Lieut.-
Col. Mclntosh, who was fatally wounded in
the recent fighting in Egypt.
H. N. Southwell got to Spain, after all.
When the Fog was sitting heavily on his
chest he fled to Madrid, then overland to
Paris. Here he struck an Arctic tempera-
ture. Huge blocks of ice were floating down
the Seine (this was in February), and the
bridges were covered with snowy whiskers.
“The cost of living in London is enormous,”
sighs Paling’s old concert manager, “and I
was glad to get out of it for a few weeks. It
has been a brutal winter on this side of the
map.”
The formal “farthing damages” verdict
does not often appear in the Divorce Court,
but a jury handed out that amount to a
petitioner who was granted a divorce the
other day. This estimate of the value placed
upon her loss must be very pleasing to the
lady !
Yours affectionately,
VANDOEIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, Mat 7, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie. —
The Stanley dame has had her name figur-
ing in large print at the foot of a special
appeal for the British Red Cross. One of
the money baits will be a raffle for a linen
chest containing £lOO worth of house napery.
Prospective brides are rushing the shilling
lottery.
The British Red Cross offensive has begun
and everybody is expected to help. Baldwin
Spencer is general, with Desboro Annear as
chief-of-staff. Manson, of Maoriland, and
Mrs. McCubbin are helping. Ye Olde Eng-
lish Fayre will happen in Melbourne Town
Hall on the first three days and nights of
June. Annear is designing and modelling
delightful houses, ranging from the period
of James I. to those of Queen Elizabeth and
dull old Anne, whose limb, if you
remember, was pulled bv the Marl-
boroughs. There will be 1G houses
in all.. The Moore timber people have offered
the wood, the fibrous cement people the
stony slabs that will be painted by local
artists. Mrs. McCubbin has begun her col-
lecting with a £4O cheque from some modest
donor. On the Saturday before the event
a party of schoolgirls will ride around dressed
as cavaliers.
Captain Lansell, son of the rich Bendigo
family, is coming wearily home. He was
blinded in battle and suffered a very serious
nervous collapse. Fortunately there is some
hope of him recovering his eyesight.
Poor young St. Leger, the pianist, came
home as a cot case and is now in Caulfield
Military Hospital. A brother of pianist
Hal Elvins arrived on the same boat, also
listed among the seriously ill.
Looking admiringly at the French colonial
piou-pious who passed through the other day
on their way to a brief spell in New Cale-
donia, Melbourne remembered the fighting
Crivellis, sons of the courtly and portly
Albert Park doctor. Louis, the eldest, went
to Paris in 1913 to become an M.D., and
was doing his military service in the French
army when the Kaiser broke loose. He has
been auxiliary surgeon with the 44th Regi-
ment of Field Artillery since August 4, 1914,
rising lately to the rank of surgeon-aide-
major. His record includes a Mention and
the Croix de Guerre, and he has been at
Mons, the Marne, the Aisne and Verdun.
Georges M„ the second boy, has risen from
non-com. to first lieutenant with the 44th
Artillery, and is now an observer in the
Flying Corps, with a Croix de Guerre and
service at Mons, the Marne, the Aisne, Roye,
Albert, the Champagne and Verdun to his
credit. Then there is Rene G., who
severely wounded while serving a “75” in
the Champagne in 1915. He took a spell in
hospital (Croix de Guerre for him too) and
is now in the first line trenches at Verdun.
McGill University, Montreal, Canada,
where humorist Stephen _ Leacock professes
political economy, is beginning to seem to
Victorians like an extension of their own
Alma Mater. It was there that Major
Norman Harris, M.C., of the Victorian
Engineers, took his degree as Master of
Science before marrying plump Rita Moss
and going away on active service. Now Dr.
Arthur Stanley Lamb, who began life as a
Ballarat carpenter, but told his parents he
could do better than make shavings, _ has
taken his degrees at McGill. He finished
head of the class list, and his kiddie, born
about the same time, was made “Class Baby”
and given a loving cup. “Dad” Lamb, as
they call him, is in the Canadian Military
Forces, and is anxious to get to France with
the Canuck Army Medical Corps. Fie won
the 50 yards swimming championship of
British Columbia twice while doing his
course.
From London —
Edith Lyttleton (“G. B. Lancaster”), in England
these several years, has issued an appeal on behalf
of the Anzac Buffet in London, where the Austra-
lian-on-furlough is offered a glad hand, with a
coffee and sandwich, from early morn until late at
night. The Buffet is threatened with extinction
through lack of funds, since it receives no cash
either from Headquarters or the War Chest, and
late advances in the cost of supplies of all kinds
have had a most depressing effect on the balance-
sheet. Running-costs reach about £3OOO a year, aU
provided from private sources. The address is
Anzac Club and Buffet, 94 Victoria-street, West-
minster.
Medico Kent Hughes has set sail for the
Front to visit son Billy. His large abode
in Toorak has been engaged as a high-class
hash fortress.
Mrs. Darrell, the smart Anglo-Indian grass-
widow, recently received a cable reporting
the illness of her husband, Captain Darrell,
in Mesopotamia. She has gone to the
mountains for a few weeks.
Lieutenant “Ronny” Larking, an old Mel-
bourne Grammar boy, bar; just got his M.A.
at Cambridge. He went to the Front, serv-
ing with the Canadians, was attached later
to the Royal Engineers, and was decorated
with the Military Cross last year.
No more Savoy dinners or suppers until
after the war! The last of those lively even-
ings was dated on Monday of last week, and
the haunt of the win-the-war feeder is now
a “tea room.” (No extra charge for sealskin
coats.) Breakfast is off; lunch and a cock-
tail may still be had; but tea is to be the
specialty. Manager Burgess broke his
patrons in to the strange habit at a special
invitation pekoe party on Monday. The
tables were adorned with pink, white and
black stripes, the teapots posed on black
mats with pink roses worked in. Real pink
roses pink shades and curtains betrayed the
penury of these unhappy times.
General Stanley in his red-trimmed khaki,
occupied a centre table with the usual batch
of smart friends. Mrs. Murray Morton had
a party which she adorned with fascinating
feathers in her black hat. Mrs. Barry
Thompson sported a Belgian (where
have I heard that name before?) bonnet
and wore a mass of fur skins. Mrs.
Pohlson, in sparrow-brown, had a big
party. The Emmertons were to the
fore, likewise the MacDonalds. Singers
mounted the rose kiosk at the far end of
the room and performed to the music of a
string orchestra without interrupting the
harmony.
The Andrew Chirnsides, of Berwick, gave
a fete to help build a new vicarage for the
local Anglican church, but well-wishers
when they drove up to the gate full of kindly
enthusiasm found that only the lady of the
house was permitted to perch her stall in
the garden. The others were kept off the
grass, so to speak. Mrs. Chirnside eventu-
ally established herself and her lolly stall
under a spreading foreign tree on her
reserved house lawn. She was bedecked in
garden-party clothes, with a pink feathered
hat and ropes of pearls. The other stallites
made the best of things in the stable road,
.while the £2OO Hag that Chirnside had bought
at a patriotic concert waved in the breeze
at the second-best gate. The first-best gate
was decorated by an elderly piper. The
squattocracy has a lot to learn concerning
the etiquette due to democracy in the private
field of Christian endeavol*.
Vera Parkes and her mother are showing
Mrs. Cass over Australia. Just now they
are at Melbourne, and they will go north
later to their native Bananaland. Colonel
Cass married a Canadian girl.
W. H. Felstead, of The Lane (the late
David Beath, of pious memory, took him
for partner), passed out suddenly last week.
He was an ideal softgoods trader, suave,
smiling and always exquisitely groomed.
Collectors for natrotic funds and charities
found him a splendid mark, and a glad smile
always lighted up the cheque.
The widow Ross Soden gave a dinner-
party to farewell youngest son Alf, back to
the Riverina, where he looks after the
family property. The food was spread at
the Toorak home, and finished up with a
whirl in the ballroom. Major MeFar-
lane attended, also Captain Ryan and
Dr. Barry Thompson. Mrs. Barry T.
wore blue, and Mrs. Millar was beautifully-
arrayed in black, like Mrs. Langlands
Thompson. Doris McKinnon hid the most
of herself in white, and the Mcßeans,
Falkiners and Fred Knights appeared in full
uniform. Batchelor Lawson, who looks
after the rolls, and Captain Andrew, a
plague-germ expert from China, were other
guests.
Adelaide rings up :
The early rains did wonderful things for the gar-
dens, to which the exhibits of the crysanthemum show
duly testified. The affair was a credit to the three
years’ old National Chrysanthemum Society, and its
president, John Lewis. The Galway lady attended to
the opening and congratulated everybody. The
proceeds go to the Y.M.C.A. Army Fund.
The Elder Conservatorium’s new violin teacher,
Gerald Walenn, is due here at the end of this month
or early next. In the seven years that he was at
the Royal Academy of Music he is said to have got
away with every possible prize, and he is credited
with successful tours in the United States and
Canada. He is to fill the place left vacant by big-
hearted Eugene Alderman.
Lieutenant H. Throssell, who won his Y.C. at
Gallipoli and got a tremendous welcome from his old
school, Prince Alfred «College, when he was here
on leave, is reported to have been badly wounded
in Egypt. His brother was killed in the same engage-
ment. And more bad news. B. S. Roach has lost
his son, Kelvin, and Warren Edwards, son of J. R.
Edwards, of Broken Hill, has been killed in action.
Miss Herring’s vineyard at the Sturt has en-
couraged other S.A. women. For some time the
old Leawood Gardens on the Mount Lofty-road have
been in feminine hands, and the present owners,
Miss Laughton and Miss De Mole, are advertising
for others of their sex “willing to work on the land.”
It is a healthier life than typing or clerking, and
it couldn’t pay worse than governessing or teach-
ing.
The Tom Barr Smiths, who fitted in a trip to
Melbourne during their move from Mount Lofty
back to town for the winter, have unpacked at
Birksgate.
Lieutenant W. L. Davies, who was school-teaching
when he enlisted and is an old P.A.C. boy, has won
the Military Cross after a brief two months at the
Front Two of his brothers have been killed, but
fyounger one is still in the line. Dr Stewart ha
had news that his son, who was killed some tune
ago, was mentioned in Doughs Haig s despatches o
November 13 for “distinguished and gallant servi e
and devotion to duty.” , ,
The South Australian Soldiers Fund adde
£24,470 (of which £10,236 was interest) to its
total during the six months ending on March 31,
and reckons its present rate of expenditure at £oo
per week. If the men still to return are to be
properly treated, the public will have to be more
generous. ,
FI. G. Garland, once of the Register s literary
staff’, to whom the war has brought a D.C.M. and a
second lieutenancy, is on his way back to Australia
on leave. . , , ~
The Goodman-Butler wedding happened at the
Walkerville church at the end of the week. The
lieutenant-bridegroom is the Goodmans’ eldest son,
and the bride is one of the “Dick” Butler daughters.
The two blue-frocked bridesmaids were Miss K.
Butler and Miss Dorothy Goodman.
Miss E. I. Benham, who lectured in botany at
the University in the interim between Professor
Tate’s death and Professor Osborn’s appointment,
died at the'end of the week. She was well known
as a teacher in the leading girls’ schools here, and
for some years had been principal of Walford School,
Malvern. A good deal of her spare time went to
the University Women Students’ Society, of which
she was for some time president.
Died at Woodville on Monday, R. D. Hanson,
eldest son of Judge Hanson, who was this States
Chief Justice many years ago. His one son is in
France with a machine-gun section.
We shook the moth-balls out of our rarely-used
evening rags for the amateur “As You Like It” show
at the Queen’s Hall, on Friday. The vice-regals
sat in the front of the dress circle, and as
every other seat was filled at 65., the French Red
Cross gets a handy cheque.
For feminine momentary fancy there is a
brand-new color on the market. It is called
Vert de boteille, which, being translated,
means green-of-a-dead-marine. Men may
later have a touch of the fashionable shade
in ties socks, hats and late hours.
Dorothy and Muriel Dangar, daughters of
the late H. C. Dungar, have left for their
country home. Their sister, Mrs. 11. Osborne,
will keep house at Grantham with her chil-
dren while they are away. A recent cable
brought the news that Major Clive Dangar
(husband of elder sister Nora) has been
wounded in Egypt.
A travelling correspondent tells me that
little Irma Caron added another success to
her Red Cross efforts during the sea trip
from Melbourne to Java. M. P. Adams
arranged a concert, at which Irma sang “One
Fine Day” and various comic opera numbers.
George Chant also sang and Elma MacLeod
stroked the piano.
The Austral Salon had a party to wel-
come singer Maggie McCann, the Scotch-
Australian girl who is now Mrs. Colquhoun
Thompson. She has spent most of her
married life in England and America, but
her Glaskie accent has survived it. all. She
sat with very pink cheeks and a bright smile
. in the midst of dark furs and under a broad-
leaf hat. Sister Mrs. Maloney showed her
off. Mrs. Quinnell, garbed in dove-grey,
was in the throng, and the Best People
rolled up in well-furred crowds to pay their
respect. A very clever little pianist played
some tremendous Liszt music, and Fred
Collier and wife Elsie Treweek sang.
Maggie has been singing for patriotic funds
in England, where husband Colquhoun
Thompson has an expert’s job in the Uni-
versity of Munitions. On Thursday the
Salon welcomes Alice Rees.
The Reps. Club matin6e at the Playhouse
was a frost. It was arranged to help the
Australian Comforts Fund and to endow cots
at the Base Hospital, and every available
star had agreed to shine in the good cause.
Yet the show went cold. The vice-regals
had their seats booked and the committee
had every helper named and thanked in print
on the back of the programmes,. but the
social landsturm didn’t follow their leaders
and there were big, cold gaps in the stalls
and hollow, dark caverns in-the dress-circle,
while the boxes looked like tombs. Lady
Stanley stepped out of her motor in a froth
of black net flounces and a big smoke-blue
overcoat. She wore a Gainsboro’ hat, and
Genthelp Conant strode after her and a little
daughter of ex-Governor. Strickland. John
Madden came along, with his white hair
nicely brushed, and his dame had Hermes
wings in her hat. The Hennessy dame was
clothed in warm autumn tints. Mrs. Geoff.
Syme arrived in a big velvet hat, with her
pretty youngsters and huge baskets of roses.
Mrs. Carse wore the smallest hat of the
season, according to telescope, and Mrs-
Fred Knight was hardly noticeable within
ermine.
The show started very late. An anxious
man stepped out and read lists of regi eis tor
absence from several performers, including
Barry Lupino and Dorothy Brunton. Connie
Ediss and other absentees had nothing to say
for themselves. The entertainment opened
by a Tivoli banjoist sticking his dusty every-
day boots well over the footlights and clang-
ing out a tune or its equivalent.. Then
Marie Eaton stamped on with, her
coal-black eyes flashing danger signals.
She had been pushed on out of her turn
wearing a fine black Napoleon hat and a
white cloth suit: With the help of others
the show proceeded by spasms and encores
till the sun went down, on everybody’s
wrath.
Marie Tempest was somewhat subdued as
“Penelope” on Saturday night. She was so
ill with a cold on Friday that the show
was shut down. The management says that
it is only the second time that this has
happened in 19 years. This was a bad week
for the stars. Barry Lupino was out of
the bill at King’s, and Dorothy Brunton
was away ill from Her Majesty’s.
“Penelope” came on stage blowing her
nose and yet looking IS. Her frock was
of silk the shade of a grey sea, with grey
chiffon hems, and when she greeted a
play-motlier clad in acres of crinolined
puce silk glace a striking color harmony
was established. Add to that cushions
of hunter’s pink on the chintz sofa —and
there’s a picture for you. Penelope runs up
big stage bills; goes off to the races in
a £l5O gown of cream lace with a gold and
black sash, and a hat that looked like a pie-
dish gone mad. A projected trip to Paris
costs hubby six new Bond-street hats, which
fascinating things are all brought on stage in
ornate bonnet-boxes by two milliner girls.
Then Penelope prepares for her motor-trip in
bouganvillia purple cloth and a saucy travel-
ling hat, and dresses for the big scene in
pink over amber silk with streaks of gold.
The house was packed. Genthelp. Conant
partied with some gay young folk. TTie
Falkiners boxed and the Fred Knights were
stalled. Dr. Atkinson was there with his
wife, and Mrs. Monash, with a bright green
tulle lining to her cloak, headed a big party.
A smart young matron wore so huge a
Spanish comb that it blocked a square yard
of the stage. Mrs. Tallis, in a box. was
clothed in severe white samite, built like a
nightgown. Bachelor Dr. Gould sat in the
D.C. centre with a party of pretty girls.
Mrs. Parkes and her daughter (Banana-
land) were close to the footlights.
Yours sincerely.
JOHANNA.

“Norseman”: During Sydney Show week
I got talking to a John Hop, and mentioned I
came from Booligal. “Do you know a chap named
Smith ?” he asked. “Yes, I'm Smith,” I answered, tak-
ing off my collar and showing- him my name. In less
than a minute there were 150 damfools crowded
round and more coining along- everywhere. Things
must be dull in Sydney. Booligal don’t get excited
over little things like that.
“The Old Soldier” : I had a good deal of
experience with gun and baggage elephants, and I
don’t think much of them. The greatest load an
Indian jumbo was ever expected to carry on bis
back was 9 to 10 cwt. In draught, two elephants
would pull a 24-pounder S.B. gun, weighing some 41
tons, on the road ; but they would not be allowed to
take it under fire, because at the first shot they
would wheel and bolt to the rear, running- over
anything that got in their way. So, before coming-
near an enemy, elephants were always unhooked,
and a team of bullocks yoked up ip their stead.
A team of eight horses or 16 bullocks easily man-
aged our 24-pr.
V * •* *
“Betty”: When dad bought the now
planner he -had the neighbors in from miles
around to hear Mary play it. As she tore into the
instrument dad kept turning proudly to the audi-
ence, saying: “Ain’t she a marvel? Ain’t she a
wonder? Jever see the like?” Then as the blushing
musician pursued herself from one end of the instru-
ment to the other he roared: “Look at that 1 Why,
the; d—d pianner ain’t long enough for her!”
“Musketoon”: While I’m on the job I’ll
give you another deadly trap for mice. Get a kero-
sene tin and half fill with water. Sprinkle a fail-
layer-of chaff, bran, and a little bit of cheese gratings
on top. The chaff makes the surface of the lot
appear solid and dry, and the mouse jumping aboard
goes right through the layer into the water and the
trap is ready for the next item on the programme.
If the ground is not too hard sink the tin so that
the top is flush with the ground. Thousands
of mice are caught about the wheat-stacks in Vic-
toria this way.

THE SKITE.
BROWN : "That chap Smith is the biggest shite I know.”
JONES : "Go on, is he?”
BBOWN : "Yes! I can’t ever tell him about something Eve done without him telling me
he's done exactly the same thing.”
“Melos”: Pass out “Yammrs” sapling
(B. 12/4/’l7) and inspect this tree-trunk. It was
a hooded-gum, drawn to Constable’s Wharf, Bellin-
ger River (N.'S.W.), lately, and measured 90 feet
from the ground to the first limb. When felled, 12ft.
was butted off and the balance cut four logs. 16ft. x
16ft. 9im, 20ft. x 14ft. 2in., 16ft. x 14ft." 2in. and
14ft. x 13ft. 6in. After deducting a pipe averaging
-14 in. the sticks will cut about 9000 super feet, or
enough to build any ordinary five-roomed cottage.
“Eddyson”: Here is a subject for an
“Aboriginal!ties” symposium. Let us have a Kid
Stakes competition. If my experience of the Aus-
tralian bush and its wild and woolly inhabitants is
of any value at all, the piquant .and pleasing process
of “kidding” is the most popular form of entertain-
ment practised in our country places. To the bush-
men proper and the dwellers in lopsided, goat-
haunted townships and more desolate places kidding
is the truest form of wit, and the highest, if not
the only, kind of humor. . The men kid each other
■with labored deliberation, and invariably where
seven men are assembled there is a combination of
six to systematically and interminably kid the odd
man out. A form of kidding universally practised
in the Backblocks is to play upon some little, harm-
less vanity in the victim, and pull his leg everlast-
ingly. Nobody ever seems to weary of the business:
it is always fresh and savory, and every little mani-
festation of its effect on- the kidded is conveyed for
miles and miles, and minutely discussed-with relish.
In one district I frequented years ago a hapless
young spud-farmer was the subject of.a great kidding
bee, the ramifications of which extended over two-
thirds of the Wimmera. The potato-breeder was
one, Keeting, and the false doctrine promulgated by
all his friends and acquaintances was that this harm-
less innocent was a poet of transcendent genius. The
feeble, bumble-footed, ham-strung verses of the poet
Keeting were recited at all local gatherings; praises
of the sweet singer of Boodleoop were on every lip.
All strangers arriving at Boodleoop were immediately
introduced to the Boodleoop poet,, and were expected
to outdo Boodleoop’s hig'h opinions of its spot bard
and chatter a rhyme or two from his pen. This kid
was an enormous success. Keeting became a nuisance
to 80 Australian editors. He poured out leagues and
leagues of rhymes, and the Boodleoop Keeting
Society, a literary association formed to foster the
poet and spread appreciation and understanding of
his works, financed their postage to papers all over
the earth. This society even paid for the publica-
tion of a small collection of Keeting’s poems in
book form, and sent review copies to every editor
in Australia. The reception Keeting's verses won
outside Boodleoop was ascribed fo vicious and uncon-
scionable jealousy, and Boodleoop gave the poet a
consolation banquet, after which he recited his own
rhymes all night. Boodleoop’s tri-weekly, the
Baxx hr, printed a Keeting poem in every issue,
because everybody in Boodleoop wanted to read
Keeting’s poems, and complained only when the
sample was not as bad as it possibly could be. The
great Keeting Kid Stakes might have been going on
to this day, but Keeting contracted typhoid and
died ar the summit of his glory. In point of fact
the kid does continue, for on the headstone of Henry
William Horace Keeting are these two lines of his
own:—•
“Write on my grave when wattles droop:
‘He was the poet of Boodleoop.’ ”

THE BOOBEROI CUP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the dust-storms come a-swirling, and the
billabongs go dry,
And the roly-polies blow across the plains;
When the station tanks are failing, and the sheep
begin to die,
And lie rotting ’mid the burrs along the drains;
When the cheque-proud shearers’ mounts are eating
“post hay” at the pub,
And the drovers wallow in fermented joy;
When you cannot find a swagman on the stock
routes through the scrub,
They hold the races out at Booberoi.
The course is not quite perfect. Truth to tell, it’s
rather rough
1 n the cracked and sun-baked gilgais at the top ;
All the furlong posts are saplings, and they’re
suitable enough,
’Though they’re difficult to see unless you stop.
If they haven’t got a grandstand like Condobolin
boasts about,
And the judge’s box is just an empty tub,
You can see each start and finish, and then turn
around and “shout,”
For you never leave the front door of the pub.
We had just cut out at Roto,' and had struck
Euabalong,
We were mates that shearing, Jimmy Hunt and me, -
And we had a speedy pony that we’d bought at
Hunthawong,
It could do an outback mile in fifty-three.
There they told us of the races, and about the
Station Cup,
That Lyn Ilaley, after coaxing, had supplied,
So we had another pint or two and chucked the
saddles up,
We were certain of the double if we tried.
On the morning of the races there were folk from
every part
Who’d gathered there to cheer their favorites in,
With everyi sort of outfit, horse and bike, and car
and cart,
And a thousand dogs that raised a frightful din.
And the drinking started early, for, the judge he
gave a lead,
And the stewards, too, helped stow the grog away,
So that when they ran the Flying, and we won it
with The Weed,
Nearly everyone was full or getting gay.
But the Cup was our objective, and it looked a .
moral cert., *
There was nothing on the track we couldn’t beat ;
And when the betting opened I’d have laid my only
shirt
That. The AVeed would lead them homeward by a
street;
But instead of taking odds about this galloper of
ours,
The bookies offered threes and fives and eights,
While the judge and the committee backed a horse
of Paddy Power’s
That had no hope against us at the weights.
While they laid the odds in five pound-notes, The
Weed still lengthened out.
Says Jim, “These chaps are putting up a joke.
This here betting looks suspicious!” And I, too,
begian to doubt,
But I planked another tenner on our moke.
M hen the field was getting ready, Jim climbed on
the pony’s back—•
As a jockey he was always reckoned smart —
So I says: “You go like blazes, keep the middle of
the track,
You’ll be home and dried before the others start.”
It was just as I expected. Jim had thirty lengths
to spare,
And I smiled to think how easy he had won;
But the last horse wasn’t home when cries of “Pro-
test” rent the air,
And even then I thought ’twas only fun.
There was no mistake, however, for they said Jim
cut the course,
And all the jockeys swore that this was true;
It completely knocked me sideways, for they testified •
in force,
And our chances for the Cup looked very blue.
Then it dawned on me to tell them that if Jim had
cut a post,
Ihere’d be tracks to show where he had gone inside,
So I asked that they be pointed out, I had the
crowd on toast,
’Twas the simplest way the protest to decide.
Then the judge became excited, and he called the
stewards in,
And they whispered all together for a while;
When he said: “All right, we'll show you, but don’t
think you’re going to win,
For the tracks are at the post just near the mile.”
Now, they’d readied Paddy Foley, who was acting
clerk-of-course,
To' gallop round this post and make some tracks,
So Paddy, full of whisky, rushed and mounted on his
horse,
\\ hilc the rest of us were saddling up our hacks.'
AVhen we reached the post in question, we could see,
as clear as day,
The imprint plain of hoofs upon the ground,
But the jucfge and the committee stared in horrified
dismay,
For Pat had gone and raced the w-rong nay round.
And now along- the Lachlan when the bailey grass is
dry,
And tlie toiling- wool-teams struggle through the
lanes,
AVhen the wild black swans are winging coastward
through the evening sky,
And the desert peas are crimsoning the plains ;
AVhen the shearers and .the shed-hands tramp away
when shearing ends,
The grizzled veteran and the beardless boy
They tell the story over by the campfires in the
bends
Of how we won the Cup at Booberoi.
N.S.W.
GORDON BENNETT.

“Hay”: I was well acquainted with
Centralia long before the Speneer-Gillen trip and
knew many of the ways and lingos of the various
abo. tribes, but never heard of a ban of silence
being imposed on women as described by “D.D.”
(B. 12/4/’l7). In my opinion it would have been
impossible for any of them to preserve silence for
a day, much less 25 years. By the way, “D.D.”
does not mention why this penalty, if it existed, was
imposed. One peculiarity of theirs was silence
about the dead. Centralian abos. fear the spirits of
the departed; They are believers in the trans-
migration of souls and carefully avoid mentioning a
deceased Binghi’s name for fear he will return to
camp in some other form and do them harm. After
the burial ceremony nothing seems to disturb and
annoy black brother more than hearing the deceased
alluded to by a heedless white fellow. In this
regard, certainly, they all were voluntarily dumb.
* * * *
“8.H.”: “Goathead” (B. 5/4/’l7) gives
us one to go on with. A pal of his, he says, in
one day snigged dunnage and spread it, then pulled
COO bales of wool*out of a stack and stood them
end-up on the logs with an air-space between. The
most convenient dunnage anyone can use for the
above job is ordinary sft. Bin. fencing posts; and
as two of these placed parallel will just take two
bales of wool, leaving an air-space between, you
want no algebra to calculate that it will take 600
posts spread out over the landscape to accommodate
the aforementioned parcel of wool. Now, for one
man to catch a gee and snig that pile of lumber from
any distance, be it 20 yards or 20 chains, is a day’s
work that would make an I.W.W. advocate gnash his
teeth. Smith knocks off for breakfast. He then
bores into the 90-ton stack of sheep-whiskers, pulls
it down and sets it up again, high and dry, by dark.
If he had taken an average of only two minutes to
deal with each bale, including the snigging and
spreading, of course it would have eaten up 20
hours. Long day, eh?
*-* * *
“Bung”: When you are measuring up
Australia’s forest giants don’t miss those that grow
thickly in the valleys of Dromedary Mountain,
South Coast (N.S.W.). One spotted gum from there
provided the keel of the steamship Wee Clyde, at
present trading between Sydney and Bermagui. The
splinter was 68ft. long, 18 by 24 inches, with a
perfectly straight grain, knotless, and free from
gumcracks, sap or heart. It had a 3Rt. curve for
the ship’s prow-seating, at one end, and this was
not • from any bend in the tree, but was carved
from the solid half-trunk. Making allowance for
sap and heart the weed must have been at least Bft.
in diameter at the butt.
* * * *
“X.Y.”: “Bung” asks (B. 19/4/’l7) for
information concerning the firing of green or damp
hay, and why fodder in the same moist condition
does not fire when stored in a silo. The explana-
tion is that in the latter case the oxygen of the
air, is almost entirely excluded. When green or
damp vegetable matter is heaped together it under-
goes a kind of fermentation, rapidly absorbing
oxygen from the atmosphere, the temperature of
the mass rising in proportion to the extent of such
absorption. If the material be strongly compressed,
or the air be otherwise excluded, a degree of fer-
mentation, with evolution of heat, still takes place
owing to the presence of oxygen in the material
itself, but this to a much less extent. The writer
has seen hay—lucerne, oaten and other —stacked in
ill sorts of conditions, and has found the interiors
GIFT DAY.
“’ Ere, you be quiet, or I’ll give you to the
gentleman to take ’ome!”
of some of the stacks reduced to a material resem-
bling ashes (like the fire-fang of stable manure
heaps), but has never known one to actually burst
into flame. Why stack green or wet material ? You
cannot make hay of it. My experience has been that
oaten or wheaten hay when sheafed by the reaper
and binder, and properly • cured in the field, heats
less than when mown, raked and cocked, the reason
being that the sheafed stuff can be packed more
elostfiy in the stack, and the air is thus, to a large
extent, excluded. .
“W.H.R.”: A similar but more humane
method of treating jibbers than “Pick’s” barbed-wire
idea (B. 12/4/’l7) is with the hair rope. This is
of soft hemp with short lengths of horsehair woven
into it. In use it is passed under the neddy’s tail
and fastened to the shafts. When he starts to back
he usually thinks better of it. This is part of a
system of breaking introduced into England by an
Australian horsetamer. Young colts are taught to
follow a lead by having the rope put under their
tail and attached in front to the halter.

“W.D.”: Will somebody of experience in
the matter tell me what to do for my horses, which,
after having a meal, will leave the stall and eat
their own droppings ? They are well fed on grain
(cracked and crushed), bran and chaff; they are
in a large yard bare of grass; they are fed regu-
larly three times each day. I keep the yard as clean
as possible, forking it out daily, but while I’m away
they get up to this new fad. •

“Six and Eight” .: In B. 12/4/’l7 the state-
ment that the venom of tiger-snakes is four times
stronger than that of the cobra is charac-
terised by “Montalex” as silly. Well, in
“Animals of Australia,” by Lucas and Le
Souef,' the tiger-snake is described as one .of
the deadliest of Australian snakes, the venom,
the authors assert, being considerably more
potent than that of the cobra. The reasons
they give for the bite of the cobra being
more deadly than that of Australian snakes
are: (1) the quantity of venom voided at one
time by Australian snakes is insignificant
compared with the amount voided by the
cobra ; (2) -the fangs of Australian venomous
snakes are not nearly so long as thoses of
the cobra and consequently have less chance
of penetrating a vein, an injection there
being at least 10 times as deadly as a mere
sub-cutaneous injection; (3) because the
points of the fangs of Australian snakes are
solid for a quarter of their length, so that
the poison is not conducted to the full length,
of the fang. In Australian snakes the poison
when ejected from the gland runs down a
groove on the outside of the fangs, and an
ordinary sock or the cloth of trousers will
suuiY ui me uiuin ui uuusers win
usually be quite sufficient to prevent the poison
entering, the wound.

“Margot”: Agree with “Ulooloo” (B.
12/4/’l7) that a snake is not harmless when its
back is fractured. The other day I met a son of
Satan 6ft. long, and my mate broke his spinal
column, whilst I vainly tried to hold the dogs
back. The reptile made several springs at the
animals, narrowly missing them. His body from the
break to the tail seemed paralysed, but his business
end was in good going order and condition.
* * * *
“Lex”: At a recent camp in Central
Queensland we discovered a sure cure for sand-fly
bites. Take an ordinary , bottle of beer, drink the
contents and clean the bottle. (If you haven’t beer
a bottle of whisky will do.) Then fill it with water
and add about two tablespoons of boracic acid.
When applied this not only relieves the pain but
stops the bites from turning into sores.
“H.V.E.”: Unnatural history from John
Foster Fraser’s “Australia, the Making of a Nation”:
“The bower-bird makes its' nest a sort of passage
and then decorates it with any bright articles it
may find.” The facts are that the bird builds an
open nest t of twigs lined with root fibres, sometimes
a quarter-mile or more distant from the bower,
which is used purely for canoodling and recreative
purposes. In its choice of “bright articles” the
satin-bower-bird confines itself almost entirely to
objects of a blue shade, such as blue-bags (10 of
which I found about one bower), scraps of blue
glass and paper, blue parrot feathers and blue
flowers.
* * * ' *
“H.V.E.”: Westralia possesses the most
peculiar example of the marsupial tribe, viz., the
nail-tailed kangaroo, sometimes called wallaby. The
tail ends in a sharp horny spine, like that which
rounds off the death-adder, but neither beast uses
this appendage as a means of offence. The death-
adder uses its stiletto to anchor with when about to
bite, or as an aid to its progress in rough country,
and the nail-tailed kangaroo probably follows suit.
This marsupial is found in the vicinity of the pearl-
ing grounds, and in some quarters is (or was) so
numerous as to afford a supply of fresh meat for the
Jap and Malay divers. Yet a live example is said to
be worth £5 at any zoo.
* * * *
“XY.”: In the coast districts of N. S.
Wales sheafed hay should be stooked as soon as
possible after the machine delivers it. This stook-
ing is the vital operation in successful hay-curing
in damp climates. It is done by two men (or
boys), who commence by selecting a high and
dry site. They then pick out the two longest and
stoutest sheaves, which they set aside as caps. Each
operator then picks up two sheaves of the next-
largest size, and these are placed on the selected
site by both men at the same time. The butts of
the sheaves are lightly bumped on the ground to
give them a firm stand. They are straddled, so as
to leave an air-space in the centre, and the pair
thus held by each man is inclined towards the pair
held by the neighbor. This results in a pile of
four well-placed sheaves. The two operators then
pick up two more pairs, which they place at the
rear of the first pairs. We have now eight well-
placed sheaves in the stook, and it only remains to
cap, or thatch, them. To do this each operator
takes up one of the cap sheaves, and by bumping it
on the ground he makes the base oblique instead of
square. That is, it would appear as a mitre-cut
instead of a square-cut base. He then inserts both
hands into the sheaf, below the band, on the side
on which the band is nearer to the sloping base,
holding the sheaf against his chest meanwhile. With
his two hands thus inserted he opens out the sheaf
into the shape of a hollow fan and places it on the
stook, standing astride of his end of the stook.
His mate does the same from the other end of the
stook, and the leading hand of the pair then hands
his mate a wisp from each side of his cap sheaf
which the other crosses and binds, thus securing
not only the caps but the heads of the sheaves
below. While this tie is being made the leading
hand presses the sloping bases of the caps together,
with the result that a sharp high-pitched roof ,is
formed which will defy all ordinary rain-storms,
while the hay actually cures more rapidly than if
left on the ground. Built thus the stooks will defy
ordinary high winds, as well as rain. Twenty
gores of oaten hay, thus stooked, were fit for the
stack when heavy daily thunderstorms set in, and
this weather lasted for two months. Much wind
attended the storms. Very few of the stooks were
blown down. At the end of the time stated, it
was decided to burn the hay, in order to clear
the ground. When the stooks were opened it was
found that the hay was absolutely undamaged, and
that the discoloration caused by the weather only
extended to the outside straws.
“Barcoo”: “Bill R.” (B. 5/4/’l7) is
talking through his whiskers when he states that
the koala, though gentle and docile, will live only
in the freedom of the bush. As a matter of fact, in
captivity Lindsay’s pals will live happy and con-
tented for years. One has been browsing in my
back yard at Rockhampton (Q.) for the last 12
years, though he is now more disagreeably perfumed
than when in the “Teddy” portion of his existence.
Death in. the early stages of captivity is caused by
the ratty idea of so many people that everything
lives on bread. Fed to the young koala that fodder
means certain death quickly.
THE OMNISCIENCE OF THE LAW.
“Your gin die on Tuesday, eh, Jimmy? How
old was she?"
“Same age belongit to me."
“But how old are you?”
“Same age belongit to her."
“ Yairs, of course. But how old are you?”
"I dunno. You ask Missa Frost, the police-
man—he know.”
THE CIRCLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now “Mulga” he told me a truthful tale that some-
one told to him;
The someone, as far as I could learn, was a bloke
, by the name of Jim;
And Jim he got the tale, it seems, from a chap at
Down-and-Out;
And the chap derived the story from an English
rouseabout.
The rouseabout he got the yarn from Wilson at
The Bore;
And Wilson had it told to him by the keeper of
the store ;
The storeman heard the yarn from Mac, who hoofed
from Piker's pub,
And swapped it plus some adjectives for a day’s
supply of grub.
Now Mae had pinched the tale from Crane, who
came from Broken Hill;
And Crane had heard it from the lips of a pirate
labelled Bill; p
The latter got the tale from Brown, who was fencing
down the crick ;
And Brown had heard the story from a bloke called
“Murdering Mick.”
Murdering Mick had heard it told by the driver
of the mail;
And from McCluskey, droving sheep, the driver
learned the tale;
And McCluskey heard the yarn from me in a season
long gone by;
’Twas never much of a yarn at best —besides, ’tuas
a blanky lie!
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.

for Brisbane, received a cable to say Inn-
favorite brother had been killed in 1 ranee.
George Mnsgrove was reported to have
made £40,000 out of his luckiest speculation
—"The Belle of New York.” it was more
than its author, 0. M. S. McLellau, left

••0.K.”: The Akgus looked a long way
back to find a prototype for Lilian Tucker,
of “Very Good Eddie.” It described her as
“a handsome giri, whose appearance and
style old playgoers at once associated with
Miss Marion Ilood, of the famous Leslie
and Barren days.” It is 25 years since
Marion Hood was in Australia, and then
she didn't come with Nellie Barren and
Leslie, but with the subsequent Gaiety co.
that Mnsgrove imported. Moreover, Lilian
Tucker isn't very suggestive 6f the well-
preserved Marion, who was a middle-aged
woman when Melbourne saw her. The Taits’
leading lady has a greater resemblance to
Lily Elsie, the original Merry Widow. By
the way, she plays the character of Elsie
Lilly, which is getting near it.
“Stargazer”: The conspicuous stupidity
in shows like “Very Good Eddie” is the
effort to introduce a serious love interest.
The comparatively lucid hero bounds on and
occupies time and attention explaining the
state of his affections towards the nearly-
reasonable heroine in a manner that suggests
the ardor of a cucumber for a spud. The
semi-sane heroine also dilates on her inner-
most feelings with regard to the almost-pos-
sible hero, as the spud might to the cucum-
ber. Then both are unceremoniously rele-
gated to the bin. They may reappear to
carol a plaintive lay, but for all that their
personal fortunes matter to the general
diversion they might as well never have been
born. In a show in which everything else
is bizzare a pair of serious lovers are as
much at home as a Carmelite procession at
a cabaret. They jolt the whole entertain-
ment out of gear every time they appear,
and are endured only because audiences have
become so egregiously polite nowanights that
they hesitate to cast open derision even on
performers who deliberately outrage their
sense of fitness.
More explorations into the queer civilisa-
tion of the Australian :—•
Muriel Shm- says the gallery girl is quite an
institution in Sydney and Melbourne. Australian
managers pav more attention to the gallery than
English and American ones do. They allot certain
blocks of seats to the girls, who take a very keen
interest in the performance. “If they like you they
cheer and applaud and crowd round the stage-door
after the play is over. But if they dislike an actor
he may as well quit. They give him the bird—a
peculiar sort of whistle —-and boo him persistently.”
Your late visitor says that at a benefit performance
she saw a couple of actors assailed from the gal-
lery in this way, and it was “really a most distress-
ing spectacle.”
“Masetto” : I can travel the same path a
good way with “E.F.” (B. 12/4/T7) in
praise of tenor Cappelli, but stop suddenly
when lie rates Dani below our present visi-
tor. Dani’s voice was not hard at all, and
he had a greater range than Cappelli. The
Gonsalez orchestra played to a low pitch
for a start, and on top of that Cappelli had
most of his principal solos transposed down
a half or a full tone. I am not blaming
Cappelli for doing this, but Dani sang all
liis in the original. The high C’s which the
sapient critics in the daily press credited
Cappelli were about B flat. And he will
have to be a lot less theatrical than “E.F.”
says he is before he impresses the opera-
gorged Old World.
“C. Ockney”: I agree with “Fax” (B.
26/4/’l7) that Marie, of “The Marriage of
Kitty,” recalls the Marie who figured in the
Fanny Brough part in “The Whip” (the
actress’s name, by the way, was Illington.
not Illingworth). But. strangely enough,
there is yet another of the same front-name
to” be considered in this connection. This is
Marie Wilton Bancroft, a wonderful come-
dienne of my youth, whose art. as the music-
hall ads. say, was “copied by many, excelled
by none.”
“J.F.H.” (London) : “Zig-zag,” the new
revue at London Hippodrome, has given
Daphne Pollard an excellent chance to
sing and dance, and she does both so well
that she adds to the credit and renown of
the old Australian theatrical family to
which she belongs. Daphne rivals Ivy
Shilling in her astonishing feats of acrobatic
dancing, and she has the added advantages
of an excellent singing voice and a madcap
style of humor. Her “Little Miss Quaint.”
illustrated with uncanny spookery and elec-
tric trimmings, is far and away the best
and most successful song in the revue.
“T. 0.1.”: “Playbird’s” Ballarat playbill
(B. 26/4/T7) is surely a fake. Lola Montez
left Australia via Sydney in 1850. She had
arrived from ’Frisco late in 1855. and in six-
months’ time had played herself out of Syd-
ney, Melbourne, Ballarat, Bendigo and Gee-
long. With her leading man, Folland, she
then left Newcastle (N.S.W.) in the Jane
Falkenberg for San Francisco. According
to one account Folland was lost overboard
before the ship reached Honolulu; another
account—l believe it to be the correct one —
was that he suicided before reaching the
Golden Gate.
Business, Robbery, etc
"Red Tape, Jim.”: The bosses in N. S.
Wales Public Service are getting rattled over
the girl clerk business. They lmte what our
chief calls the “feminine element.” and most
of them have managed to keep Miss Skirtau-
blouse'at bay except at the typewriter; but
here’s trouble. Just before the election
Willie Holman sidled up to the temporary
male clerk with 325. a day, but recipients
had hardly finished giving three cheers when
a knock came to the door. It was the Pub-
lic Service Board that had called round to
make these few remarks to the chief: (3)
the temporary male clerk at 12s. is expen-
sive; (2) the temporary female clerk is
cheap; (3) think it over. As I write this
there are 327 flappers on the stairs with
references from Snooks’s Business College
waiting for the early doors to open.

“Te Bnbarantiko”: In spite of all the
blather about the debt we owe him and
never can discharge, Billjim, back with a few
pounds of deferred pay to draw, seems to
he regarded as fair game by everybody with
a gold-brick. Frequent ads. appear in the
dailies offering returned soldiers plantation
overseers’ jobs in the sickliest Pacific Island
groups where feiv men can retain their health
for more than a year, and some die. Young
men, strong and sound in body, have gone
to those jobs only to return in a very short
time, sick with fever, loneliness and dis-
appointment. The pay—from £lO per
month, quarters supplied—looks good to the
newelmm who is cheerful about “finding
himself” until he discovers that his food will
cost him not less than £6 a month. The
frequency with which the same ads. appear
is indication enough of the quality of the
jobs and what men think of them.
