“Sugitrloaf" : If enough loan money ever
comes along to finish it. the new reservoir
which the Vic. Government building at
Stigarloaf, on the Upper Goulburn, will be
easily the biggest in Australia, and, as things
stand at present, the biggest in the world
except possibly for one or two in U.S.A.
The plans provide for a 180 ft. dam
which will hold up 018,000 acre-feet or
41,000,000,000-odd cubic feet of water, as
against the 33,000,000,000 cubic feet in
Burrinjuck. That would give the present
population of Vic. nearly 200,000 gallons of
water per head. But at the present rate of
going it will take several years to finish the
first stage, which means a wall 135 feet high
to impound 280,000 acre-feet of water.
Whether it goes any higher depends. If it
is ever finished right out it will provide
enough water to irrigate a million acres at
a cost of not much over £1,000,000. At the
ss. an acre-foot charged for the Burrinjuck
water there ought to be good money in it.
And with a head of 180 ft. and such a supply
of water, there ought to be something doing
in the generation of cheap electric energy.
“Croson” : No, “Adelaide," the milch cow
sold at Thebarton (S.A.) market on 23/3/’l7
for £34 is not quite a record. Two Jersey
milkers were sold in the Melbourne cattle-
yards in March (I have not the exact date)
for £05 —-one bringing £2O 10s.. the other
£3B 10s. They were bred by Miss Locking,
who. with her sister, runs a. dairy-farm at
Nutting Ilill, between Oakleigh and Fern
Tree Gully.
“Ilalf-Moon”: Personally I take no count
of black cats or 13 at a table or two at a
beer ; but a story told me the other day by
a Cape York sandahvood-getter seems worth
passing on. After the inter-State boat Yon-
gala was lost he was camped on the shore of
Lloyd's Bay (N.Q.), and a travelling basket
was washed ashore. It contained some male
apparel including a dress-shirt with opal-
links and studs. There was little doubt
where it had come from. The finder offered
the opal set to a friend as a present, but the
friend wasn’t taking any chances'with fate.
Eventually it was traded to a Thursday
Island jeweller who sold it to the engineer of
a boat. The buyer wasn't concerned in the
least about its history. But soon afterwards
he joined the unlucky Koombana and was
lost with her in the Westralian hurricane.
-Good Australian" : A liquor war is being
fought in California for two principles. (11
the abolition of bars and (2i the prevention
of the sale and use of "strong drinks" —
whisky, gin and brandy. The Rominger
Bill, introduced in the* State Parliament,
will shut up saloons and bars everywhere
and will allow the use of beer and wine, but
not of spirits, in hotels, homes, restaurants,
trains, steamers, etc. Therefore the brewers
and winegrowers have joined forces with the
wowsers to push the Bill through. This
union is akin to the co-operation between
the Churches and the bookies against the
totalisator in Australia. But the Bill is
regarded by the fanatics as merely the half-
way house to total prohibition. In a very
few years, it seems to me, the United States
will go "bone dry. - ’ and be a country neither
making nor using alcohol.
“Uckle” : The mice plague in the Central
West and over the Bland country (N.S.W.)
is bad business. Go through a recently-
sown wheat paddock and you will find mil-
lions of tiny burrows. The inmates are
living on the newly-sown seed, and next sea-
son’s harvest will show the effects. The
corn crops' have been chopped to piece# by
the little pests, who climbed up and muti-
lated nearly every cob. Hayricks fairly stink
with mouse, and heaps of grain husks are
kicked out around stacks after tin 1 style of
the earth from rabbit burrows. Chaff-cut-
ters are having a bad time with the
machines getting clogged with mouse-fur and
particles of flesh. Tomato crops are also
much fancied by the visitors, and store-
keepers are wondering whether they can
legitimately add to the selling price of cheese
the loss in that line. The 1.0. d only knows the
extent of the damage in the many big wheat
stacks throughout the west. The contamin-
ating smell will alone spoil thousands of
bushels. The footprints of the marching
millions in the dusty roads are a peculiar
feature, resembling somewhat the marks
caused by a short rain shower on -the dust.
Every idea in trapping is being tried, but the
numbers of the invaders show no signs of
growing less. The animals seem to be an
under-fed species, not much more than half
the size of the ordinary house mouse.
A liquid poison encouraged by Britain's
glorious freetrade was the "special Scotch
whisky” distilled in Berlin. Millions of
gallons of this raw potato spirit went to
careless England during the 10 years before
the war, the labels being so printed that
the patriotic importer could cut off the
words “Produce of Germany'’ without leav-
ing any evidence of his illegal act. Ger-
many seems to have created its army of
chemists for impurely commercial reasons
and the purpose of foreign invasion. Fraudu-
lent manufacture was the soul of their
cheap science, and their experiments were
mainly devoted to the art of concocting
imitation foods and other instruments of
destruction.

“Counter" : I'm in the carpet department.
When the dear little girl rustled in I knew
her for a bride and hoped for a good order
as I juggled cheerfully with the rolls and
squares for 20 minutes. But she was hard
to please, and I began to think she’d want
to see every line in the warehouse. At
last she decided that No. 24 exhibit would
do. though it wasn’t quite what she wanted.
“You can cut me off half a yard,” she
drawled, “though I expect it’s really more
than I shall require. It’s for a pair of
carpet slippers.”
* * * *
The well-bred London visitor never takes
two lumps of sugar in her tea now. A
returned soldier has been telling his Toorak-
ese friends how well off they are with brim-
ming sugar-basins. If you went into a
London shop and asked casually for a
pound of sugar the shopman would call for
help. A buyer of a pound’s worth of goods
may be accommodated with a pound of sugar
at a stiff price; but the favor is great. And
the stuff you get after all the fuss is gener-
ally the treacly brown sugar we once used
for home-made toffee.

1 “8.D.”: One of the finest timbers ever
grown in Australia is the fast-disappearing
Murray pine. White ants won't eat it, and,
apart from that, it is hard to beat as
material for houselining and all sorts of
indoor work. On the sandy ridges in the
Murray country lots of it have been rooted
out to make room, for wheat. The sand
grows a good crop in a wet season, but in
a dry one it is apt to go over into the next
county once the pines are gone. The fact
that white ants won’t eat the Murray pine
is probably the reason why a paternal Gov-
ernment doesn’t use it up in that country,
but sends imported pine, which the little
dears do like.

“Bombardier B.” : We’ve renamed all the
little estaminets ; so if you want a drink at
any of the following pubs you can just
enlist and come to France: “The Laun-
derer’s Reunion,” “The Beautiful View of
the Railway Station,” “The Bundle of
Straw.” “The Old Joke” and (about 200
yards down the road) “The New Joke,” and
“The Assurance Against Thirst.” But just
to let the world see that we know our
French, we allow a shell-smashed estaminet.
with only the front wall left intact, to still
do business bravely under the sign “A La
Paix.”
“ILII.B.” : A miller-friend tells me that in
addition to the 6fd. taken off the recent in-
stalment of Is. in connection with the Wheat
Fool, the cocky has to pay s|d. per bushei
for “administration.” Assuming our total crop
to be over 40 million bushels, which I believe
is not far from the mark, this represents
something like £1,000,000 spent on “adminis-
tration.” Is it a stupendous leg-pool or are
they really making a Canberra job of the
Empire’s bread, as well as of its metal and
its munitions?
OUR SPONGES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The thirst of our sponges is exceptional, one
sample recently taken from the eastern coast
absorbing 17 times its own weight of liquor with-
out a drip. Another smaller sample absorbed 24
times its own weight.”
A sea-grown item, brown and small,
It weighs perhaps two ounces;
Yet as a willing drinker all
Competitors it trounces.
Three pounds of beer ’twill swallow down,
Or that dead weight of whisky,
And make no bid to paint the town.
Or ape the capricornian clown
With actions free and frisky.
Could Brown into his depths lot flow
Of beer the same' proportion,
He’d hold four ton of booze with no
Abdominal distortion ;
But even when his bursts appal,
And recklessly he plunges,
With half his bulk he couldn't crawl —
Which proves he is no class at all
When ranked among our sponges !
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.
THE PUZZLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She came out gaily on the Mage,
And threw herself about.
It, seemed she’d presently engage
In some fantastic rout.
tVhen she regained her equipoise
1 fancy that I heard a noise.
Alternately, she worked her jaw,
And threw a pointless prance.
1 looked the programme up, and saw
This was a song and dance.
And now I have a lasting itch —
1 live and wonder which was which !
VICTORIA.

THE G.E. ELECTRIC FLAT IRON!
How do you do ? Here I am again. Permit me the pleasure of drawing your attention to the
little lady in this picture. How happy she looks. Why ? Well, having already installed B. I .H.
Mazda Electric Lamps throughout her home, she now seeks further comfort and economy, so has
purchased a G.E. ELECTRIC FLAT IRON. Ironing day is now transformed—it has become
“ a thing of beauty and a joy forever.” All she has to do when she desires to iron, is just
switch on the current, and the iron becoming heated immediately, remains hot until the ironing
is finished. How convenient! how economical ! how simple !
In these days when domestic servants have all “ folded their tents like the Arabs, and as
silently stolen away,” an electric iron is a necessity. It is even more than that—it is one of
the finest and most indispensable labour-saving devices that a modern housewife can adopt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Those who lead fat oxen should themselves
be fat. Consider William Morris Hughes as
leader of the Fat party. Yet he has some
to consideration as a Pauncheous
The Commonwealth will have nearly
reached the age of discretion when the next
election happens along. The birth certifi-
cate was issued in 1900.
Since the. departure of Gorge Reid Bigjohn
h orrest is the predominating fat man of
Australian politics. In the course of a
recent campaigning appearance the vast ex-
tent of his equator drew from a shrill elector
an admiring: “My word, John, it’s a long
time since you dropped anything on your
toes.” J
SAME THING.
H ECK: “They say that when a man is drown-
ing his past life is brought up before
him.”
PECK: “That happens also when he has a
quarrel with his wife.”
There are no mudless victories these times.
Down _at bedrock it transpires that to
about 715 organisers and party bell-wethers
the real significance of repatriation is that
hope deferred makes the smart trick.
Labor made three Prime Ministers of Aus-
tralia—Watson. Fisher and Hughes—and as
not one of these now carries the old brand,
the Commonwealth can claim to show
something that in the party sense looks
like a record Anyway, this paper
knows of no parallel to such a happen-
ing. And there is the further circumstance
that outside of Bananaland there is not one
of the former State Premier’s who climbed
to where he was on the back of Labor
occupying the old address. Holman, Scad-
den, Vaughan, McGowen—all are in the
other paddock, there has been some
earthquake right enough, and it’s shaken up
dross in more ways than one.
BUSINESS AS USUAL.
HUBBY : “ Humph! I suppose now you’ve
joined this War Economy Society you’ll
he cutting out new dresses?”
SHE: “Oh, dear, no! My dressmaker will
still do that.”
Mi’s. Seery, more or less chivalrously
given a chance by the P.L.L. of seeing what
she could do in the hopeless Robertson
(N.S.W.) electorate, did better than was
anticipated. At the previous election J. A.
Fraser went down by 1500 votes, and that
was at a time when the Labor colors were
flying high, and the party that Cook led was
in general disfavor. In reducing the adverse
majority substantially under less favorable
conditions Mrs. Seery has done sufficiently
well to cause a review of the old judgment
that a woman candidate has no chance; and
that may mean many more feminine
aspirants at the next elections. N. S. Wales
narrowly missed having a lady candidate
amongst the Labor Senate three, also, for
when the election was being made, Mrs.
Kate Dwyer was well up amongst a plethora
of candidates.
Joe Cook explains himself:—
I have changed my mind on many subjects since
the war broke out. I will probably change it
again.
Quite so—especially about W. M. Hughes
and various Sosh tigers now feeding out of
the hand of Fat.
Eddyson” : George Reid’s summing up of
the late Charles Kingston comes to hand per
rugged manliness and good fellow-
ship.” As anything like an adequate esti-
mate of Kingston the Democrat this is just
piffle. I hannen to have a very clear recol-
lection of Kingston on Ried. Another
eminent politician of opposite fiscal views
then co-operating with George had claimed
that Reid was. of an indolent disposition,
and would not be the dominating factor in
the partnership. Kingston wagged a long,
impressive forefinger. “Don’t flatter your-
self, man,” he said. “If George Reid and
you were cast upon a desert island, Reid
would not only convince you that it was
your duty to be cooked; damn it, he would
persuade you to carry the wood for the fire!”
W. H. Burgess, ex-Mayor of Hobart and
member of the State Parliament, w’ent
through scores of elections for the City Coun-
cil and Parliament in his time, and a
remarkable item was that he always headed
tne poll. Burgess used to tell a funny story
about his soldiering. In the early ’Bo’s of
I? St century- a Bill was introduced into
the Tasmanian Parliament for the payment
of fees to officers and men who attended drill.
Burgess was a major, and it meant £l2 a
year to him. Giblin, who was Attorney-
General at the time, told Burgess that he
must resign his seat in the Assembly for
Hobart, as he was drawing Government
money. W.H. replied that £8 was now due
to him, but he would give the Department
a receipt for it, and let it go. But the Go-
vernment would not let him do this; thrust
£8 into his breeches pocket, and then com-
pelled him to resign his seat. Burgess went
to his constituents, told them he had got £8
Government money “under duress,” and the
electors laughed well and sent him back to
the House. The election cost him a good
lot more than £B.
Blundell. Minister for marine affairs in
S. Aus., lately took the unusually sensible
step of cutting a deputation short after the
opening speech. It came to him to press for
a graving-dock at Port Adelaide, and Blun-
dell’s simple reply was that the Government
was already pledged to the work, and had
selected the site and instructed the Harbors
- Board to get on with the plans. So the
deputation was “an absurd waste of time,”
and the curtain was rung down with : “The
Government is going on with the dock.
Gentlemen, the deputation is at an end.” •
I cannot but admire the thoroughness
with which the National party organisation
has covered the ground. My two off-siders,
aged seven and four, received letters from
that body, addressed with the receivers’ full
names, exhorting them to vo>e for the Phew-
sion Senate three. And I have heard of
other such enlightened citizens who received
a similar compliment to their intelligence.
Unfortunately my youngest citizen can’t
read—and here I detect an error of psycho-
logy in this electioneering. That letter
should have been written in pictures,
preferably colored. For lack of this
her vote is lost to those who are now
about to win the war. Now, how and where
did the wonderful W.T.W. organisers dis-
cover the names and addresses of these good
but immature Australians?
[Arthur 11. Adams, somewhere in Cremorne.]
Just how safe Australian industry is in
the hands of the Ilughes-Cook crowd is in-
dicated by Cook’s sneer:—
Mr. Tudor had promised to reopen the tariff.
But it was Mr. Tudor’s tariff, and if it was already
so bad that it wanted ripping open it did not say
much for that gentleman’s statesmanship. While
the war was on the tariff could wait.
Cook’s argument is a rotten one. If the
tariff, is Tudor’s, and he (Tudor) admits
that it needs ripping open, it is the best of
all indications that it does want the knife.
Nor can the operation be left till after the
war. Australia clamors for a tariff that
will protect its industries long before the
next war (commercial) begins, so that there
will be work for our returning soldiers, and
so that the Commonwealth can get down to
the job of wiping off its awful war indebted-
ness.
“Wart”: When the Launceston trams
were started five years ago the aldermen
decently refrained from following the prac-
tice of voting themselves free rides. Since
then the codlin moth seems to have under-
mined their virtue, because at their last
meeting a resolution was passed to cart the
municipal meat on the nod. What makes
the job more glaring is the fact that a short
time ago the Bumbles turned down a propo-
sition to allow the wounded Billjims who
are convalescing at the local base hospital a
similar privilege.
THERE OR THEREABOUTS.
FIRST ARTIST : “Do you mean to say that
that picture you sold him was a work
of art?"
SECOND ARTIST: “No, hut the yarn I told
him about it was!”
Reid’s attempt in his just-produced remin-
iscences to hit off Bill Lyne: “Amiable in
private life, but rude in public life.” It
was Bill Lyne who, when The Bulletin’s
old model said he had been ordered by his
doctor to take riding exercise, and com-
plained that he had been unable to secure a
suitable horse, said heavily: “Most likely
the doctor was thinking of an elephant.”

beginning to be guessed at. The loss in still-

eased people who would otherwise be useful
citizens, can’t be estimated ; nor yet the mere
loss of working time by casual sufferers
while they are getting cured. But, on this
last economic point, a new book by a mem-

BILLJIM : “Why, it’s Thursday morning,

ber of the recent Royal Commission in Eng-
land remarks incidentally that in one year,
just before the war, the British navy 'and
army together lost 485,000 working days
from venereal disease. So, in the only
quarter-million men about whom statistics
are available, well over a thousand years of

What would the figures for the whole nation

The passing of “Bill” Ferris, of Parra-
matta (N.5.W.)., recalls political wizardry
of the old town. In the early ’Bo’s
Hughie Taylor, of the flowing beard and
square hat, was the sitting Freetrade mem-
ber ; Ferris was the Protectionist candidate.
They were friends of the same (R.C.) faith
and purred at the municipal council table;
but politically they fought “all in.” Ferris’s
growing popularity brought things to a climax,
and, with the business eye of a showman,
Taylor built a staging in the spreading tree
which overhung the footpath in the main
street, and placed thereon two loaves of
bread —the ordinary 21b. (labelled “Free-
trade”), and another half the size (ticketed
“Protection”). The foolish electors wept
over the broken bread and Taylor got the
seat.
“Selected Inmate’s” proposal:—■
Maybe it’s too late, but it has just occurred to
me that our whole system of elections is wrong,
expensive, futile and subversive to the glorious
principles of democracy. The idea struck me just
after having made three crosses on a sheet of cheap
paper, using my own pencil, the official implement
having been neatly detached from its string by
some business-like elector who got there before me.
I put those three crosses in the squares allotted to
three names that doubtless represented living indi-
viduals ; but I had neither heard them speak nor
ever to., my knowledge seen them j nor have I ever
felt the least longing to se* or hear them. And if
only enough other individuals as feckless as myself
put enough crosses in those particular squares, those
three unknown persons will make quite a lot of
laws for me. They will represent me; they will do
what a majority of “mes” vaguely tell them to do;
they will become official gramophones giving out
the desires and yearnings of a million inarticulate
myselves. Well, why not choose those three grama-
phones by a lottery ? Everybody who wanted to be
a representative of democracy could send in his
name and be allotted a numbered marble. These
marbles would then be put into a barrel and drawn.
The first 100 or so drawn would be the Federal
Parliament; and the holders could then ballot for
a Prime Minister and the other accessories or -orna-
ments of government. The method would be cheap,
expeditious, scrupulously fair, and would appeal
to our sporting instincts. The resulting body of
legislators would infallibly represent the democracy;
the theory of chance would see to it that the various
views held by the community were proportionally
represented ; and as our leaders disclaim any desire
to lead the community, and leave all important
public questions to be decided by ourselves, why
worry about the names or morals of the official
gramaphones selected as I suggest to record our
views ?

THE WAR WINNER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The bitter brawl that raged round William Hughes
Is ended now. Election Day is o’er.
The loud proclaimer of Phewsonic views •
Must Win the War.
What Watt inferred, or Mannix didn’t say,
Or Joe Cook stated when he held the floor
Will hardly help the Empire, anyway,
To W T in the War.
The blood is up of William Morris Hughes,
And, ever thirsting for a foeman’s gore,
He stands, undaunted by disturbing news,
To IVin the W’ar.
Aye, stands or sits, and eloquently talks
Of what he thinks or what he thought before;
And 0! he’s taking steps where’er he walks
To Win the W’ar.
The fighting spirit animates his face.
And, still the Billy that he was of yore,
He battles in his self-appointed place
To Win the War.
His thumb tight pressed against his valiant nose,
His fingers turned towards the Caucus door,
He nerves himself for further mighty blows
To Win the War.
Yet, when he looks around the corpse-strewn field
He wipes his eye—his tender heart is sore.
Old comrades dead, who would not Party yield
To Win the War 1
Himself would yield all principles, but not
The billet that ambition hankered for;
Stern duty bade him stick to what he’d got
To Win the War.
Still fighting on, no matter which the side,
He has no dread of reckonings in store;
He knows his epitaph will be—“He tried
To Win the War.”
O, God of Battles, Judge of mortals who
Embrace the creed that once they did abhor,
Guide Billy Hughes and teach him what to do
TO WIN THE WAR.
MELBOURNE.
EDMUND FISHER.

COMSTOCK’S “DEAD-SHOT” WORM
PELLETS.
A SAFE, SURE, AND RELIABLE SPECIFIC FOR
THE ERADICATION OF WORMS IN CHILDREN
AND ADULTS.
The general symptoms of worms are a
tendency to pick the nose and a sensation of
itching'in the anus. Other symptoms are
nervous affections, such as fretfulness, irri-
tation of temper, wakefulness or disposition
to excessive sleep, disturbed sleep, and grind-
ing the teeth, sudden starting out of sleep as
if from fright, dizziness, spasmodic move-
ments of the eyelids, dilated pupils, per-
verted vision, temporary blindness and deaf-
ness. General convulsions are not infre-
quent in children, and symptoms resembling
water in the head have been ascribed to
worms, and have ceased on their expulsion.
The long round or stomach worm is the
commonest kind of worm from which man-
kind suffers, and is of a light, transparent
color, resembling the ordinary ground worm,
except that the extremities are more sharply
pointed than the ground worm.
Young people are more subject to these
worms than middle-aged or elderly people.
They live in the bowels, and sometimes make
their way into the stomach and throat, caus-
ing sickness, vomiting and choking. Gener-
ally one or two would be the most a person
would be afflicted with, but sometimes a
much larger number is met with. The length
varies from four to eight, inches, but young
ones an inch to two inches long are also found.
The first thing to do is to rid the system ot
the worms, and Comstock's “Dead bhot
Worm Pellets, taken according to directions,
are an effective remedy for the thorough
eradication of the long round or stomach
worm—the common enemy oi the child, the
youth, the middle-aged and aged. A light, nour-
ishing diet, out-of-door exercise, and, if pos-
sible, a change of air, will greatly improve
the general health.
Threadworms or pinworms arc ot very
common occurence in children. They are
very small, from one-sixth of an inch to halt-
an-inch in length, and resemble cotton
thread—they are sometimes called seat
worms, and'reside in the lower bowels, llic
chief symptom of this worm is severe local
itching, restlessness and irritability, and a
frequent desire to urinate, picking at the
nose; loss of appetite and anaemia are also
observed. To get rid of the worms and then

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Acting-Premier Fuller, of N. S. Wales
professed to be tremendously astonished the other
day on discovering that instead of a revenue of
£200,000 having been received from the tax on
totalisator investments, only £llOO had been wheeled
in. The probability is very strong that even this
£llOO does not represent any actual income and has
been got rid of on red-tape and printing incidental
to giving the totalisator a false start. But anyhow,
there is nothing for Puller to be amazed about unless
it be Ins own gullibility. What is happening could
have been easily foreseen, and The Bulletin has no
doubt was actually foreseen by some of Fuller’s
friends. This paper pointed it out immediately the
alleged policy adopted with reference to the
machine was permitted to see daylight. The whole
business has been a fraud on Parliament and on the
people, and everything that has occurred more than
justifies the opinion expressed on this page that it
would have been far better for the State if introduc-
tion of the totalisator had been left to hands which
would give it a fair deal. There was no demand
to have the totalisator crucified. As the thing stands
to-day with a division of earnings which merely
appear advantageous to the State the interests of
every racing club are against doing more than for-
mally obey the law. The 7 and 3 per cent, basis
leaves nothing of any value to the racing clubs, and
with Ikey in the foreground the 7 per cent, yields
not a fraction of what 2J per cent, would under
rational, decent conditions of running the machine.
Either Fuller will have to make up his mind to
give the totalisator legs to stand on or continue
to be astonished. The present method of running
the contraption is sheer farce from every point of
view.. At Menanglc Park, near Sydney, .last week the
machine earned 16s. 9d. to devote to the encourage-
ment of racing, pay employees, settle the bill for
printing and help maintain the course and the appur-
tenances thereof. At Sydney pony meetings the
clubs are deriving a revenue of £3O to £4O from
14 and 15 races. This is a travesty on every argu-
ment put forward for introduction of the machine.
At Menanglc Park, one of Sydney’s “outer
circles” courses, last week, the totalisator staff were
called upon to handle fully £2B while six races
were being run. There was £2O on two races, £4
on two others and nothing on the other two. The
State revenue was enriched by £1 19s. 2d. The
coffers of the club were enriched by what was left
after paying the staff and printers and buying
stationery out of 16s. 9d.
If the books bet straight out or “1, 2” on
the lines they followed in Maoriland a few years
back punters would have less cause for complaint
than they now have under the deadly 1,2, 3 system.
There, if your fancy won, you got 75 p.c. of the
gross amount on the face of the ticket, or 25 p.o, if
it filled second berth. If you stipulated “to win”
you got the full amount as usual, but it is a million
to a struck match that Ike will never give this a
go in Sydney as long as he is allowed to place-bet
on the atrocious plan now sanctioned by the clubs.
* * * *
Of course, paying out totalisator divs. with
the deliberation of a deacon handing out change of
a dollar from the collection-plate merely makes
people angry. On Sydney courses where events are
run off quick and lively the result of this leisurelv
proceeding is to drive business to the bawl-and-yell
department. Also, the habit of getting tangled up
in the arithmetic and making bloomers is not calcu-
lated to induce the crowd to rush to the lightning
calculators, who can do a sum in simple division
wrong in at least 15 minutes. If a dinkum attempt
were being made to use the machine in N. S. Wales
the frequency with which something goes awry
among its innards would be a subject of inquiry.
From what the writer observes the calaboose is row
only a subject for suppressed laughter. Meanwhile
the question of whether the dividends are being cal-
culated by multiplying the investments on the last
horse by y+x, and dividing the weight of a pound
of sausages into the apprentice allowance remains a
subject for hilarious speculation.
* # ■» *
Being called upon at Sydney Quarter Ses-
sions the other day to sentence a woman who bv a
crude tale-spin had induced several trusting indivi-
duals to hand her their cash, the everlasting Judge,
Docker, proceeded to argue strangely from the front
of his wig. It had been alleged that the woman
played up her plunder at race meetings. 'Thus his
Honor: “I am afraid that there will be an increase
in this class of crime now that the State has legal-
ised a form of gambling, and proposes to take a
percentage of the earnings.” The reference is, of
course, to the tote, and wins the Stupidity Stakes in
a walk. This woman was not a tote fiend, any-
how. She did business witli Moses, and as Moses
is a legal institution Docker’s remarks were as point-
less as a pumpkin. There is absolutely no evidence
that people who refrain from highway robbery, arson,
barratry, embezzlement, bag-snatching, conversion of
trust funds, and using faked scales in order to have a
• go at the books will at once take to safebreaking,
larceny and sly booze-peddling, and rush the pro-
ceeds up to the machine. The idea is preposterous.
What there is evidence of —and Svdney has heard a
good deal of it lately—-is that the individual with
an illicit wad can .get rid of his assets in fairlv
quick time somewhere else. Tf Judge Docker could
be induced to disguise himself in clerical attire a"d
spend a .happy day or two at Randwick, he might?,
begin to get next to' the subject he thought he was
making profound observations upon.
*■ 9 * *
The story of the most amazing assault on
Israel this paper ever heard of was unfolded last
week by an individual charged with embezzlement
at a. Sydney Court. Somewhere about Christ-
mas he Was made a teller in the Savings Bank
and commenced operations by annexing £4OO and
rushing out: to Randwick. Then he annexed some
more and went to Mooreficld and appropriated a
hundred or two a week for a flutter at the ponies.
IBy the time Easter arrived he was short £1600.' and
seems to have judged that the time was ripe to get
busy. Wherefore-he pushed £4OOO out of the till
into his pockets and invaded Randwick again.
Detaching himself from wads varying from £lOO to
£6OO he left Ikey in possession of £3400 at the
end of two days. On the last day he dropped into
the office for £7OOO on the way to the course and
went in pursuit of the missing eurrenev. There was
£BOO on Eastcourt, £1250 on Cetigne, £lOOO
Green Cap, £l5OO on Brozetti, and a last despairing
dollop of £2500 on Spurn. After paying his tram
fare- home the enterprising punter had £5OO in his
clothes, and next day gave this to the boss and ex-
plained that about £14,000 had vanished. Whereupon
the staggered boss weakly yelled police and the mag-
nificent jag was over. It all seems wildly incredible
this yarn about a man going to Randwick with
£4OOO and £7OOO in his hands, but it is what the
speculator says happened. What Ikey thought when
he saw a customer approaching- with lumps as big
as the grandstand has not been explained.
* * * *
.Mo enjoyed the run home through the
countryside from Warwick Farm (N.S.W.) on
Saturday last, particularly because his pockets
bulged with cash and the etceteras were of the best.
At the course during the afternoon two unbacked
winners and several others which were onlv nibbled
at arrived safely. The tote worked at the Farm for
the first time, but in such a manner that adherents
were shy of betting with it. The Hurdles attracted
an investment of over £5OO to the machine, and as
the favorite landed the goods the rush to collect was
too great for the staff to grapple with, and the
pay-out was not complete until 10 minutes before
the second race. To a Government that evidently
wants to give the tote a black eye this would
naturally be “highly satisfactory.” The proceedings
tickled the club.
* * * *
The Rftjah of Pudukota, who races in
Sydney, has added to his collection of live stock bv
acquiring the Maoriland 2-year-old Kilfinn, which
recently snuffed out Desert Gold, and the ’chaser
Old Mungindi. The visitor has so far not seen his
colors near the front with excessive frequency, but
he lives in hope. Anyhow, whatever the filly may
do the jumper ought to get there, unless the "handi-
capper or the exalted ownership overwhelms him. He
is a good horse, and the Rajah is a good sport.
A couple of years ago Dick Wootten sent
from England the chestnut horse Omaha to be raced
in Australia. The animal has never done anything
remarkable, but has had a share of success in'minor
events. Held on lease by Dr. G. M. Slate, who. has
been racing gallopers and trotters for a quarter of
a century, Omaha has earned his oats on Sydney
suburban courses and tracks outside the metropoli-
tan area. On Saturday he was first home in Flying
Handicap at Warwick Farm. He was third fancy in
Israel, and, beat a rank outsider in Esteban. Dr.
Slate trains Omaha himself.
PRIVATE FRANK BROWN, ex-amateur
champion hurdler, and well Mown in
Vic. athletic circles.
A moke likely to be heard of again gal-
loped into notice at the right end of Warwick E arm
Pace Welter, six furlongs, on Saturday. This was
Martian Loch, the property of J. McMaster and
Rand wick-trainer T. F. Scully. Jockey McLaehlan
was on board. The nag was quoted at 33 to 1 in
the ring, and the tote paid £l9 10s. for half a £.
There Were only 15 tickets out. Martian Loch, a
three-year-old, was unknown to the “heads,” and
apparently those behind the gelding were not aware
of his ability. Martian Loch is by the Maoriland
sire Martian out of Lady Lochiel, and was bred in
the Shaky Isles. The reason for the name of the
beast _is obvious. . McMaster and Scully had all to
do with Marvel Loch when that great mare was at
the top of her form, which incidentally was good
•: enough to land a Caulfield Cup. Martian Loch shot
away at the right end on Saturday, and won with
plenty in hand by two lengths.
* * * *
• The Bulletin put in a good word for
Eurobin last week, and the kindly gee promptly
acknowledged the compliment by winning a First
Division Handicap at Mentone (Melb.). It was only
a six-furlongs flutter, worth less than £lOO to the
winner, but there were some decent ones in the
fairly-numerous field, and Eurobin was giving weight
to the best of them. Moreover the gelding’s price,
as first favorite, was seven to two, so he was well
worth hacking.
* * * #
Talking about names for nags. Anyone
having a moke with a stain in its pedigree might do
worse than call it Phewsion. It ought to be home
and dried first go. Then if it were sold to the high-
est bidder and fell down dead on test it would be
the specidator’s funeral.
» • * *
If the two-year-old Lady Lucre had been
as badly treated- by the handicapper as by the
individual who found, a name for her something else
would have won. Being by Murilld-Lucre the method
followed was simple and foolish. Lucre is not an
English word. There may be something to be said
for such names as Lady Comely, though if the words
were reversed the result would he better. It.
has all grown mighty wearisome. Lady Lucre- is
about as sensible as Lady Spaghetti or Fraulein
Mcßeth or Madamoiselle Murphy. Surely there are
. enough words in all the vocabularies on earth to
enable a better choice to be made. It happens that
Lady Lucre is owned by the former proprietor of
Cetigne, and in the case of that good horse they
had to give him a name spelled the wrong way.
Yet quite a good dictionary can be acquired for Is.
* * * *
Last week Cetigne was sold in Sydney
for £l5OO and a contingency of £5OO more:
Minster Bill, a well-known plater, was sold at New-
market (England) for two guineas 1 The only thing
wrong with this nag was the shortage in the feed-
box. The time seems to have arrived for Australia
to help win the war by buying up a few hundred
yearlings at a dollar apiece and bringing them out as
regimental pets on the hospital ships. This would
reduce the “stupendous difficulties’’ associated with
repatriation to the ridiculous simplicity of present-
ing every Billjim with the winner of the next Caul-
field Cup or the Flying Handicap at Rosehill and
telling him to get busy.
* * * *
“Musketoon”’ Have read at times of
jockeys who, having had a leg broken during a race,
have hung on to their mounts till the finish. But
till a few days ago I never heard of a horse finishing
with a broken leg. This actually happened at
Orange (N.S.W.) quite recently. Barbaric, ridden
by Rosen, was noticed to be in a bad way in
the Trial Stakes, and on being pulled up was
found to have broken the leg just above
the hock. It is believed the accident occurred
two furlongs from home, but the horse was
up with the rest as they passed the post. The
favorite, Mooltana, when running like a winner,
broke a bloodvessel in his head. This broke several
punters’ hearts.
* * * *
“Playbird”: There are compensations in
the trenches. This from my cobber’s letter: “It’s
a bit of all right here. We have a two-up school
every day, and there are no blushing Johns about to
disturb us. I’m over seven quid ahead of it up till
last Wensdy.”
* * * *
The only question in doubt at Sydney
Stadium on Saturday was whether Sid Godfrey would
pass Frank Thorn out sooner or later than he did in
Brisbane. _lt transpired that Sid was a supporter of
early closing. Finding he could do nothing with
Thorn as a boxer he tried punching the enemy hard.
Thorn took the change of programme more fre-
quently than was good for him, though for a long
time the wallop missed the assigned mark. On
points the aggressive party hardly won a round, but
the leakage of Thorn's strength was continuous, for
when a pug has reached his years there is little
in the way of reserve left to be drawn on. Nature
had him beaten in the eleventh, for though he had
given the youth in front of him a lesson in box-
ing up till then, he was almost too exhausted to
stand. “Have you had enough, Frank?’’ asked
Arthur Scott. He nodded his head and reeled into
the corner. Godfrey on receiving the verdict was
unmarked and fresh. But the old man got the
cheers. He was as quick and adroit as ever except
in keeping steam up.
* # * *
Sydney Rugby League hoofballers go
along merrily, despite the biT forward game in the
mud over in France. Competitions are announced to
start next Saturday, and in order to assist recruiting
the tramway authorities are going to carry the
crowds out to the grounds on special cars, where
lusty youth will whoop after a ball instead of whoop-
ing after the man-eater. An erstwhile crack League
footballer wrote recently: “I would rather a million
times be here in Flanders, up to my neck in slush,
waiting to hop over at Fritz than be among those
unblushing loafers in Sydney.”
* * * *
It looks as if John Madden was not far
wrong when he described lacrosse as a game for
thoroughbreds and gentlemen. Anyhow, some well
bred ’uns played the game in Victoria, for of the
Association’s roll of over 400 members 85 p.c. are in
khaki. Good bovs! The 120 players in the nine
teams entered for this season’s competition are
ineligible for military service, being nearly all
under age. Two seasons ago 42 teams were in the
fray.
* * # *
The opening run of Victorian cross-country
clubs was a significant contrast to what happened in
past years. A field of over 400 was a decent average
then; at last week’s burst only 48 mustered. The
shortage is commendable. Nearly 70 per cent, of
A.A.A. athletes have pulled on khaki.
* * * #
The late Methodistical Felstead, of Mel-
bourne, was almost a sport where swimming was con-
cerned. Some 12 years ago he succeeded George
Turner as president of Melb. Swimming Club. He
used to donate a handsome trophy to the competitions
every year, and when the club sent a team to Syd-
ney it was quite customary for Felstead to defray
the expenses. So the club reckoned him a
proper kind of president. Probably it was the
only club that spoke very kindly of this painfully
respectable citizen. In a general way he was not
popular among the godless, for popularity smacks
of sinfulness.
* * * #
The championship of the Deyonnort (Tas.)
Bowling Club was won by H. Wilkinson, who
defeated H. J. Richmond by 31 to 28. Wilkinson
has now won the championship of six clubs in Vie.,
S.A., W.A. and the Speck. He is a Methodist par-
son in his off time, but there isn’t too much off.
* * # *
One-time sculler, pigeon-shot and cyclist,
Dick Arnst, has lately dropped 3000 quail in six
weeks near Coolamon. Easter Monday’s bag num-
bered 160. The Fortune Hunter’s,., Day! Shooters
hereabouts are netting from £l2 To £2O a week.
Good shooting profit that, and a holiday trip thrown
in.
* » * *
A many-sided sportsman dropped off the
track when Bill Ferris crossed out at Parramatta
recently. In that district he was a front-ranker
at rifle-shooting, bowls and cricket; a pioneer of
swimming, rowing and cycling. Ferris was one ; of
the three judges in that Svdney Thousand bike-race,
over which a batch of pedallers (including Yankees?
Lawson, Downing and MacFarland) were disqualified:
for collusion. The prize was awarded to “Widely”,
O’Brien (now the ponderous bandmaster of Sydney
Professional Musicians Union).
* * * »
There will be the usual hustle im Sydney
baseball this season, between the rival League and
Association factions. The struggle has been going
on for three years, and hope of a patched-up peace
is as far off as ever. Originally-the Association con-
trolled winter ’ball, while the League attended to
the summer aspect of the game; but the rivals quar-
relled, and the League, affiliating with the National
League of America, ran an all-the-year-round series.
Premierships are being handed out by both sides
and there is more argument about it all than over
the fate of civilisation.
* * * *
Gerald Taylor’s attempt on the Victorian
and Australian 100yds. professional records at Mel-
bourne didn’t disturb anything. He covered the
stretch in 9|sec., well outside Jack Donaldson's
9fsee. at Melbourne in 1911, and 9|sec. in S’Africa
in 1910. But as the track was not in record-break-
ing condition, Taylor hopes to rattle the pace up
next time.
* * *
Officials of the South Gippsland Sports
Association and three timekeepers certified the
other day at Womerah (Vic.) sports that the
Australian wood-chopping record for two-feet stand-
ing blocks had been fractured bv the winner of
the competition. His name is Janies Bineen and he
got through his job (the wood, by the way, was
solid blackbutt) in 3 min. 58 l-ssec.
A cynical American sports-writer calcu-
lates that golf has added a total of 543,768 years to
the lives of those playing it in the U.S.A., but that
the exertion of getting away from the office early
on Saturdays has shortened them by 652,660, leaving
a net loss of 8892 years. Also that the game has
produced a crop of 217,000 “partial widows, who
might just as well have no husbands at all on
Saturdays and Sundays.” Further, that the ground
unintentionally dug up by the players’ clubs would
in four Saturday afternoons be enough to excavate a
canal 10 feet deep, 100 feet wide and two miles
long. There are a solid half-million of pros, and
amateurs at it, anyway.
X- * * *
“Cylinder”: Agree with “Eryander” (P».
29/3/’ 17) that shooting in front of hurrying target
is right in principle, but where does he get his
information that No. 6 shot propelled by 45 grains
smokeless powder travels 40yds. in 1.20 th of a
second, or at the rate of 2400 ft. per second? That
is faster than the service rifle sends its bullet. Have
never heard of the most enterprising cartridge manu-
facturer claiming up to 1200 ft. per second for his
product. The usual thing for standard shell (45
grains and l£oz. No. 6) is between 1050 and 1100,
over 20 yards, which is standard test distance. Over
a 40 yards course the average rate would drop to
870 ft. per second. Generally speaking, the gunner
will not have to face anything much faster than a
teal in full flight, or a clay pigeon sent at
full tilt, and they are reckoned to do only
40 miles per hour, while his blaek-cheek falcon
works out at over 80. Going at that it would have
moved 15ft., while the shot charge was en route.
It’s all in the instant judgment of a bird’s speed
and at what angle he is going. My figures are only
for true crossing birds; all others require a reduc-
tion. Have shot at what seemed absurd distances
ahead, and got ’em, and experimenting, once, got too
far out on a fast snipe. The charge appeared to pass
(according to where it struck the water) about 2ft.
in front, but the game turned round and went the
other way like a flash before crossing the line of fire.
There’s a lot in being quick on the trigger too, while
to shoot deliberately yards ahead requires practice, for
the tendency to hold on is almost irresistible. What
size of shot would shotsters consider as the best for
teal?
“Ponto”: Can any sport tell me why, if a
rabbit is shot in the nut from a head-on position,
one of his back legs is found to be broken? This
was made clear to ,me at Main Brothers’
Retreat station, near Cootamundra, and good guns
assure me that it is invariable.
* * * *
“Nick Bottom”: No bigger mistake was
ever made than that quickness is essential to success
in catching sand-worms for baijt. Also, only
the veriest amateurs at the game resort to pliers.
A piece of raw meat or pippv and a steady hand
are all that are required. After locating yoiir grub
all that you have to do is to coax him to crawl
across the butt-end of your forefinger (this is where
the raw meat comes in) arid quietly place your
thumb upon the back of his neck. Then pull up
steadily. Along the Queensland beaches worms five
feet long are common. No angler who knows any-
thing of. the game uses anything but worms as bait
for whiting; but for bream the best bait is fowl gut
or mullet gut; then comes pippy, with sand-worms
trailing behind. P.S.—From low water to half
flood is best time for worm-stalking.
“H.V.E.”: My biggest fiatbead was about
3ft. in length, but I think exceptionally large ones
will go to 4ft. or a little over. These big fellows are
best stuffed and baked, otherwise they are apt to eat
dry. As a rule, in tidal lakes, estuaries, etc., in
which flathead of ordinary size (say, 12in. to 16in.
long) are caught, there are usually some monsters,
but they rarely bite except to a very large bait, and
are generally taken in nets. Similarly, the rock-
fisher who uses a very large bait, hooks the
biggest snapper, but he is more bothered by sharks
than his mate who doesn’t. By the way, one Dam-
pier, when visiting Australia some little time ago
(16th century, I think), caught some fish which he
called “foxes,” and which from the description given
were flathead. His crew went nap. on them.

-had ; . already been digested), sundry lead-sinkers, and
rloscriDtions, human and other.

A Well-known Actress Tells How She
Darkened Her Grey Hair with a
Simple Home-made Mixture.
Miss Blanche Rose, a well-known actress,
who darkened her grey hair with a simple
preparation which she mixed at home, in a
recent interview made the following state-
ment : “Any lady or gentleman can darken
their grey hair and make it soft and glossy
with this simple recipe, which they can mix
at home. To a half-pint of water add loz.
of bay rum and a small box of Orlex Com-
pound. These Ingredients can be bought at
any chemist’s at very little cost. Apply to
the hair twice a week until it becomes the
required shade. This will make a grey-
haired person look 20 years younger. It is
also fine to promote the growth of hair, and
relieves itching and dandruff.” —Advt. [1

It is the proper thing to gibe at Geelong for
being slow; and no doubt when the direc-
tors of this concern made £2500 and divided
only £5OO the well-worn old brick was
thrown at them. But they can reply that
they have paid a rear onable 10 p.c. for
very many years and are in a far better
position to go on paying 10 p.c. than most
breweries. The bulk of the money which
they didn’t divide in past years they put
into pub properties, and to-day, though the
assets have been kept well written down,
this line’ stands in the books at £22,988.
which is £5OOO more than the whole paid
share capital of the co. This policy has also
secured for the co. a very fair trade con-
nection : wherefore, though brewery premises
and plant and rolling-stock have been gradu-
ally written down to under £12,000, profit
on manufacturing account was £2372 during
the past 12 months. The future of the whole
grog trade is uncertain but so long as there
is any profit at all in it this little co. seems
to have gone the ri_.ht way to secure it—so
far as any manufacturing and trading con-
cern can assure its future.
* * # •
In view of the fact that compounds of
asbestos and Portland cement are coming
into ever-increasing favor as building
material and are now beginning to be manu-
factured in Australia, more interest is being
shown in asbestos deposits. One Sydney co.
which has lately entered into the manufac-
turing business has put in a certain amount
of development work on a deposit at Iron-
bark Creek, Barraba. The fibre occurs, as
it generally does, in serpentine, and there is
a large quantity of rock carrying asbestos, but
the length of fibre is not too satisfactory.
Any amount of very short stuff, technically
known as “ribbon” asbestos, is available, but
seams of any width are not plentiful, and
as every seam has a break of fibre at or
near the middle, a feature common to all the
asbestos in the district, a 2-inch seam is
required to yield a one-inch fibre. Anyway,
after having done some little work on the
area in the shape of costeening in various
places, operations have been discontinued,
at any rate for the present. As a sidelight
upon the fact that the habit of importing
everything ready-made has an exceedingly
deleterious influence upon tire mining industry
of Australia —a country which abounds in
unutilised reserves of industrial minerals onlv
awaiting a decent local demand to keep
thousands of men in lucrative employment—
the matter has its instructive value. A
country that persistently neglects the develop-
ment of its own resources in favor of those
of every other country is hardly likely to get
much beyond the wild-rabbit stage.
* * * *
“M. 8.”: Near where Dick Bets f com-
monly and mistakenly called Bates) found
the Black Swan, between Laverton (W.A.)
and the Great Vic. desert, there was a party
working on a nice leader about as wide as
a brick, in soft sinking, and worth 14oz. to
the ton under the stamps. This was the
average; sometimes it went a little better,
sometimes not as much, .but this was what
it ran for months for 20-ton parcels sent to
the little 5-head 30 miles away. An expert
from Cripple Creek, or one or other of the
authorities on gold and what to do with it
when you have some, came along and
knocked a bit of the stuff on the dump to
pieces. Saw color in it. and asked the chaps
what they were doing. They suggested that
they were mining in a small way. “What
for?” lie inquired. “For gold,” they said.
“This stuff isn’t gold,” he told them.' “For
God’s sake, don’t tell ’em that at the bank.”
pleaded one of the lads. “The d—d fools
are givin’ us four quid an ounce for it.”
* * * •
“B.”: When Arthur Bayley decided to
discover Coolgardie in 1892 he was on the
Niehol, the first field in the world found by
a Chinaman. Ford was not with him there.
He was at the Cross awaiting instructions.
Bayley made up his mind finally and cut
the Niehol out of his calculations with the
suddenness which was one of his character-
istics. He and his mate there had been on
good gold, and when the time came to part
they had to divvy up in the metal. Spreading
it all out on a piece of paper on the box
which was the table in the humpy, each
took a pannikin full, levelling the top off
with the back of a knife, and tossed
for what was left over. There was still a
slug of 40oz. or 50oz. to divide and no scales.
Bayley laid the slug on the block outside, cut
it in two with the tomahawk, and invited
his mate to take his pick. That is the sort
of hairpin he was.
* * * *
The little tin shows on the Tas. East
Coast have been having a good time
lately with tin at a high price, and a plenti-
ful supply of water. Many shows regarded
as merely winter propositions have been
able to work continuously during the sum-
mer. The Clifton Creek Co. lately shifted
its pontoon to a site eight chains higher up
the creek. A dam was built behind the
pontoon, and water was pumped over until
sufficient height was obtained to float into
a position further uphill.
* * * *
“F.H.W.”: Gold coinage is still circulat-
ing in abundance in S’Africa. Recently I
tendered an Australian note at a Cape shop
and was surprised to receive a half-sovereign
amongst the change. Before the day passed
a party of us found no difficulty in obtaining
a yellow curio for every Australian note we
held.

Gradual Satisfactory

“Dingbat” (from the firing-line): In
December last The Bulletin told of the
return to Sydney of Corporal E. A. Roberts,
sti etcher-bearer of the 2nd, who helped Stan
Carpenter, of Newcastle (N.S.W.), bring
m five wounded from an exposed part of the
beach at Gallipoli, two days after the Land-
ing. The bearers were being sniped at all
the time, and “the Anzacs reckoned it was
worth a D.C.M. apiece; but the decorations
never arrived. Carpenter later won and
got his at Pozieres.” Here is a postcript to
the story. Roberts won a Military Medal
at lozieres, but it did' not come to light
till he had left for home. Perhaps he does
not know of it!
“The Oldest Inhabitant” sees a friend off:
Over the Divide recently went Phil Mylechrane,
86. He was son of a pioneer of the Hartley district
(N.S.W.), where he arrived in 1832. As a partner
of the late John Ford, later of Hereford, Bathurst,
he ran mail roaches from Penrith, west, in the old
bushranging days before Cobb and Co. (Jim Ruther-
ford, Walter Hall and Frank Whitney) took to the
business. Phil married a daughter of William Red-
fern Watt, of Bumbaldry (nephew of Dr. Redfern, of
Nore Mutiny fame), who represented Carcoar in the
third and fourth Parliaments, 1869-1862. The’late
J - B. Combes, brother of Ned, also married a Miss
Watt. After a turn at mining and pastoral work
Mylechrane formed Goldsbrough and Go.,- and was
for years engaged in “wool.” Likewise he had an
interest in the -turf and kept a string of handsome
thoroughbreds. Among his early jockeys were
“Brickey” Colley, Jim Ashworth and Joe Cavin. A
brother died a few weeks before Phil.


PROFIT BY THE EXPERIENCE OF OTHERS
BUY GOODYEARS NOW
There is an attraction and an
allurement to low price that only
experience can counteract -- we
realise that.
In motor cars the difference in
price is not vital—if we count them
solely as a means of transportation
from one place to another.
But in motor tyres there is no
middle ground. A tyre is either
good or bad.
If it is good, multiplied miles —
pleasant, untroubled and economical
—prove it beyond question.
If it is bad—disappointment,
delay, toil in the dust or mud, and
ultimate extravagance prove it just
as unmistakably.
The man who has not had an
experience with cheap tyres—who
has not suffered such disappoint-
ment and delay—cannot properly
know the folly of surrendering to
the lure of low first cost.
But the man who has - who has
tried it once and learned his lesson
—this man does know, and is
through with the cheap tyre forever.
The Goodyear clientele—the
largest single group of tyre=buyers
in the world—is composed largely
of men of this latter type.
They chanced it on the cheap
tyre, pocketed their loss and then
came to Goodyear.
We are satisfied with this
arrangement; we prefer a man to
choose our tyres out of the wisdom
of experience—such buyers are per-
manent buyers.
But the wise man profits by the
experience of others and saves his
pocket the cost of buying it.
Repeat business almost invari-
ably follows a first purchase of Good-
years—from whatever reason made.
Of course, we could compete with
the cheaper tyres if we chose to. We
have the factories, the facilities for
buying and production, and the
sales volume to meet any compete
tion. Simply by cutting the quality
we could meet them on more than
even terms.
But—we do not choose to cut the
quality. We do not believe it to be
good business. We do not believe a
prosperous future can be reared on
an insincere foundation.
The best proof that our content
tion is the right one is to be found in
the increasing preference for Good-
year Tyres. /Tore motorists buy
them now than ever before.
If you are considering the pur-
chase of a low=priced tyre, perhaps
it is best that you go through with
it now and get it over with. Ex-
perience is always valuable - though
often costly.
ATj/hen you are ready to demand
value given for value received -to
make your tyre purchase an invest-
ment in integrity—we recommend
that you visit the Goodyear Service
Station or Goodyear Dealer in your
neighborhood.
He is there on the ground to sell
you Goodyear Tyres, to show you
how to get the greatest possible
mileage out of them —to help you
in every way to realise the sure
economy such a purchase means.
He knows that your casings
won’t give up their full measure ol
wear and endurance unless good
tubes are inside them— and he knows
Goodyear Tubes enforce this service.
He knows also that the Good=
year Tyre=saver Kit is bound to add
life to your tyres, by correcting little
troubles before they grow big.
Both the Goodyear Tube and the
Goodyear T.S.K. are essential to
complete tyre satisfaction and
generous tyre service—upon which
depend the future of his business,
and of ours.

THE
CHANDLER
AIX
“I heard a really remarkable story recently about
a really remarkable performance of an equally remark-
able car,” writes an Atlanta (Georgia) man. “Dr.
J. W. Livingston, of New York City, told me the
story. I refer to Dr. Livingston as being from New
lork City, when, as a matter of fact, the doctor
simply has his headquarters in the Big Village and
trots from one corner of these old United States to
the other, and sometimes, if the notion strikes him,
he ambles up into Canada for a change.
“The last time Dr. Livingston passed through
Atlanta he purchased a brand-new Chandler Six.
Goes 6725 Miles Over Bad
Roads Without Costing
Penny for Repairs!
Then the travelling bee got into his bonnet and he
burned the trail from here to Canada. But I am
getting ahead of my story, and I’ll let Dr. Livingston
tell it. I quote him accurately, I think, but I can-
not make it so entertaining because I cannot draw
the vivid picture that he did in his pleasantly charac-
teristic manner. The doctor said :
“ ‘I left Atlanta in my new Chandler and travelled
through the Virginia mountains to Washington, D.C.
“ ‘From Washington I went to New York and up
through the New England States. Business called
me back to New York, and I spent a day there and
PRICE
£490.
left immediately for Buffalo. From there I went to
Hamilton, Ont. Then I went to Montreal, thence to
Quebec, to the Thousand Islands. Upon my arrival
there I returned by exactly the same route to Atlanta,
and when I rolled into the Southern Saxon garage my
speedometer registered 6725 miles, and I hadn’t spent
a penny on the car exceot for gasoline and oil.
“ ‘lf that isn’t a remarkable record I never heard
of one. I have owned more costly cars than the
Chandler, but I have never owned 'a better one. I
wouldn’t sell my Chandler for $lO,OOO if I thought I
couldn’t buy another one.’ ”
MARCUS CLARK CO. LTD.,
SOLE AGENTS.

IN BANANA LAND
Slumber Wins Again.
The enfranchised democracy of Bananaland
rose in its might on Saturday, and
ascending the ballot-box gave loud, frantic
cheers for Dodder. Also on that date
Premier Ryan discovered that he had fallen
heavily into the soup, and that the next best
thing for him to do was to knock off being
a smart Alick and slip a piece of ice under
his hat. The rejection of the proposal to
abolish the Legislative Council followed
naturally upon tactics that reached the very
limit of futility. Apart altogether from
whether the poll was legal—which in itself
is very doubtful —and whether it had any
constitutional force, the moment was about
as badly chosen as if the selection had
been made by the Council itself. Ryan's
immediate case against Dodder was lost in
the larger issues of the Federal elec-
tions. Instead of it being a wise
move to drag the subject forward on
Commonwealth election day the step
was ludicrous. Ryan calculated appar-
ently that the fact of the State electors being
called upon to vote on an issue appealing
directly to such radical opinion as the State
contains would harden up the Labor vote
for the House of Reps, and Senate. What
be did not realise was (1) that the hold of
Labor on State politics as revealed by the
votes cast at last election is mighty pre-
carious; and (2) that in the Federal arena
the tide had been running the wrong way.
That this plebiscite represents the measured
opinion of Bananaland about the House of
Dodder is almost incredible. But it reflects
fairly accurately the opinion of the electors
about Ryan and the tremendous cleavage
created by the earthquake originating at
Melbourne. This is not what the Govern-
ment wanted to find out. But it has spent
huge wads of the taxpayers’ cash to make
the discovery and as the thing stands now
Dodder looks as if it could take charge of
the situation and make ribald remarks about
the Ministry and everything associated there-
with. Meanwhile there is the cheerful cir-
cumstance that while Australia generally
has pushed a Phewsion majority into the
Senate, N. S. Wales has just added a wierd
assortment of individuals to the Leg. Council
of that State on life tenures, and the public
of Bananaland has shouted for all the world
to hear that the local Fossils must be left
to fossilise till the crack of doom. All of
which this paper sorrowfully admits is a
fairly substantial wallop for those well-
meaning citizens who have spent most of
their lives holding Fossildom up for public
inspection. The days of Fat are apparently
going to be long in the land.
[To express the views of The Bulletin, by S. H.
Prior, P. J. Kirby and D. M. Wright, 214' George
Street, Sydney.]
“Marloo”: The Bananaland tick is crawl-
ing southward all the time. The officers in
charge thought they had the pest cornered
in certain limits which were quarantined and
cleaned. Everybody in the hunt was smiling
when late April despatches reported the
appearance of the enemy in the South Wood-
burn district between the Richmond and
Clarence (N.S.W.). In less than a week
the inspectors located badly-infested herds
on five different holdings 20 miles south of
the pest’s last known outposts. In northern
N. S. Wales these outbreaks have been
mostly unaccompanied by redwater, but re-
cently two or three slight attacks of this
scourge have appeared. If someone doesn’t
deal brother tick a clean knock-out he will
presently boss the whole coastal area, where
the valuable dairy herds graze. Then you’ll
hear the stricken cow barons howling !
“Boro-Belle”: The absence of child labor
in the sugar country as compared with the
cow districts is due not to any kindness of
heart of the sugar cocky, but to the oil-
engine. It does almost everything on the
sugar-farm except cut the cane. It works
the chaffcutter, saws wood, ploughs, plants
cane, pumps water, hauls the cane-trucks
along the portable tramline to the nearest
permanent track leading to the squeezing
null. It even takes the boss’s daughters to
the dance and brings them home again. One
engine, which for years drove a tug on the
Pioneer River, later on took a turn ashore,
hauling some 20 tons of cane along a tem-
porary tram track where roads were too bad
for horse haulage. This year it is to be tried
at cane-cutting.
Another coincidence. While a recruiting
meeting was in progress at the Brisbane
G.P.O. the other day a bushman pushed his
whiskers through the crowd and mounted the
platform in response to the appeal for Bill-
jims. After the applause someone was heard
saying: “That’s my brother —I haven’t seen
him for nine years.” The recruiting-sergeant
caught the words and threw out a line. “Too
late,” said the interjector. “I’m in camp
already.”
Warwick is mourning two of its leading
representatives on the Big Job. Major
Arthur Chambers, light horseman, enlisted
in the first month of the war and saw a fair
share of the Gallipoli argument. Thence to
Sinai, and fatal!v hit in the battle of
Gaza. The other is Lieut. V. Watkinson,
a volunteer who went to the Peninsula in
1914. Afterwards to France, and has been
missing since early April.
BEFORE IT WAS HERS.
HE: “What beautiful hair she has!"
SHE ( sweetly , in the background): “Yes-but it looked even more beautiful in the
window."
After residing in North Queensland for 49
years, William Graham has succumbed to
the awful climate. Age, only 82. He was
at the Palmer in its palmy days, and was the
first man to take a team from Bowen to
Cloncurry.
Exit at 56, William Maguire, police sub-
inspector. He started business in the
Leinster Infantry Regiment, and spent a
few sweltering years in India. Pie
joined the Queensland John Hop service in
’SB, and, as drill-instructor at the Petrie
Terrace depot, spent 15 years cursing large-
footed recruits. Later, he did duty at Allora,
Gympie, Brisbane and Charleville.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, May 5, 1917.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
The air about me resounds with cries of
“liar” and “traitor” ; and everywhere is the
uncomfortable general insinuation that the
whole place is sunk in the depths. It is
election day and both political parties are
using the same slogans of vituperation. Vot-
ing is a complex business; and all day
females with a fluttered sense of having done
their civic duty have been straying out of
the booths only to discover that they had
forgotten the Local Option or Legislative
Council polls. Feverishly anxious to tear out
the useless portion of the constitutional
machine or smite Bung under the fifth rib,
they have charged back to demand papers
from the wrong official and to keep up the
pleasant sense of confusion which women’s
franchise ought to supply.
Mrs. Jim Irving gave a party at the Coo-ee
cafd on Wednesday for Gladys Moncrieff,
Minnie Love, Nellie Payne and some of the
other J.C.W. girls; and the guests dragged
away scantily-clad kewpies as souvenirs of
the event. Beatrice Yaldwyn, who belongs
to -these parts and counts her relatives in
scores and her friends in battalions, has also
been heavily tea-ed all the week.
The foundation stone of the Residential
Club for Returned Soldiers and Sailors is
to be well and truly flanked in mortar on
May 24; and cash is to be raked in for
the club funds. This show has been a long
time getting started; but now, with a real
memorial stone under it and plenty of
enthusiasm surrounding it, the job should
not hang fire any longer.
Has anybody seen a Billjim who was “kind
friend” to Nicholas Flett, killed in action
November 15, 1916? Mrs. J. Flett, Wood-
vale, via Bendigo. Vic., is advertising for
him in Bananaland papers.
Presbyterian missionary Noble McKenzie,
who has been converting the heathen in
Korea for the last seven years and who for
15 years before that spread the Word to
brown brother in the New Hebrides, is with
his wife in Brisbane. It seems a fair thing
that he should have an occasional look at a
white man’s country.
Died on Friday, James Chapman, head of
Chapmans, Ltd., aged 79. He was a popu-
lar man with a repute for straight going,
and had been a member of the Fire Brigade
Board, vice-president of the Chamber of
Commerce and president for many years of
the Queensland Flying Squadron. He first
smelt the heather at Kintillo, Bridge of
Earn, Scotland, but got a whiff of eucalyp-
tus as far back as 1864.
Still more crepe. Mrs. H. C. Cleeve, who
has a son on the Courier literary staff, died
this week. The old lady was a fine conver-
sationalist, and, as she came to Australia in
1852 and spent her girlhood in the Marooeli
district at a time when binghi was very
wild, she had much to talk about. In her
day Mrs. Cleeve was a fine horsewoman.
Mrs. Alex. McPhie, whom Bananalanders
once knew as Miss Carpendale, of Grantham
station, also died last week. Her eldest
son is with the A.1.F., and a brother is with
the forces that are hounding the last rem-
nants of Teuton-armed nigger t« a finish in
German East Africa.
The committee of the 26th Battalion Com-
forts Fund tea-ed Lt.-Col. and Mrs. Fer-
guson at the Botanical Gardens kiosk on
Wednesday. The Colonel maintained a
glowing silence about himself, but talked
with a lot of enthusiasm of the things his
'‘boys” did at the Somme. As a result of
their display of pluck and muscle they had
gathered in two awards of the D. 5.0., two
Military Crosses, five Distinguished Con-
duct medals, 11 Military Medals and IS
mentions in despatches. The Lt.-Col. was
severely wounded last August and has a
D.S.O. of his own; but he would rather
walk into a mac|iine-gun ambush than men-
tion how he got 'the ornament.
The Mayor gave a reception at the Coun-
cil Chainbers on Wednesday in honor of the
recent successes of the Brisbane Austral
Choir which is the champion choral con-
spiracy of the State. At the Warwick
Eisteddfod it chortled everything else to a
finish, and gathered in nearly every prize
that could be won.
The Governor unveiled an honor board at
the Lands Dept, the other day. The decora-
tion is framed in Queensland silky oak with
a panel of red bean, and displays 83 names.
It was explained that 67 per cent, of the
eligible men in the Department had gone to
the Front. How’s that for a record?
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Another well-known Brisbane man who
passed over this week was A. H. Frew.
Although over 60 at the time the present
war started, Frew felt keenly enough about
things to try to enlist and when the Aus-
tralian military people turned} him down he
removed his whiskers, dyed hismair and tried
again in England and France. He came
back to Queensland, via the United States,
bitterly disappointed at his rejection. A
lot of railway construction works in the
North and elsewhere took shape under
his theodolite. The Chillagoe, Etheridge,
Atherton and Normanton to Croydon lines
were his, as were also those at Mount Mol-
loy and Mount Garnet; and he has it to
his credit that he was the first engineer to
tackle railway work for the Government by
the day-labor system. After the S’African
racket he did survey work in East Africa.
R. D. Frew, another Bananaland engineer
of repute, is his brother.

Hupmobile Conforms to Best Standards of Two Hemispheres
WHEN you purchase a Hupmobile you have the satis-
faction of knowing that you have purchased a car
which conforms to standards approved by the whole world.
The best European practice in motor car construction has
entered just as much into the building of the Hupmobile
as has the highest American standards. The best is
adopted wherever it is found.
In proof of our statements, look at the Hupmobile. Its
lines are European lines. Consider the Hupmobile motor.
It is four-cylinder and has always been four-cylinder.
And tile Hupmobile manufacturers believe, as most Euro-
pean manufacturers and owners believe, in four-cylinder
power, simplicity and economy of operation. In many
lands the Hupmobile is known as the “ European car at
an American price.” This is an apt phrase which describes
that added carefulness which enters into Hupmobile con-
struction that surer appreciation of beauty in design
that recognition of the value of comfortable riding qualities
and luxurious appointments in combination with a price
that unusual production facilities has made lower than the
average price of cars in the Hupmobile class.

Grey Hair —Home
Remedy.
An-old-fashioned, home-made recipe restores
youthful appearance.
There are plenty of reasons why grey hair
is not desirable and plenty of reasons why
hair dyes should not be used. But, on the
other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want it.
To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has only
to get from the chemist two ounces of con-
centrate of tammalite and mix it with three
ounces of bay rum. Apply to the hair with
a small sponge for a few nights and the
greyness will gradually disappear. This
liquid is not sticky or greasy and does not
injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations w T ith most satisfactory
results by those who have known the for-
mula.— Advt. [2
