SPIDERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The white-haired, mild professor said
Of this peculiar spider,
When her first warm affection sped
She seized her husband by the head
And he was soon inside her.
The male then served as nourishment
To fortify the other.
A grave-eyed student, sitting bent,
Said, with a sigh most eloquent:
“How like a woman, brother! ”
VICTcRU.
SILAS.
“Lip” tells how Australia led the way:—
Towards the end of 1915 a clearing station (the
second of its kind) left Australia for foreign parts
with one or two extras amongst its equipment. The
first was a dynamo for electrically lighting- its
wards and the second an X-ray apparatus. Arriving
in Egypt, it was dumped in the Desert on the Asian
side of the Canal ready for the threatened Turkish
attack. When the pill-Colonels and Generals realised
that the d —d Australians intended to do jobs in the
field that were usually saved up for base hospitals,
they galloped about demanding explanations. Confis-
cation of the things and their transfer to a base
hospital was threatened. But the station hung on to
its goods, and eventually got with them to France,
finally finding a resting-place in a cow-paddock in
Flanders, a few miles behind the trenches. In a
month the cow-manune was cleared away, a swamp
filled in, duck-board paths laid, and the station ready
for business. On the opening night close on 900
casualties were treated and passed on in under 12
hours. Now the wounded man who is suspected of
concealing “foreign bodies” about his person is
whipped under the crackling tubes, photographed, and
presently goes on to the operating-theatre. When
he wakes he finds himself between snowy sheets with
any souvenirs he had in the way of bullets, shrapnel
or shell fragments in a little gauze bag tied round-
his neck. I’ve seen more than one life-or-death opera-
tion performed with the surgeon guiding his knife
by means of the X-ray screen. Since the winter
started and frostbite and “trench feet” have begun
to make gaps in the ranks, the utility of the dynamo
has been extended to a. high-frequency jigger (made
on the premises) to restore the circulation to Bill-
jim’s numbed toes. That somebody had learnt the
lesson became evident a little while ago when the
station was asked if it had any spare electricians
who could be transferred to other clearing-stations to
instal electric-light plants. Advance Australia 1
The N. S. Wales Testator’s Family Main-
tenance Act, passed last year, has just shown
a tine disregard for the vile testamentary
wishes of a man who died leaving a widow.
86 and nearly blind, two elderly daughters,
and a thin little £444, out of which only £25
was to go to the widow' and £10 to one of
the daughters. The balance was to be cut
up amongst churches. The new law snort-
ingly upset the will, ordered a regular 35s.
a week to be paid to the widow, and what
remains of the estate when she dies will be
cut up between the daughters.
Arthur H. Adams gives away a signed
guarantee of its veracity with this: —
After all, Life is more wildly melodramatic than
“The Story of the Rosary” ; and the hairy paw of
coincidence intrudes more often than it does even
in “Turn to the Right.” I was lunching with a
Sydney man at a cafe. We selected a vacant table.
In the middle of our lunc’h another man entered and
took one of the empty seats. Neither of us knew
him. I happened to ask my friend—whose son, a
lieutenant in France, had been reported seriously
wounded —for particulars of the casualty. He told
me a graphic story of the advance by moonlight, of
his son being shot in the abdomen, of his having
been hastily given first-aid by a chum of his, who
managed to. stow him in a shell-hole before con-
tinuing in the charge. Later, this good Samaritan
heard that the stretcher-bearers had found and re-
trieved the officer from No Man’s Land, and that he
had been sent to an English hospital. A common-
enough yarn, perhaps; but I noticed the stranger
listening eagerly. Still, l would listen with the
same attention to any such yarn. At the end of the
story the stranger quietly introduced himself. “I’m
the father of the boy who bandaged up your son,”
he said. Just here is where the melodramatic play-
wrights, including myself, go wrong. Instead of a
moment of tense emotion, followed by a warm clasp
of the hands and a few broken words of thanks, the
two men merely stood up and shook hands in the
conventional way, muttered a “Glad to meet you!"
and returned to their cold roast duck. The rest of
the talk was desultory, and the scene ended with the
other man reaching for his hat and nodding us a
“Good-day.” No curtain, no climax, just cold roast
duck —and it was excellent cold roast duck. Evi-
dently the fathers of Billjim take these services to
each other as Billjim takes them at the Front. What
worries me, though, is that in a recent play I had
put in a similar situation; but now I’ll have to re-
write it and make it chiefly cold roast duck.
“Murkah”: Recently a returned soldier
who had lost a leg was, among others, an
applicant for the position of rate-collector to
the Benalla (.Vic.) Water Trust. In the cir-
cumstances the ballot could very well have
been done without. But it was proceeded
with, and resulted in the man who had done
his duty achieving one vote, though the work
was well within his capabilities. A shire
rate-collector receiving a salary of £270 got
the job, and the £75 per annum it carried.
It’s time we took some practical measures to
gibbet people who do things like this.
As the Toorak tram pulled up at Bourke-
street an elderly passenger thrust her knit-
ting and ball of wool into her muff and
started kerbwards. As she reached the path
the ball of wool was jolted through the end
of her muff find bounced off unnoticed. The
strand connecting it with the muff soon
became taut, and the wool started to pursue
its proprietor, tangling itself with all sorts
of hurrying legs. Two sundown beer-
drinkers emerged from an adjacent pub, and,
seeing the ball of wool approaching, lurched
forward to field it. The first missed it by a
short length and hiccupped on towards a
verandah-post; the second planted one boot
heavily down on the wool and instantly a
scarf ' attached to the other end of
the wool leaped out of the muff. By
this time a stray brown-and-white mon-
grel had managed to entangle himself
with the strand, and his excited revo-
lutions only tethered him the tighter. As
the scarf fell the point of a needle came down
on his rump, and he left for home at 40
miles an hour trailing the scarf and 100
yards of wool behind him. The elderly
Toorakese stopped, shrieked and told the
policeman on point-duty that she had been
robbed by the I.W.W.

THE SECRET OF A
GOOD COMPLEXION.
By MME. ST. CLAIRE.
Any woman nowadays may quickly reju-
venate her complexion at home by a safe and
painless process of gentle absorption. The
days of expensive and dangerous “operations”
are past, and each woman can be her own
beauty doctor. It has been discovered that
ordinary mercolised wax, applied like cold
cream, at night, will cause the thin veil of
lifeless, sallow or blotchy cuticle to gradually
and gently flake off in invisible, flour-like
particles, revealing the fresh, vigorous and
beautiful young skin underneath. Mercolised
wax absorbs only the unhealthy and un-
sightly outer fi’m, and does not affect the
healthy tissue at all.
The process defies detection, and unveils
a lasting and beautiful complexion, which is,
of course, a perfectly natural one. For this
purpose thousands of women are now using
mercolised wax, which may be obtained at
any chemist’s. Naturally the process also
removes all such surface blemishes as
freckles, blotches, pimples, tan, liver spots,
etc. It makes the face look years younger
and prettier, and renders the use of face
creams unnecessary.
Another valuable piece of information,
which' many women will appreciate, concerns
the success of the new treatment for super-
fluous hair. A remedy for permanently erad-
icating this ugly form of facial disfigurement
now seems to have been found. It is simpli-
city itself. About one ounce of ordinary pure
powdered pheminol, which can be obtained
at any chemist’s, is all that is needed to put
one in the way of completely ridding oneself
of superfluous hair. When applied to the
obaoxious growth this causes it to immedi-
ately disappear, and the continuance of the
recommended treatment is said to finally
destroy the hair roots, so that there will be
no renewal of the growth. 15

oratory would be insufficient'to proclaim
the full virtues of “Plume” Spirit to the
world. Motorists have learned to rely upon
“Plume,” they know its value.
Plume Motor Spirit
Eastj Starting—Sweet Running —More Miles
“Plume” is sold all over Australasia, and
wherever it is sold it is of the same high
quality. Gives more miles to the gallon,

The Inky Way.
“A.C.” : Jimmy Martin, just passed out in
Ballarat, was the plumpest working press-
man in Australia. It was sheer joy to see
him try to squeeze into an armchair at a hos-
pital-hoard , meeting and not quite succeed.
I recollect, too, with what envious amaze-
ment he used to gaze at the remarkable F. T.
Outtrim, secretary of the Tullaroop (Vic.)
Shire Council, who, at well past 70, could
streak up and down stairs faster than the
most joyous office-boy. At that time capable,
good-natured Jimmy was the whole crew of
the Maryborough STANDARD, his last job
before leaving active press work for a little
printing joint. Previously, he had been on
the Age, Ballarat Courier and half a dozen
country sheets.
“Flayer” : Harold Ashton, one of J.C.W.’s
staff, just dead, was 58, a widower since ’9O,
addicted to silk hats, cigars and catching
cold, and at the back of his mind harbored
a bitter contempt for the stage and the
boom, insincerity arid glorification of banality
associated therewith. He viewed his calling
as an advance guard with the cynical tolera-
tion of a man of the world, and wrote the
long paragraph loaded with adulatory slush
and waited to see what happened to it
much in the same spirit as a butcher might
prepare the pile of sausages and speculate
on the destiny of his handiwork. In Maori-
land he had known' every pressman from
Auckland to the Bluff for over 20 years—-
and had loaded them all with pars. He used
to turn up three or four times between New
Year and Christmas with details of the
pending attraction and a determination to
tell all about it in a column per day for
14 consecutive issues. One sub. startled him
clean out of his wits. He sent to do the
opening notice a chap who travelled after
politicians, as a rule, and had a cold eye.
Harold knew him, and seeing him enter the
theatre felt a chill running up and down his
spine, for it was a fairly dinky show apart
from the dresses and scenery. At the first
interval he invited the emissary of the
Daily Yell to have a drink, and casually
inquired what he thought of the production.
“Rotten!” said the unemotional critic.
Harold made even time for the sub “Look
here, old man, I don’t want to interfere, but
if that chap Flayer cuts loose on ‘The Girl
with a Squint’ I’m done absolutely, and the
season ruined. For Heaven’s sake satisfy
yourself that he is right in his judgment.
Ask somebody else.” The worried _ sub.
fobbed him off and turned to his nightly
slaughterings’. Harold never slept a wink
that night and was up at break o’ day to
get a Yell. The critic had anointed “The
Girl with a Squint” with the oil of gladness.
# # • *
“B. J.” : Lord Lucas, who was killed while
doing his bit as a member of the air service
in France, has set a precedent by leaving
£3060 to Hilaire Belloc and £lOOO to G. Iv.
Chesterton. Perhaps some of our wool or
quartz kings will follow this lead. Still, I
wouldn’t advise Bedford to wager his hat or
“Bellerive” his reputation on it.
* * * *
From an old Londoner in the same trade:
Frank Burnand ran on the same lines as \V. S.
Gilbert, but he didn’t run so gracefully or so far.
Both of them were called to the Bar as very young
men, and neither of them ever had any practice to
speak of. They both began their connection with
the press by writing for Fun when H. J. Byron
was editor; but whereas Gilbert’s first Bab Ballad,
“The Yarn of the Nancy Bell,” was refused by
Punch before it appeared in Fun, the other man
wrote for Fun before he went over to Punch.
Burnand was the earlier writer for the stage, but
only in one instance did he get in front of his
brilliant rival as a satirist. His comedy, “The
Colonel,” a satire on what was called “the aesthetic
craze” at that intensely stodgy period, preceded Gil-
bert and Sullivan’s “Patience.” Burnand never
struck a rich reef of humor as Gilbert did, but in
a lesser degree he was as versatile. He wrote 120
plays of one sort or other, and some of them
brought him a lot of money. Australia has seen
two or three of the best. “Betsy,” a farcical comedy
that Brough and Boucicault played, was one. When
Arthur Garner returned to Melbourne many years
ago, and tried his luck in management at the Bijou,
ho produced Burnand’s serious comedy, “The Turn
of the Tide,” under its sub-title of “The Morals
of Mayfair.” There was some good stuff in this
and in many other things that Burnand wrote, but
he never scored a great artistic success. He was
humorous rather than witty, and he hadn’t a keen
natural taste in dramatic effect. As for his editing
of poor old Punch, well, he had to take Punch as
he found it, a laboriously “comic” paper that had
been born at the dullest moment of the dull Vic-
torian age, and was expected to try to live up to
its past. Nevertheless Punch flourished exceedingly
in Burnand’s time, though many rival publications
of the same kind withered and died.
EQUIPPED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He found himself a cosy room,
A shaded lamp to banish gloom;
A splendid desk he bought, with nooks
For paper, pens and little books.
He used the best typewriter known,
A fountain pen set with a stone.
The blackest kind of ink he had,
A most expensive writing pad,
And copy paper by the ream
Of which a simple bard might dream.
Now being furnished for the strife
He faced the literary life.
And then for years he sat and wrote—
A poem or a little note,
A novel or an epic grim.
They all came drifting back to him.
He thought his writings very fine,
But. no one cared to print a line.
“What is’t,” he wondered, “that remains?”
He’d overlooked a pinch of brains.
One day he died, and in a den,
Upon a box, with borrowed pen
A scamp wrote out his finish fit,
And quickly got three quid for it.
victoria.

Revelation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PAN went by with his rout —
Rough hair and a woodland shout —
And a pink-white nymph between
The boles of the elm-trees seen.
Pan and his rout 'went by
And we did not care;
For Athene’s robe in the sky
And Athene’s voice in the air
Drew us with clearer eyes
To the thing that was fair and wise.
Bacchus went down the way.
We saw the thyrsus swing
And the Maenads madly sing
To the clash of the cymbal’s play.
Red wine spilt on the sod ;
And a twisted ivy crown
On the brow of a sotted clown
Lush riot hailed as god.
Bacchus went down the way
And we did not care;
For Delius stooped to play
And the music of his lyre
That flooded- sky and air
Set heart and soul afire.
Plato walked in the streets — *
Still look and a shoulder 5t00p....
Oh, the woodland was full of sweets;
And the winding river-loop
With the shade of a willow tree
Mocked his philosophy.
Christ and His cross went by,
World-passion in His eyes
And a look as meek and high
As the dew-star in the skies.
Christ and His cross went by ;
But we did not follow the cry
Of grief or of unbelief,
But sat in a quiet place
Reading the Book of Grace.
Ares blew on his horn !
Thunder of guns on the wind !
Death at the flush of morn,
Death Avhen the night was blind!
Rivers of ruin red !,. . . ,
Pan with the rout he led,
Bacchus in lust of wine,
Plato with thoughts’ fine line,
Christ daily crucified —
How the world ached and lied!
Ares blew on his horn !
And our heavy souls were born
From the shadows of yesterday
To the truth of the beasts that slay,
To the truth of the asp and toad.
To the truth of the ass and the goad,
To the folly in you and me
That lives from eternity.
For a pint of blood or so
Is all that we are or know.
If it be truth, slow won,
That a man is less than his gun.
N.S.AV.
DAVID MCKEE WRIGHT.
“Ore in Sight.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FATHER HANRAHAN was bound for
his new parish in the new green
township of Cowville after being sun-
dried for a dozen years as the priest of
many North Queensland mining-towns,
where everywhere he had made good among
the miners, being always a man, and a
priest in season. His face, weathered red
and made comfortable by a cheerful heart,
was his unfailing letter of introduction; the
blue eyes laughed, the mobile mouth
laughed; he laughed even with his spatulate
nose. On the Palmer and the Gilbert and
the Woolgar and the Coen he had perfected
the art of robbing the rich to give to the
poor. The lame dogs of the North gravi-
tated to Father Hanrahan as if gravitation
to him was their only trick ; in such cases
Father Hanrahan made his rounds and
separated the strong from money enough to
give the latest lame dog a splint. He hated
but one thing, and the less he had of it
he hated it the' more —that sectarianism
which belongs to cold countries where chil-
blains are common and the continuous uSe
of handkerchiefs imperative, and which dies
on the boundaries of new mining-camps and
great distances.
Fifteen years of mining-camps had left
him with a heavy arterial pressure. He
had friends in the South, as in the North,
East and West, and they insisted that the
sight of green grass and a parish that knew
a winter was necessary to him. So Father
Ilanrahan was sent south and west 2000
miles to Cowville. It is green even to many
of the people who are the only cruel things
in it. They give their children to the
slavery of the cowbail; in the township
itself their chief pleasures are skinning flints
and comforting themselves with the hope
that some of their fellow-townsmen have
permanent appointments after death with
the worm that dieth not and the flame that
is not quenched. Yet Cowville is very
beautiful, and on clear days in the keen
air of early Spring you can see the snow
on old Macedon. So here was Father
Hanrahan at Spencer-street, in the Ben-
digo train, his blood chilling to air colder
than that of the North; his fingers
mechanically playing with the medallion
hanging from the chain that was a presenta-
tion of the miners, w*ho had given also the
big gold watch that bulged his waistcoat
pocket. He stopped playing with the
watchchain and turned around and around
on his left little finger the big pearl-and-
gold ring—the pearl of Torres and the gold
of York Peninsula—given him by another
lot of men who had liked the priest because
they loved the man.
He had yet five minutes before the train
started; but methodical habit had brought
him to the station so early that he had read
the evening paper and rose to take from
his bag the best Irish novel ever written—
to be read by him now for the twentieth
time —Gerald Kickham’s “Knocknagow, or
the Graves of Tipperary.” He turned to
the chapter of “The Hunting of the Wren,”
and smiled at Burney’s boast of having
missed killing the wren though he had
"knocked a hatful of feathers out av him”
—smiled as at an old friend. And then he
raised his eyes as the clamor of departure
awoke the platform, and did indeed see an
old friend —an alert, sinewy little man with
a greying, short moustache* and very bright
■ and humorous eyes. He carried a rug and
magazines, and a porter with a suitcase
followed him.
“Hey!” cried Father Ilanrahan, and as
the traveller turned in pleased, surprise—
“Misther Godwin.”
“Father Hanrahan! It’s good to see you,
Father. Any room?”
“Boom- for five —come you in and be all
of them.”
Godwin boarded the train, entered the
compartment and shook hands with Father
Hanrahan. They were old friends of the
Northern goldfields, and they talked of all the
rushes until the train slowed into Sunbury.
They talked old friends fo Gisborne, and
beyond there Godwin told the Father of his
job near Cowville —the sampling of a gold-
dredging proposition on an old field aban-
doned ’way back in the middle ’sixties.
Father Hanrahan got his punch in al
that point. “Be more thorough, me dear
Godwin, than you’d be at the Angle Bend.
Y’know that time you sampled the Angle
Bend in Big Creek, an’ they salted the boxes
on you.”
Godwin flushed, but he laughed too. “It’s
a good thing a friend says it, Father. I
trusted that lop-eared gentleman who did
the salting, and I fell in.”
“An’ who would blame you for your con-
fidence in human nature, Misther Godwin?
On’y a bad, unthrustful man would. But
God sends bad men into the world to make
good men careful.”
“I know. But it was a good thing I
found it out before any harm was done.”
“So it was, so it was. But didn’t I tell
you at the time what you knew before, an’
that is any sampling is imperfect except
the kind you’re going to use in ackshual
practice an’ operation! What ye wanted
at the Angle Bend was” sharfs, an’ then put
all the dirt from the sharfs through a Long
Tom. That was your only thrue samplin’.”
“I know, Father, and I did it the second
time to check the results of the bores. And
I took the bores in good faith.”
“If there’s one thing mine samplin’ should
have no dealin’s with ’tis faith—good or bad.
Y’ can’t have the thrue religion without
faith, but there’s no religion in minin’,
though there’s a lot in miners.”
“Where you have religious miners I
believe you have to search them twice a
shift in specimen mines, Father,” said
Godwin slyly.
“Didn’t I say the thrue religion—answer
me that! Of course I did. But in min : n'
y’ must trust nothing but the tape an’ cnkl
blooded samplin’. Look at some of thim .
men talkin’ ore in sight an’ thryin’ to mak»
a sum out of one figure. Y’ can’t measure
ore in sight unless ye have four side,*
exposed. ’Tis flyin’ in the face of honesty
an’ Providence to estimate ore in sight if
one dimension is concealed —I would never
do it.”
“You don’t know, Father. Hope springs
eternal in the human breast, and a man is
more sanguine in a boom than a slump. Put
the right excitement around him on a new
field, and only a lizard will refuse to give
the new field a sporting chance —a warm-
blooded man couldn’t.”
“No, no! An’ here we are at Ivyneton
an’ I’ve talked like a boastful new-chum to
a man about his own job. Ah, me dear
Misther Godwin, if I could find a man to
teach me humility I’d guarantee to teach him
astronomy, though I don’t know Sirius from
Alderboran.”
* * * *
From their little wayside station they
drove a dozen miles through bird song, quiet
beauty and mid-spring to Father Hanrahan’s
new cure of Cowville. The road rose and
fell gently through green grass, stone pines
and brown furrows, with new wheat, pale-
pink hedges of the dog-rose, lambs butting
on lush hillocks, cows suckling calves knee-
deep in strawberry clover; yellow ribbons
of capeweed ; peach and apricot blossom fall-
ing to the breeze: a row of poplars ram-
parting a ditch and saying “Normandy,” find
a cypress in the little country cemetery
speaking of death and Italy.
To Father Hanrahan, burned with the
years of the Northern mines, it w r as a wide,
great, endless oasis. Godwin, prepared for
it by the continuous change of scenes in his
wanderings as mine examiner, the drive
touched little less nearly. Y r et Spring is
always new even to a pensioner; and a pen-
sioner finds change or death almost impos-
sible.
“ ’Tis Heaven,” said Father Ilanrahan.
“ ’Tis Eden come to earth again—green as
the raths of the old land, and tender like
a mother that feels every cry of the child.
They told me there was bitter black sec-
tarianism here, but ’tis wrong. It couldn’t
be thrue with all this bounty from the good
God to soften men. Look at the lamb there
fightin’ his brother like a divil. An’ a crow
there on a dead lamb’s eyes—the Divil’s
here, too. And why not when he got into
Paradise?”
“That capeweed is gorgeous,” said God-
win.
“ ’Tis the only time I saw yellow with
beauty in it, then. An’ see! the capeweed
an’ the grass don’t fight.”
“Yes, they do, Father; the capeweed can
kill even couch grass.”
“An’ couch grass is the devil. Well, well,
and the beauty of it all. They wrote me
there’s a deputation of the congregation to-
morry night at the presbytery. Come you
along. Godwin dear, an’ it will seem that I
brought with me to Victoria some of the
dear hot sunlight of the North.”
* * * *
Father Hanrahan received the deputation
of the congregation with his usual kindliness
but a new dignity, for he could not yet
warm to the temperature of his friendship
for the more natural men. the miners and
prospectors of those new fields where indi-
viduality is capital and men count for more
(Continued on next page.)

KIDNEY DISEASE
Why is it so Serious? Poisonous Waste is
Left in the Blood when the Kidneys are 111.
Every Picture
Te/Is A Story *
•W : »
\ '--S
V&X-Sk
V/
Kidney disease is serious because it is so little understood. Many men
and women do not know what the kidneys are, where they are, or what' they
do. Consequently, when any sign of kidney disease appears (such as
backache, urinary troubles, puffy swellings, irregular heart, etc.) it is often
mistaken for some other trouble, and the kidney complaint grows, unnoticed,

than money. - Also he had proved in one
day’s knowledge all the forewarnings of the
bitter sectarianism of his new parish as less
than the truth. Godwin, entering the pres-
bytery from the garden where one great
green shady walnut tree told all the softness
of the Spring, saw the seriousness in his
face replacing the chronic cheerfulness and
wondered; but not for long.
The congregation by its deputation wel-
comed the new priest, and Father Ilanrahan
rendered thanks and returned to seriousness
immediately.
“Me dear friends, - ’ said Father Ilanrahan,
’’l'm glad to be among ye, for here I see a
great work to be done if me eyes see thrue,
as they need to do. I come from the Far
North of the Commonwealth ; from the primi-
tive ends of the Australia we love so well,
the Australia that God kept secret and
alone except for the poor black men until
free men from all the world should be ready
to come to it. Up North I met men who
struggled with the rocks for gold and with
the sea for pearls; who fought hunfler and
thirst, somethin’ for their own winnin’; but
much that they didn’t know of for the good
of the dear mother Australia. There
was not much religion in them except the
best—the religion of love for the other man,
an’ especially when he was ill or in trouble,
hungry or thirsty, or poor—but their kind-
ness was a church that was never built with
hands. The country was cruel to them till
they tamed it; 50-mile, 80-mile stages with-
out water; brazen sky, incandescent, scrubby
grey sand to make a furnace of the sun’s
rays, trees without shade, the world crepi-
tatin’ in the heat. An’ they met it cheer-
fully, gold or no gold; an’ typhoid fever, or
breakbone fever, or dysenthery or the dog
disease gave you a mor’gage on the heart of
nearly all of them. Suppose one well man
had said to another of -a sick man, ‘lie’s a
Catholic! Don’t touch him’; or ‘He’s a Wes-
leyan—pass him by’—the first well man
would not have been a well man long. Ilm !
11m ! Out of all that hardness of inanimate
things and that kindliness of nearly all men
to their neighbors, I come here to all this
beauty and beneficence —the birds liltin’, the
lambs buttin’ other lambs, the cow and the
calf knee-deep in the clover and the grass;
every wildflower, every hedgerose singin’ ‘Te
Deum,’ the yellow of the capeweed and the
green of the corn growiu’ together in kindli-
ness and amity. An’ then I find in one day
that so lost is the lesson to ye of the charity
of Nature here and the gracious expression in
the meadows and the fields of the eternal
goodness and love of Almighty God that this
township is torn with sectarianism—Orange
and Green, and all the shades and the schisms
and the tinctures of both —the Wesleyan
fightin’ the Presbyterian, an’ the Baptist
fightin’ the Anglican, an’ all of them fightin’
the one thrue religion, universal and catholic,
the Church of Rome.
“When I left the old land I thought I had
left the black and bitter curse of sectarian-
ism, and I find it here—introduced to the
freest land on earth with the fox and the
rabbit and the gorse and the whin and the
old hates and class misunderstandings which
are a greater curse than any pest, animal,
vegetable or—or —mineral. Oh. me men, me
brethren, ’tis terrible.
“So. knowin’ you were cornin’ to-night, I
determined to speak to you of this matter:
to show you how the returning of God’s love
for you with hate of any of his creatures is
black ingratitude an’ mortal sin. I will say
this for the Catholic, that in this saturnalia
of sectarian hatred he is the last to blame.
But do ye think that is any credit to ye?
Ye know ye have the only thrue religion, an’
therefore ye should harbor no black thoughts
of people of other congregations, because it
should be enough for men of the thrue
religion to know that the others will burn in
hell fire! An’ isn’t that enough for ye, ye
wicked an’ perverse generation? Answer
me that!
“Now, this thing so exercised me mind
that I searched for a homely illustration to
bring the full horror of the curse of uncharit-
ableness home to your dear thick skulls; an’
passin’ under that great umbrageous walnut
tree in the presbytery garden here that typi-
fies the goodness and the beauty of the God
who made us all, I idly plucked this green
walnut that I show you after takin’ it out
of me pocket, an’ God put it into me mind to
see in the free sections of this walnut the
Free warrin’ sects of this beautiful township
of Cowville.
“Now, here we have the husk—the green,
bitter uncompromisin’ husk, useless for food
for man or beast, without any charity or
kindliness. It is Wesleyanism, an’ I tear it
off an’ throw it away. (Yet all Free sections
of the nut are necessary to the fruit). We
.come to the shell —the greater necessity to
the kernel itself, protected by the lesser—-
tlie husk. The only value of the shell is that
by diggin’ beneath it, by breakin’ it open
an’ discardin’ it, we progress from the lialf-
lie to the whole truth. The shell, except for
the protection of the kernel, is without
value. It has not even bitterness, it has
not even an essential oil; it is but the coffin
of the glorious body. In this shell there Is
no charity or usefulness or nutrition. The
shell is Presbyterianism. An’ I break it
open an’ show you”
The strong fingers with the northern
miners’ ring flashing on the little finger of
the left hand broke the shell open.
The nut was rotten!
* * * «
“ ’Twas a sad ending to the discourse,”
said Father Hanrah'an. when the deputation
had departed, “an’ thank ye for the decorum,
Misther Godwin, though I knew ye were
dyin’ to laugh.”
“Mining, ’ said Godwin, slyly, “should
only be approached after careful and cold-
blooded sampling. Faith is out of place in
demonstration.”
“Now, Godwin, dear.”
“Bores are no good—shafts should be sunk
and dll the dirt put through a Long Tom.”
“Well, you’re a hard-hearted”
“And yon mustn’t estimate ore in sight-
unless four sides are exposed.”
“Man, dear, let me down softly, an’ I’ll
make the presbytery produce the whisky,”
said Father Ilanrahan. setting out the
decanter and the seltzogene.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Over the Top.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“THROWN there?”
ij “Yes, sergeant! What’s up? Tea
ready?”
"Look lively and clean your rifle. You’re
going over the top with me to-night. Wiring
party.”
“Gor’struth! What, me again?”
Private Brown grabs the first rifle he sees
and curses under his breath.
After dark we creep over the parapet and
flounder until we reach the wire. Shells have
torn a gap through it; iron posts stand
gauntly, with a few twisted strands and
stumps of wire sticking to them.
“Strike me pink! Do a bloomin’ route
march through it, couldn’t yer?” «
"Sli! Stop that there !”
We unroll the coil with stifled curses. A
Very light goes up from Fritz, and we
crouch among the prickly thistles in the
mud. As the light dies down we carry on,
fixing posts, fastening wires, and filling in
holes with many turns and twists. A strafe
breaks out on the left and the lights go up
iu showers until the front looks like the
promenade at Manly. We crouch, our knees
soaked through, our hands and arms covered
with mud, scratched and bleeding. Brown
catches his trousers on a barb, tearing a
great hole.
“Dear mother,” he remarks gently, “this
is a terrible war. Your lovin’ son, Bill.”
A Very light curves close to the party. A
machine-gun spits, the shots, traversing
along the wire, striking sparks, with a nasty
ping. Just before the ijhots reach the party
the gun stops.
The party straightens up again and
remarks "Dud.” This happens two or three
times—hooray, the party is outside the arc
of the gun! This realised, we take no
more notice of it, and the wire begins to
look shipshape.
Then a Bosch sniper gives ns his atten-
tion. The covering party gives its attention
to the sniper. The Bosch gives his attention
to the covering party. One of our flank
machine-guns gives its attention to the
Bosch. So things grow lively; but risks
must be taken now and the wiring goes on
until the gap is closed.
“Pack up !”
One by one we crawl back and drop ovi r
the parapet.
“Had a nice evenin’. Brownie?”
“Oh yes, if it hadn’t been for the blasted
thistles!”
Private Brown always did have a fine
sense of proportion.
BILL BOWYANG.
The Dancing Heart.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SWUNG slioon and the bend of the ankle.
the lift of the curving hips,
■ Strong shoulder thrusting the wind-
waves, and the kiss of the air on
the lips,
Planets prone in the star-dust like glimmer-
ing globes of steel,
And the throbbing blood in the temples
which answered the plodding heel;
Bruised flowers and crushed green grasses
told well how the strider sped
Who quested the swerve of the old grey hill
when the blithe heart danced ahead.
The nibbling rabbit heard him, and crouched
in the dew-wet grass;
The ground-lark wakened upon her nest, and
quivered to hear him pass;
The drowsy kine in the hollow tvHce caught
at the rolling cud
When the strong, strong feet strode down-
ward at the beck of the singing-blood ;
And a gay song welled and gathered from the
curve of a singing throat
Chaste and cool as the star-white dew which
clung to the swinging coat.
Glances brighter than star-gleams upflashed
from the valley-place;
A moon-white song took moon-wliite wings
and sped from a moon-white face;
Up through the wind-swept rushes it sang on
the knee of the hill,
And the dancing heart danced downward and
the strong feet knew its will;
Flowers and grass and star-beams, the call
of the young night there
Told of a white brow flowing with foun-
tains of night-black hair.
Then Love took hold of the shoe-strings, and
stronger the strong feet paced,
Treading the curve of the hillside, soft as the
girlish waist;
Fragrant paths of the hill-world, ribbons of
ripest brown,
Twined round the feet of' the strider and
hurried him down and down;
Yet ever the - heart danced onward, fleeter
and lone and gay,
And ever the blithe song left his lips who
travelled the Lover’s way.
Five little hillside pathways met at the high
hill’s feet,
Whispered a while together and joined in an
ordered street:
Smothered their high-world laughter, yield-
ing the town its due,
And the strong feet followed after when the
dancing heart danced through ;
And a thousand town-moons glimmered
when the town was lit and late,
And the panting dancing-heart stood still
outside an ivied gate !
Steps on the steel-grey pavement echoing
near and near.
Beating time to a love-tune sweet to a moon-
white ear ,
Strong brown hands through the night-air,
stretched where the bright heart stayed,
Claimed it his princely dower, to give to a
queenly maid :
And the Love-path sang on the hillside, for
the path the blithe heart trod
Led to a place where four bright eyes gazed
straight in the eyes of God.
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
